The Five Universals
intermediate
8 min
Defending Non-Linguistic Natural Signification
The commentator defines and exemplifies the three types of signification, with critical defense of natural non-linguistic signification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the commentator's argument for non-linguistic natural signification and evaluate his response to the objection that such signs are merely intellectual.

Core Idea
Natural signification is not limited to language—instinctive reactions like blushing are signs by nature, not by convention or intellect.

In the previous lesson, we learned the three types of signification. Here, we focus on the commentator’s vigorous defense of non-linguistic natural signification against scholars who deny its existence. This passage is a mini-debate: the opposing view, the objection, and the commentator's counter-argument. Let us unpack the dialectic.

Guided Path
1
State the opposing view
The commentators of Sharh al-Matāli‘ and al-Mutawwal held that non-linguistic signification is only conventional or intellectual, making the divisions five. They deny a distinct non-linguistic natural signification.
Their position: al-dalāla ghayr al-lafẓiyya hiya wad‘iyya aw ‘aqliyya.
2
Extract the commentator's counterexamples
The commentator offers redness for shame, yellowness for fear, and the rapid pulse for a particular temperament as examples of non-linguistic natural signification.
These are bodily changes that naturally accompany emotional states.
3
Analyze the objection and response
The objection: these are really intellectual (effect-cause) or correlative significations. The commentator responds by noting that even if an intellectual relation exists, natural signification can coexist, as shown by those unaware of the causal link (e.g., animals or untrained people).
He denies that the opponent's distinction (nature compels here but not in 'akh akh') holds universally.
4
Understand the verification (taḥqīq)
The commentator's final position: if the connection is intellectually necessary, then intellectual signification exists, but that does not preclude natural signification. The natural signification is based on habitual practice, not on conscious inference.
He argues that natural signification remains even if the intellectual relation is absent (e.g., supposing no causal link, the habit would still cause the transition).
5
Identify additional examples
The commentator gives more examples: a horse pawing the ground upon seeing barley, a person dancing involuntarily to pleasant music, and facial expressions of pain. These all show natural compulsion without intellectual inference.
These examples reinforce his stance that non-linguistic natural signification is evident.

(۱۱) و هي الخطوط والعقود والاشارات و النصب، اما دلالة الخطوط فكدلالة حروف زيد المكتوبة على الذات المشخصة واما دلالة العقود كدلالة عقد الاصابع في علم الاصبع على مرتبة من مراتب الاعداد و اما الاشارات فكدلالة اشارة الحاجب على الدخول والخروج مثلاً و اما النصب فكدلالة العلامة المنصوبة لمعرفة الطريق مثلاً على الطريق ولا شك ان جميع ذلك انما هو بسبب الوضع (محمد علی)
(۱۲) قوله وان كان بسبب اقتضاء الطبع: اراد به طبع اللافظ فانه يقتضى تلفظه بذلك اللفظ عند عروض المعنى كما قال المصنف بعد التمثيل للدلالة الطبعية بقوله كدلالة اح اح على وجع الصدر فان طبع اللافظ يقتضى التلفظ به عند عروض الوجع له او طبع معنى اللفظ لانه يقتضي التلفظ به او طبع فان طبعه يتأدى الى فهم ذلك المعنى عند سماع اللفظ لا لاجل العلم بالوضع بل بتأدى الطبع اليه السامع عند التلفظ به.
قال المحقق الشريف هذا الاحتمال الاخير مشترك فيه الطبعية والعقلية اذ ليس الفهم في كليهما مستنداً إلى العلم بالوضع فلا يصح فارقاً فالتعويل فى الفرق على احد الطبعين الاخيرين قطعية اذ باقتضاء الطبع صار الدال دالاً على المدلول فيكون منسوبة الى الطبع. (عبد الرحيم) (۱۳) قوله كدلالة اح اح بفتح الهمزة وضمها وقولهم اح الرجل كمه اذا استعمل مولداً منه ليس من اصل لغتهم، ومن الطبعية دلالة اخ يفتح الهمزة وضمها مع تشديد الخاء المعجمة وتخفيفها على الوجع ودلالة اف على التضجر و اوه على التوجع
قال المحقق الشريف في بعض حواشيه اخ بفتح الهمزة وتشديد الخاء الساكنة دال على التحسر وترك في بعض حواشيه قيد المشددة فقال أنه دال على التحزن والحق ما ذكرناه (شیخ عبد الرحيم) (۱۴) قوله و دلالة سرعة النبض على الحمى : هذا مثال للدلالة الطبعية غير اللفظية و المفهوم من شرح المطالع والمطول ان دلالة ما ليس بلفظ :قسمان وضعية كدلالة الخطوط واخواتها وعقلية كدلالة الأثر على المؤثر فاقسام الدلالة خمسة وليست هيهنا دلالة طبعية غير لفظية، والحق ما ذهب اليه المحشى (ره) فان دلالة الحمرة على الخجل و الصفرة على الوجل من الطبعية غير اللفظية و كذا دلالة حركة النبض على المزاج المخصوص.
فان نوقش بانها من قبيل دلالة الاثر على المؤثر اواحد معلولى علة على آخر امكن اجرائها في اح اح ايضاً فان فرق بان الطبعية تضطر في هذه الصورة الى اصدار هذه الآثار بخلاف اح اح ، تمنع الاضطرار في الثاني ايضاً لاسيما عند اشتداد المرض.
والتحقيق انه ان كان المرض المخصوص مستلزماً للصوت المعين والمزاج المعين للحركة المعينة و الكيفيات النفسانية لتلك الالوان استلزاماً عقلياً كانت لها دلالة عقلية و لا ينافي ذلك تحقق الدلالة الطبعية ايضاً فان من لا يعرف الارتباط العقلي بين تلك الدوال و مدلولاتها ينتقل اليها بمجرد ممارسته عادة الطبيعة ايضاً ولا شك ان هذه الدلالة ليست عقلية لانها ليست مستندة الى العلاقة العقلية حتى لو فرضنا انتفائها كانت باقية على حالها وبالجملة تحقق الطبعية فى غير اللفظ ظاهر و من امثلتها ركض الدابة الارض بيدها عند مشاهدة الشعير ودلالة اخذ المستمع للنغمات الطيبة في الرقص على وزانها على تأثير تلك النغمات في نفس ذلك المرتقص و على ان طبعه يقتضى ان يتحرك تلك الحركات اذا تأثر من طيب الاحوال و ملائم الاصوات وقس على ذلك عروض بعض الاوضاع لوجه المتألم وحاجبه عند الله. (شيخ عبد الرحيم) (۱۵) قوله كدلالة لفظ ديز المسموع من وراء الجدار: انما اختار لفظاً مهملاً و ان كان دلالة اللفظ الموضوع على وجود اللافظ ايضاً دلالة عقلية و لذا الجمهور باللفظ مطلقاً، لثلايتوهم المبتدي في بادى الرأى ان هذه الدلالة انما هي بالوضع لا بالعقل و ان كان هذا التوهم منه باطلا فان دلالته بالوضع انما هي دلالته على ما وضع له لا على وجود اللافظ.
غير
وقيل: لانه لو كان موضوعاً لكان لللفظ دلالتان وضعية وعقلية فلا يظهر ما قصد بالتمثيل كمال الظهور.

و اما تقييده بكونه مسموعاً من وراء الجدار الجدر بفتح الجيم وسكون الدال و الجدار الحائط، الجدار الجُدر بضم الجيم والدال و جمع الجدر: جدران بضم الجيم وسكون (الدال مع ان تلك الدلالة متحققة في المسموع من المشاهد ايضاً، فقيل: ليظهر دلالة اللفظ فان وجود اللافظ المشاهد يعلم بالحس ايضاً ودلالة اللفظ فى جنبه بمنزلة العدم بخلاف المسموع من وراء الجدار، و لعل هذا مراد المحقق الشريف في حاشيتى شرح المطالع و شرح الرسالة حيث قال في الاولى و التقييد بذلك اشارة الى ان
(9) That is, if the source of the aspect mentioned in the definition of signification is the positing of the positor, i.e., his specifying the sign for the signified, then the sign is conventional, i.e., attributed to convention because convention has an entry in it. And the word 'according to' if it is governed by a preposition, its sin is vowelized with fatha and its alif is quiescent, and perhaps it is made quiescent in poetic necessity according to the first view. (10) Because it is attributed to the convention which has an entry in this signification. (11) And they are lines, knots, gestures, and signs. As for signification of lines, like the signification of the written letters of Zayd for the individual essence; and as for signification of knots, like the signification of the tying of fingers in the science of finger-counting for a rank of the ranks of numbers; and as for gestures, like the signification of a frowning gesture for entering and exiting, for example; and as for signs, like the signification of a signpost erected to know the road, for example, for the road. And there is no doubt that all of that is only due to convention. (12) His saying: 'and if it is due to the requirement of nature': he intended by it the nature of the speaker, for it requires his uttering that word upon the occurrence of the meaning, as the author said after giving an example of natural signification by his saying: 'like the signification of 'akh akh' for chest pain.' For the nature of the speaker requires uttering it upon the occurrence of pain to him, or the nature of the meaning of the word because it requires uttering it, or a nature such that his nature leads to understanding that meaning upon hearing the word, not due to knowledge of the convention but due to the leading of nature to it for the listener upon the utterance. Al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharif said: 'This last possibility is shared between the natural and the intellectual, since understanding in both is not based on knowledge of the convention, so it is not valid as a differentiator. So reliance in the difference on one of the two last natures is decisive, for due to the requirement of nature the sign becomes a sign for the signified, so it is attributed to nature.' (13) His saying: 'like the signification of 'akh akh'—with fatha and damma of the hamza, and their saying 'akhha al-rajulu' is a derived usage, not from the original of their language. And from natural signification is the signification of 'akh' with fatha and damma of the hamza and with tashdid of the khā' and its takhfif for pain, and the signification of 'uff' for vexation, and 'awh' for affliction. Al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharif said in some of his glosses: 'Akh' with fatha of the hamza and tashdid of the quiescent khā' indicates regret, and he omitted in some of his glosses the condition of tashdid and said it indicates sorrow. The truth is what we mentioned. (14) His saying: 'and the signification of the rapid pulse for fever': this is an example of non-linguistic natural signification. And what is understood from Sharh al-Matāli‘ and al-Mutawwal is that the signification of what is not a word is two types: conventional, like the signification of lines and their like, and intellectual, like the signification of the effect for the cause. So the divisions of signification are five, and there is no non-linguistic natural signification here. But the truth is what the annotator (may God have mercy on him) held: for the signification of redness for shame and yellowness for fear are non-linguistic natural significations, and likewise the signification of the movement of the pulse for the particular temperament. For if it is objected that they belong to the type of signification of the effect for the cause or one correlate of a cause for another, it could be applied also to 'akh akh'. If one distinguishes by saying that nature compels [1] in this case to produce these effects unlike 'akh akh', we deny compulsion in the second also, especially when illness intensifies. And the verification is that if the particular illness necessitates the particular sound and the particular temperament necessitates the particular movement and the psychological qualities necessitate those colors by intellectual necessity, then they have intellectual signification. But that does not preclude the existence of natural signification as well. For one who does not know the intellectual connection between those signs and their significations still transitions to them merely by his practice of natural habit. And there is no doubt that this signification is not intellectual because it is not based on an intellectual relation, such that if we suppose its absence it would remain as it was. In short, the existence of natural signification in what is not a word is evident. Among its examples are the animal pawing the ground with its hoof upon seeing barley, and the signification of the listener’s being moved to dance in rhythm to pleasant melodies—that these melodies affect the soul of that dancer, and that his nature requires that he move those movements when affected by pleasant states and suitable sounds. And analogize to that the occurrence of certain expressions on the face of the one in pain and his eyebrow [looking] toward God. (15) His saying: 'like the signification of the word 'dīz' heard from behind a wall': he chose a meaningless word intentionally, although the signification of a conventional word for the existence of the speaker is also intellectual. Therefore the general rule is with the absolute [conventional] word, in order that the beginner does not initially suppose that this signification is only by convention, not by intellect, even if this supposition of his is false. For its signification by convention is only its signification for what it is posited for, not for the existence of the speaker. And it is said: because if it were conventional, the word would have two significations, conventional and intellectual, so what is intended by the example would not be fully apparent. And as for his restricting it to being heard from behind a wall—'al-jidr' with fatha of the jīm and sukūn of the dāl, and 'al-jidār' is the wall, 'al-judur' with damma of the jīm and dāl, and plural of 'judr' is 'judrān' with damma of the jīm and sukūn of the dāl—even though that signification is also realized in the heard from a seen [speaker], so it is said: to make apparent the signification of the word, for the existence of the seen speaker is also known by sense, and the signification of the word in his case is like non-existence, unlike the heard from behind a wall. And perhaps this is the intention of al-Muhaqqiq al-Sharīf in his two glosses on Sharh al-Matāli‘ and Sharh al-Risāla where he said in the first: 'and the restriction to that indicates that...'
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the three types of signification (dalala): conventional (wad‘iyya), natural (tabi‘iyya), and intellectual (‘aqliyya). The commentator begins by defining conventional signification as that which originates from the positing of a positor, i.e., the deliberate assignment of a sign to a meaning. He includes examples of non-linguistic conventional signs: lines (written letters), knots (finger-counting), gestures (frowning to indicate entry/exit), and physical signs (road signs). All these are conventional because they depend on human agreement. Next, natural signification is explained through the phrase 'due to the requirement of nature' (iqtiḍā’ al-ṭab‘). The commentator offers three interpretations: the nature of the speaker compels him to utter certain sounds upon experiencing a meaning (e.g., 'akh! akh!' when in pain); the nature of the meaning itself prompts utterance; or the nature of the listener leads him to understand a meaning upon hearing the sound without knowledge of convention. The last interpretation is noted to be shared with intellectual signification, so the proper differentiator is that the sign is a sign due to natural compulsion. Examples of natural linguistic signs include interjections like 'akh' (for pain), 'uff' (vexation), and 'awh' (affliction). The discussion then turns to non-linguistic natural signification, such as a rapid pulse indicating fever, redness indicating shame, and pallor indicating fear. The commentator defends the existence of such signification against objections that these are actually intellectual (causal) relations. He argues that even if an intellectual relation exists, natural signification can coexist, as demonstrated by animals and people who respond instinctively without grasping the intellectual connection, like a horse pawing the ground upon seeing barley or a person dancing involuntarily to pleasant music. Finally, intellectual signification is exemplified by hearing the meaningless word 'dīz' from behind a wall; the mere sound of an utterance indicates the existence of a speaker, which is an intellectual inference. The commentator explains why a meaningless word is chosen: to avoid confusion with conventional signification. He also clarifies the restriction 'from behind a wall' ensures that the speaker’s existence is not known by direct sense, so the signification is purely intellectual. The passage ends abruptly mid-sentence, but the core typology and illustrations are complete.

Checkpoint
What is the commentator's main argument against the claim that non-linguistic natural signification does not exist?
A) He says that all non-linguistic signs are actually conventional
B) He provides examples like blushing and the rapid pulse, and argues they are not intellectual because the connection is instinctive and persists even for those unaware of the causal link
C) He admits the claim is correct but redefines natural signification to include only linguistic signs
D) He relies on the authority of earlier logicians without giving counterexamples

Key Takeaway
Natural signification exists in non-linguistic forms like blushing and involuntary movements, based on instinctive impulse, not convention or intellectual inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The commentator argues that a horse pawing the ground upon seeing barley is natural signification. Identify whether this is linguistic or non-linguistic, and explain why it is not intellectual according to his criteria.
?
Consider the Islamic practice of raising the index finger during tashahhud (testimony of faith). Is this a conventional, natural, or intellectual sign? Justify your answer using distinctions from the passage.

The Conditioned Division of Singular Terms
Author defends the division of singular terms by conditioning it on unity/multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given singular term is included in the division of singular terms based on its qualification by unity or multiplicity of meaning.

Core Idea
Not every singular term is divided; only those qualified by unity or multiplicity.

The author begins by clarifying that the division of singular into types (proper noun, univocal, etc.) is not unconditional. Verbs and particles, for instance, may fall outside if they are not qualified by unity or multiplicity. This refinement answers a potential objection about the completeness of the division. Let's trace the logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the muqassam (divided term)
The muqassam here is 'the singular' (al-mufrad), but not absolutely; it requires a condition. Look for the phrase 'the division of the singular is not absolute but conditioned'.
The condition is explicitly stated in the opening sentence.
2
Extract the condition
The singular term must be qualified by either 'ittihad' (unity) or 'takathur' (multiplicity) of meaning. The passage gives 'in' and its counterpart as examples.
Verbs and particles are excluded if they are not so qualified.
3
Apply the condition to verbs and particles
If a verb or particle does not exhibit unity or multiplicity (e.g., a simple verb form considered without context), it is not part of the division. This prevents the objection that the enumeration is incomplete.
The author notes this avoids the falsity of the enumeration.
4
Understand the deeper point about universality
Universality and particularity are attributes of meanings, not words. The division ultimately concerns meanings, not mere word forms.
This is stated explicitly in the passage.

تصور معنى
(۶۸) اعلم انه لابد لتحقيق الحال من تفصيل المقال بايراد مقدمة ذكرها بعض الافاضل في نظير المقام على نحو من الاجمال غير مفض الى الاخلال وهى انه لاريب ان الواضع اذا اراد وضع لفظ لابد له ان يتصور له معنى والا لامتنع منه ذلك ضرورة ان الوضع أنما هو نسبة بين شيئين، فحينئذ فان جزئياً وعين بازائه لفظاً مخصوصاً او الفاظاً مخصوصة متصورة تفصيلاً او اجمالاً، يكون خاصاً الخصوص التصور المعتبر فيه اعنى: تصور المعنى، والموضوع له ايضاً خاصاً وهو ظاهر و ان تصور معنى عاماً تحته جزئيات اضافية او حقيقية فله ان يعين لفظاً معلوماً او الفاظاً معلومة بالتفصيل او الاجمال بازاء ذلك المعنى العام ، فيكون الوضع عاماً لعموم التصور المعتبر فيه، والموضوع له ايضاً عاماً وله ان معلوماً أو الفاظاً معلومة بالتفصيل او الاجمال بازاء خصوصيات الجزئيات المندرجة تحته لانها معلومة اجمالاً إذا توجه العقل بذلك المفهوم العام نحوها والعلم الاجمالى كاف فى الوضع فيكون الوضع عاماً والموضوع له خاصاً. اذا تمهد هذا فنقول :

لفظاً
اختلفوا في تحقيق أن وضع الضماير واسماء الاشارة والموصولات و المعرف باللام وغيرها مما سوى العلم هل هو من القسم الثاني او الثالث؟ وذهب الى كل فريق والمختار عند المصنف لما كان هو الأول
اشتمل قوله ان اتحد معناه عليها فاخرجها بقوله وضعاً على ما ذكره المحشى (ره). (محمد علی (ره) (۶۹) قوله وهيهنا كلام وهوان المراد بالمعنى الذي هو فاعل لقوله ان اتحد معناه و ان كثر فى هذا التق التقسيم اى تقسيم المفرد الى العلم والمتواطى والمشكك و المشترك والمنقول والحقيقة و المجاز، اما الموضوع له اللفظ تحقيقا او ما استعمل فيه اللفظ سواء كان الاستعمال عن وضع تحقيق او تأويلي كما في في الدلالات ٢٠٧ الاستعارات فان اردنا بالمعنى ما وضع له اللفظ تحقيقاً فلا يصح عد الحقيقة والمجاز من اقسام متكثر المعنى لان المعنى الموضوع له بالتحقيق واحد وهو الحقيقة والمجاز ليس بموضوع له بالتحقيق وان أردنا بالمعنى ما استعمل فيه اللفظ سواء كان مستند الاستعمال هو الوضع التحقيق او التأو على يدخل نحو اسماء الاشارة مما هو موضوع بالوضع العام والموضوع له عام ايضاً في قسم متكثر المعنى لان المعنى الموضوع له عام فموارد استعمال اللفظ الموضوع له كثيرة لعمومية معناه الذى وضع له وهكذا يدخل المتواطى والمشكك في قسم متكثر المعنى لان المعانى المستعمل فيها لفظ المتواطى والمشكك كثيرة و ان كانت من عنصر واحد وعليه فلاحاجة الى اخراج نحو اسماء الاشارة على رأى المصنف الى التقييد بقوله وضعاً. وعلى كل حال فالمصنف يلزمه احدامرين لا محالة فانه اما ان يقول : اريد من قولى ان اتحد معناه، معناه الموضوع له اللفظ تحقيقاً فالحقيقة والمجاز يخرجان عن قسم متكثر المعنى لان الموضوع له اللفظ بالتحقيق هو الحقيقة وحدها. واما ان يقول اريد معناه المستعمل فيه اللفظ اعم من ان يكون الاستعمال من جهة الوضع التحقيق أو التأويلى فالحقيقة والمجاز يكونان من قسم متكثر المعنى وكذلك نحو اسماء الاشارة والمتواطى والمشكك فيكون قوله «وضعاً زائداً بالطبع لان المفروض هو المعنى المستعمل فيه لا الموضوع له بالتحقيق جيء بها لطرد نحو اسماء الاشارة عن متحد المعنى بالوضع فاذا دخلت اسماء الاشارة ونظائرها في متكثر المعنى يكون هذا القيد زائداً. ويمكن الجواب عنه بما يبقى الحقيقة والمجاز فى متكثر المعنى ويصحح التقييد بكلمة «وضعاً» وهوان يراد بقوله: «اتحد معناه المعنى الموضوع له اللفظ بالتحقيق وبقوله: «وان كثر المعنى المستعمل فيه وهذا لامانع منه وهو المسمى بالاستخدام من ابواب البديع كما في قوله : شبوه و سقى الغضا والساكنيه وانهم بين جوانح و قلوب و قوله : اذا نزل السماء بارض قوم رعیناه و ان كانوا غضابا و قوله : رأى العقيق فاجرى ذاك ناظره متيم لج في الاشواق خاطره و قوله : يا تاركي في حبه ابدا حديثي ليس بالمنسوخ مثلا من الامثال سائر الا في الدفاتر (التقريب ص ٢٢-٢٣)
(۷۰) اى فى قوله و ان كثر الخ بعيد هذا وذلك لان الموضوع له الحقيقى في الحقيقة والمجاز لا يلزم ان يكون متكثراً كما لا يخفى. (محمد على)
(۷۱) لان المستعمل فيه فيها ليس بمتحد بل متكثر كما هو ظاهر وايضاً على هذا لا يصح جعل المتواطى والمشكك من اقسام متحد المعنى ضرورة ان المستعمل فيه فيها ايضاً ليس بمتحد بل متكثر و كانه لم يتعرض له المحشى لظهوره
We say: The division of the singular is not absolute but conditioned by its being qualified by unity or multiplicity, as is apparent from the word 'in' and its counterpart. The verb and particle, if they are not so qualified, are not included in the divided term in reality, so that the falsity of the enumeration would not be avoided by their exclusion from the divisions while the division is for the absolute singular. This is a detailed explanation of what I alluded to in the text, which I presented in the form of question and answer from him (may God have mercy on him). (65) His saying: 'The verb and particle when they are united in meaning' i.e., when their materials have a single meaning, not multiple, like homonyms. (66) The point of reflection is that the criterion of universality and particularity is based on meaning, and the particle's meaning is not independent, and the verb also, according to the meaning of correspondence, is not independent, and their meanings are not described by them. So universality and particularity are in reality attributes of the meanings of words, not attributes of the words themselves. (67) That is, the purpose of 'unity of meaning' is not the agreement of the word in number, as is understood, otherwise it would follow that different proper nouns placed for one meaning would not be proper nouns due to the lack of agreement of word and meaning then, while they are not like that. (Muhammad Ali). (And Shaykh Abd al-Rahim (may God have mercy on him) said in verifying the statement of the annotator): If his purpose from the interpretation by unity is that the meaning of the singular being united is that it has one meaning in reality, then it would follow that common proper nouns are not proper nouns. And if his purpose is that what is meant by the unity of meaning of the singular is that the meaning is one, whether in reality or by consideration and observation, then there is no problem, but the first is what is understood. (68) Know that to realize the matter, it is necessary to detail the discourse by presenting a premise that some of the virtuous have mentioned in a similar context in a way of conciseness not leading to deficiency, which is: There is no doubt that when the assigner (wadi') wants to place a word, he must conceive a meaning for it, otherwise it is impossible for him, necessarily because placing is a relation between two things. Then, if he conceives a particular meaning and a specific word or specific words corresponding to it, conceived in detail or in summary, then it is particular: the conception considered in it, i.e., the conception of the meaning, and the object of placing is also particular, and that is apparent. And if he conceives a general meaning under which there are additional or real particulars, then he may assign a known word or known words in detail or in summary for that general meaning; then the placing is general due to the general conception considered in it, and the object of placing is also general. And he may assign a known word or known words in detail or in summary for the specific particularities subsumed under it, because they are known in summary when the intellect turns towards that general concept to them, and summary knowledge suffices for placing; then the placing is general and the object of placing is particular. If this is established, we say: They differed in verifying whether the placing of pronouns, demonstratives, relative pronouns, the definite article, and other than proper nouns is from the second type or the third? Each group holds an opinion, and the chosen view according to the author is that it is the first. So his saying 'if its meaning is united' included them, so he excluded them by his saying 'by placing' as stated by the annotator (may God have mercy on him). (69) His saying: 'Here is a statement' and it is that what is meant by 'meaning' which is the subject of his saying 'if its meaning is united, even if it is multiple' in this division, i.e., the division of the singular into proper noun, univocal, equivocal, homonym, transferred, literal, and figurative: is it the actual object of placing of the word or what is used in the word, whether the usage is from actual placing or interpretative, as in metaphors? For if we mean by 'meaning' what is actually placed for the word, then it is not correct to count literal and figurative as divisions of multiple meaning because the actual object of placing is one, i.e., the literal, and the figurative is not actually placed. And if we mean by 'meaning' what is used in the word, whether the basis of usage is actual placing or interpretative, then demonstratives etc., which are placed with general placing and the object of placing is also general, enter the division of multiple meaning because the object of placing is general, so the instances of usage of the word placed are many due to the generality of its meaning which is placed. Similarly, univocal and equivocal enter the division of multiple meaning because the meanings used in the word of univocal and equivocal are many, even if they are from one element. Accordingly, there is no need to exclude demonstratives etc. according to the author's view by the qualification 'by placing'. In any case, the author is inevitably faced with one of two things: Either he says: I mean by my saying 'its meaning is united', 'its meaning actually placed for the word' — then literal and figurative exit from the division of multiple meaning because the actual object of placing is only the literal. Or he says: I mean its meaning used in the word, whether usage is from actual placing or interpretative — then literal and figurative are from the division of multiple meaning, and similarly demonstratives etc., univocal, and equivocal. Then his word 'by placing' is superfluous by nature because the assumed is the meaning used, not the actual object of placing; it was brought to exclude demonstratives etc. from the united-in-meaning by placing. So if demonstratives and their likes enter multiple meaning, this qualification becomes superfluous. And it can be answered in a way that keeps literal and figurative in multiple meaning and corrects the qualification by the word 'by placing', which is that by his saying 'its meaning is united' he means the meaning actually placed for the word, and by his saying 'even if it is multiple' he means the meaning used in it, and there is no obstacle to this; it is called 'istikhdam' from the chapters of badi', as in his saying: 'Wa saqa al-ghada wa al-sakinihi wa annahum bayna jawarihi wa qulub', and his saying: 'Idha nazala al-sama'u bi-ardi qawmin ra'ainahu wa in kanu ghidaba', and his saying: 'Ra'a al-'aqiq fa-ajra dhalika nazirahu mutayyamun lajja fi al-ashwaqi khatiruhu', and his saying: 'Ya tariki fi hubbihi abada hadithi laysa bi-l-mansukh', for example from the well-known examples, except in the notebooks. (70) That is, in his saying 'even if it is multiple' etc., this is far because the actual object of placing in literal and figurative does not have to be multiple, as is not hidden. (71) Because the used meaning in them is not united but multiple, as is apparent. Also, on this, it is not correct to make univocal and equivocal from the divisions of united meaning, necessarily because the used meaning in them is also not united but multiple. It seems the annotator did not address it due to its obviousness.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a super-commentary (hashiya) that critically refines the earlier commentary's classification of singular terms (mufrad) and the concept of 'unity of meaning' (ittihad al-ma'na). The author begins by defending the original division: the division of singular into types (proper noun, univocal, etc.) is not absolute but conditioned on the term being qualified by either unity or multiplicity. Verbs and particles, if not so qualified, are excluded from the division. This answers an objection about the enumeration being incomplete.

The author then clarifies that universality and particularity are attributes of meanings, not of words themselves. The term 'unity of meaning' does not mean the word agrees in number (like having one name for multiple referents), but rather the meaning is one in itself.

A key premise is then presented about types of wad' (linguistic positing). There are three types: (1) particular wad' and particular object (e.g., proper names), (2) general wad' and general object (e.g., 'insan' for the universal human), and (3) general wad' but particular object (e.g., pronouns and demonstratives, where the meaning is general but each instance is particular). The author notes that scholars disagreed on the classification of pronouns, demonstratives, etc., and the original author (musannif) considered them as type (1) or (2) and thus excluded them with the qualification 'bi-wad''.

A detailed dilemma arises about the term 'meaning'. If 'meaning' means the actual object of placing, then figurative (majaz) and literal (haqiqa) cannot both be in the 'multiple meaning' division, since only the literal is actually placed. If 'meaning' means what is used in speech, then demonstratives etc. would fall under 'multiple meaning', making the qualification redundant. The author offers a resolution using the rhetorical device 'istikhdam', where 'meaning' is understood differently in two parts of the same statement: in 'itihada ma'nahu' it refers to the placed meaning (so demonstratives are excluded), and in 'wa in kathura' it refers to the used meaning (so literal and figurative are included). This preserves the original classification.

Finally, marginal notes critique this resolution, suggesting it is far-fetched, and note that if usage is taken as criterion, then univocal and equivocal would also be multiple in meaning, contradicting their classification as united-in-meaning. The annotator (muhashshi) is said to have omitted discussion of this due to its obviousness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are verbs and particles sometimes excluded from the division?
A) Because their meanings are independent.
B) Because they are not singular terms.
C) Because they may not be qualified by unity or multiplicity.
D) Because they are always equivocal.

Key Takeaway
The division of singular terms applies only to those terms whose meanings are either united or multiple, not to all singular expressions.

The Three Types of Linguistic Positing (Wad')
Author presents the premise of three wad' types to clarify the classification of pronouns and demonstratives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the positing of any term (e.g., proper names, common nouns, demonstratives) into one of three types: particular-particular, general-general, or general-particular.

Core Idea
The type of positing is determined by whether the conceived meaning and the assigned meaning are particular or general.

The author presents a premise about the three ways a language assigner (wadi') can pair a meaning with a word. This classification is crucial for understanding terms like pronouns and demonstratives, which have a general meaning but apply to particular instances. Let's examine each type and its application to the debate about pronouns.

Guided Path
1
Understand that wad' requires conception
The assigner must conceive a meaning before assigning a word. This is necessary for the relation between word and meaning.
The author states this as a premise.
2
First type: particular wad', particular object
If the assigner conceives a particular meaning and assigns a specific word for it, the positing and the object are both particular. Example: proper names like 'Zaid'.
This is the simplest case.
3
Second type: general wad', general object
If the assigner conceives a general meaning and assigns a word for that general meaning, both are general. Example: 'insan' for the species human.
The word applies to all individuals falling under that concept.
4
Third type: general wad', particular object
If the assigner conceives a general meaning but assigns a word for the particular instances under it, then the positing is general, but the object is particular. Example: demonstrative 'hadha' (this), which is assigned for each particular referent.
This type is key for pronouns and demonstratives.
5
Apply to the debate
Scholars differed: are pronouns etc. from the second or third type? The author's chosen view is that they are from the first type, thus excluded by the qualification 'by placing'.
The passage says 'the chosen view according to the author is that it is the first'.

يكون هذا القيد زائداً. ويمكن الجواب عنه بما يبقى الحقيقة والمجاز فى متكثر المعنى ويصحح التقييد بكلمة «وضعاً» وهوان يراد بقوله: «اتحد معناه المعنى الموضوع له اللفظ بالتحقيق وبقوله: «وان كثر المعنى المستعمل فيه وهذا لامانع منه وهو المسمى بالاستخدام من ابواب البديع كما في قوله : شبوه و سقى الغضا والساكنيه وانهم بين جوانح و قلوب و قوله : اذا نزل السماء بارض قوم رعیناه و ان كانوا غضابا و قوله : رأى العقيق فاجرى ذاك ناظره متيم لج في الاشواق خاطره و قوله : يا تاركي في حبه ابدا حديثي ليس بالمنسوخ مثلا من الامثال سائر الا في الدفاتر (التقريب ص ٢٢-٢٣)
(۷۰) اى فى قوله و ان كثر الخ بعيد هذا وذلك لان الموضوع له الحقيقى في الحقيقة والمجاز لا يلزم ان يكون متكثراً كما لا يخفى. (محمد على)
(۷۱) لان المستعمل فيه فيها ليس بمتحد بل متكثر كما هو ظاهر وايضاً على هذا لا يصح جعل المتواطى والمشكك من اقسام متحد المعنى ضرورة ان المستعمل فيه فيها ايضاً ليس بمتحد بل متكثر و كانه لم يتعرض له المحشى لظهوره
We say: The division of the singular is not absolute but conditioned by its being qualified by unity or multiplicity, as is apparent from the word 'in' and its counterpart. The verb and particle, if they are not so qualified, are not included in the divided term in reality, so that the falsity of the enumeration would not be avoided by their exclusion from the divisions while the division is for the absolute singular. This is a detailed explanation of what I alluded to in the text, which I presented in the form of question and answer from him (may God have mercy on him). (65) His saying: 'The verb and particle when they are united in meaning' i.e., when their materials have a single meaning, not multiple, like homonyms. (66) The point of reflection is that the criterion of universality and particularity is based on meaning, and the particle's meaning is not independent, and the verb also, according to the meaning of correspondence, is not independent, and their meanings are not described by them. So universality and particularity are in reality attributes of the meanings of words, not attributes of the words themselves. (67) That is, the purpose of 'unity of meaning' is not the agreement of the word in number, as is understood, otherwise it would follow that different proper nouns placed for one meaning would not be proper nouns due to the lack of agreement of word and meaning then, while they are not like that. (Muhammad Ali). (And Shaykh Abd al-Rahim (may God have mercy on him) said in verifying the statement of the annotator): If his purpose from the interpretation by unity is that the meaning of the singular being united is that it has one meaning in reality, then it would follow that common proper nouns are not proper nouns. And if his purpose is that what is meant by the unity of meaning of the singular is that the meaning is one, whether in reality or by consideration and observation, then there is no problem, but the first is what is understood. (68) Know that to realize the matter, it is necessary to detail the discourse by presenting a premise that some of the virtuous have mentioned in a similar context in a way of conciseness not leading to deficiency, which is: There is no doubt that when the assigner (wadi') wants to place a word, he must conceive a meaning for it, otherwise it is impossible for him, necessarily because placing is a relation between two things. Then, if he conceives a particular meaning and a specific word or specific words corresponding to it, conceived in detail or in summary, then it is particular: the conception considered in it, i.e., the conception of the meaning, and the object of placing is also particular, and that is apparent. And if he conceives a general meaning under which there are additional or real particulars, then he may assign a known word or known words in detail or in summary for that general meaning; then the placing is general due to the general conception considered in it, and the object of placing is also general. And he may assign a known word or known words in detail or in summary for the specific particularities subsumed under it, because they are known in summary when the intellect turns towards that general concept to them, and summary knowledge suffices for placing; then the placing is general and the object of placing is particular. If this is established, we say: They differed in verifying whether the placing of pronouns, demonstratives, relative pronouns, the definite article, and other than proper nouns is from the second type or the third? Each group holds an opinion, and the chosen view according to the author is that it is the first. So his saying 'if its meaning is united' included them, so he excluded them by his saying 'by placing' as stated by the annotator (may God have mercy on him). (69) His saying: 'Here is a statement' and it is that what is meant by 'meaning' which is the subject of his saying 'if its meaning is united, even if it is multiple' in this division, i.e., the division of the singular into proper noun, univocal, equivocal, homonym, transferred, literal, and figurative: is it the actual object of placing of the word or what is used in the word, whether the usage is from actual placing or interpretative, as in metaphors? For if we mean by 'meaning' what is actually placed for the word, then it is not correct to count literal and figurative as divisions of multiple meaning because the actual object of placing is one, i.e., the literal, and the figurative is not actually placed. And if we mean by 'meaning' what is used in the word, whether the basis of usage is actual placing or interpretative, then demonstratives etc., which are placed with general placing and the object of placing is also general, enter the division of multiple meaning because the object of placing is general, so the instances of usage of the word placed are many due to the generality of its meaning which is placed. Similarly, univocal and equivocal enter the division of multiple meaning because the meanings used in the word of univocal and equivocal are many, even if they are from one element. Accordingly, there is no need to exclude demonstratives etc. according to the author's view by the qualification 'by placing'. In any case, the author is inevitably faced with one of two things: Either he says: I mean by my saying 'its meaning is united', 'its meaning actually placed for the word' — then literal and figurative exit from the division of multiple meaning because the actual object of placing is only the literal. Or he says: I mean its meaning used in the word, whether usage is from actual placing or interpretative — then literal and figurative are from the division of multiple meaning, and similarly demonstratives etc., univocal, and equivocal. Then his word 'by placing' is superfluous by nature because the assumed is the meaning used, not the actual object of placing; it was brought to exclude demonstratives etc. from the united-in-meaning by placing. So if demonstratives and their likes enter multiple meaning, this qualification becomes superfluous. And it can be answered in a way that keeps literal and figurative in multiple meaning and corrects the qualification by the word 'by placing', which is that by his saying 'its meaning is united' he means the meaning actually placed for the word, and by his saying 'even if it is multiple' he means the meaning used in it, and there is no obstacle to this; it is called 'istikhdam' from the chapters of badi', as in his saying: 'Wa saqa al-ghada wa al-sakinihi wa annahum bayna jawarihi wa qulub', and his saying: 'Idha nazala al-sama'u bi-ardi qawmin ra'ainahu wa in kanu ghidaba', and his saying: 'Ra'a al-'aqiq fa-ajra dhalika nazirahu mutayyamun lajja fi al-ashwaqi khatiruhu', and his saying: 'Ya tariki fi hubbihi abada hadithi laysa bi-l-mansukh', for example from the well-known examples, except in the notebooks. (70) That is, in his saying 'even if it is multiple' etc., this is far because the actual object of placing in literal and figurative does not have to be multiple, as is not hidden. (71) Because the used meaning in them is not united but multiple, as is apparent. Also, on this, it is not correct to make univocal and equivocal from the divisions of united meaning, necessarily because the used meaning in them is also not united but multiple. It seems the annotator did not address it due to its obviousness.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a super-commentary (hashiya) that critically refines the earlier commentary's classification of singular terms (mufrad) and the concept of 'unity of meaning' (ittihad al-ma'na). The author begins by defending the original division: the division of singular into types (proper noun, univocal, etc.) is not absolute but conditioned on the term being qualified by either unity or multiplicity. Verbs and particles, if not so qualified, are excluded from the division. This answers an objection about the enumeration being incomplete.

The author then clarifies that universality and particularity are attributes of meanings, not of words themselves. The term 'unity of meaning' does not mean the word agrees in number (like having one name for multiple referents), but rather the meaning is one in itself.

A key premise is then presented about types of wad' (linguistic positing). There are three types: (1) particular wad' and particular object (e.g., proper names), (2) general wad' and general object (e.g., 'insan' for the universal human), and (3) general wad' but particular object (e.g., pronouns and demonstratives, where the meaning is general but each instance is particular). The author notes that scholars disagreed on the classification of pronouns, demonstratives, etc., and the original author (musannif) considered them as type (1) or (2) and thus excluded them with the qualification 'bi-wad''.

A detailed dilemma arises about the term 'meaning'. If 'meaning' means the actual object of placing, then figurative (majaz) and literal (haqiqa) cannot both be in the 'multiple meaning' division, since only the literal is actually placed. If 'meaning' means what is used in speech, then demonstratives etc. would fall under 'multiple meaning', making the qualification redundant. The author offers a resolution using the rhetorical device 'istikhdam', where 'meaning' is understood differently in two parts of the same statement: in 'itihada ma'nahu' it refers to the placed meaning (so demonstratives are excluded), and in 'wa in kathura' it refers to the used meaning (so literal and figurative are included). This preserves the original classification.

Finally, marginal notes critique this resolution, suggesting it is far-fetched, and note that if usage is taken as criterion, then univocal and equivocal would also be multiple in meaning, contradicting their classification as united-in-meaning. The annotator (muhashshi) is said to have omitted discussion of this due to its obviousness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when the wadi' conceives a general meaning and assigns a word for the particular instances under it, this is an example of:
A) Particular wad' and particular object
B) General wad' and general object
C) General wad' and particular object
D) Particular wad' and general object

Key Takeaway
The three types of positing show that the correlation between the generality of the assigned meaning and the object of assignment can vary, which affects how we classify terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the wad' of the following terms: (a) the proper name 'Zaid', (b) the common noun 'rajul' (man), (c) the demonstrative 'hadha' (this). Justify each using the three types.

12 min
The Dilemma of Meaning and Its Resolution via Istikhdam
Author presents a dilemma about the referent of 'meaning' and resolves it with istikhdam, but faces objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dilemma regarding the term 'meaning' in the division of singular terms and evaluate the proposed resolution using istikhdam.

Core Idea
The word 'meaning' can refer to placed meaning or used meaning, allowing a consistent classification.

The core of the critical refinement comes here. The annotator raises a dilemma: if 'meaning' in the division refers to the actual placed meaning, then figurative (majaz) and literal (haqiqa) cannot both be in the 'multiple meaning' group. If it refers to used meaning, then demonstratives etc. would be multiple, making the qualification 'by placing' redundant. The author offers a neat resolution using the rhetorical device istikhdam, but it faces critique. Let's unpack the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possible referents of 'meaning'
The phrase 'if its meaning is united, even if it is multiple' contains 'meaning' which could refer to (a) the actual object of placing (al-mawdu' lahu haqiqatan) or (b) the meaning used in speech (al-musta'mal fih).
The passage explicitly sets up these two options.
2
Explore consequence of option (a)
If 'meaning' means placed meaning, then literal (haqiqa) is placed, but figurative (majaz) is not actually placed. Thus literal and figurative cannot both be in the 'multiple meaning' division. Only literal would be multiple? Actually literal is single; so that's problematic.
The passage says: 'then it is not correct to count literal and figurative as divisions of multiple meaning because the actual object of placing is one, i.e., the literal.'
3
Explore consequence of option (b)
If 'meaning' means used meaning, then demonstratives, proper nouns? Actually, used meaning for demonstratives is multiple (since each use refers to a different instance). Thus they would fall under multiple meaning, making the qualification 'by placing' unnecessary to exclude them.
The passage says: 'then his word 'by placing' is superfluous by nature.'
4
Understand the resolution via istikhdam
The author resolves by interpreting 'its meaning is united' as referring to placed meaning (so demonstratives are excluded), and 'even if it is multiple' as referring to used meaning (so literal and figurative are included). This use of the same word in two senses in one statement is called istikhdam.
Istikhdam is a rhetorical device where a word is used in two different meanings.
5
Consider the critique
The marginal note objects: if used meaning is considered, then univocal and equivocal also have multiple used meanings, so they would be multiple meaning, contradicting their classification as united meaning. The annotator left this unaddressed.
This shows the resolution is not fully satisfactory.

We say: The division of the singular is not absolute but conditioned by its being qualified by unity or multiplicity, as is apparent from the word 'in' and its counterpart. The verb and particle, if they are not so qualified, are not included in the divided term in reality, so that the falsity of the enumeration would not be avoided by their exclusion from the divisions while the division is for the absolute singular. This is a detailed explanation of what I alluded to in the text, which I presented in the form of question and answer from him (may God have mercy on him). (65) His saying: 'The verb and particle when they are united in meaning' i.e., when their materials have a single meaning, not multiple, like homonyms. (66) The point of reflection is that the criterion of universality and particularity is based on meaning, and the particle's meaning is not independent, and the verb also, according to the meaning of correspondence, is not independent, and their meanings are not described by them. So universality and particularity are in reality attributes of the meanings of words, not attributes of the words themselves. (67) That is, the purpose of 'unity of meaning' is not the agreement of the word in number, as is understood, otherwise it would follow that different proper nouns placed for one meaning would not be proper nouns due to the lack of agreement of word and meaning then, while they are not like that. (Muhammad Ali). (And Shaykh Abd al-Rahim (may God have mercy on him) said in verifying the statement of the annotator): If his purpose from the interpretation by unity is that the meaning of the singular being united is that it has one meaning in reality, then it would follow that common proper nouns are not proper nouns. And if his purpose is that what is meant by the unity of meaning of the singular is that the meaning is one, whether in reality or by consideration and observation, then there is no problem, but the first is what is understood. (68) Know that to realize the matter, it is necessary to detail the discourse by presenting a premise that some of the virtuous have mentioned in a similar context in a way of conciseness not leading to deficiency, which is: There is no doubt that when the assigner (wadi') wants to place a word, he must conceive a meaning for it, otherwise it is impossible for him, necessarily because placing is a relation between two things. Then, if he conceives a particular meaning and a specific word or specific words corresponding to it, conceived in detail or in summary, then it is particular: the conception considered in it, i.e., the conception of the meaning, and the object of placing is also particular, and that is apparent. And if he conceives a general meaning under which there are additional or real particulars, then he may assign a known word or known words in detail or in summary for that general meaning; then the placing is general due to the general conception considered in it, and the object of placing is also general. And he may assign a known word or known words in detail or in summary for the specific particularities subsumed under it, because they are known in summary when the intellect turns towards that general concept to them, and summary knowledge suffices for placing; then the placing is general and the object of placing is particular. If this is established, we say: They differed in verifying whether the placing of pronouns, demonstratives, relative pronouns, the definite article, and other than proper nouns is from the second type or the third? Each group holds an opinion, and the chosen view according to the author is that it is the first. So his saying 'if its meaning is united' included them, so he excluded them by his saying 'by placing' as stated by the annotator (may God have mercy on him). (69) His saying: 'Here is a statement' and it is that what is meant by 'meaning' which is the subject of his saying 'if its meaning is united, even if it is multiple' in this division, i.e., the division of the singular into proper noun, univocal, equivocal, homonym, transferred, literal, and figurative: is it the actual object of placing of the word or what is used in the word, whether the usage is from actual placing or interpretative, as in metaphors? For if we mean by 'meaning' what is actually placed for the word, then it is not correct to count literal and figurative as divisions of multiple meaning because the actual object of placing is one, i.e., the literal, and the figurative is not actually placed. And if we mean by 'meaning' what is used in the word, whether the basis of usage is actual placing or interpretative, then demonstratives etc., which are placed with general placing and the object of placing is also general, enter the division of multiple meaning because the object of placing is general, so the instances of usage of the word placed are many due to the generality of its meaning which is placed. Similarly, univocal and equivocal enter the division of multiple meaning because the meanings used in the word of univocal and equivocal are many, even if they are from one element. Accordingly, there is no need to exclude demonstratives etc. according to the author's view by the qualification 'by placing'. In any case, the author is inevitably faced with one of two things: Either he says: I mean by my saying 'its meaning is united', 'its meaning actually placed for the word' — then literal and figurative exit from the division of multiple meaning because the actual object of placing is only the literal. Or he says: I mean its meaning used in the word, whether usage is from actual placing or interpretative — then literal and figurative are from the division of multiple meaning, and similarly demonstratives etc., univocal, and equivocal. Then his word 'by placing' is superfluous by nature because the assumed is the meaning used, not the actual object of placing; it was brought to exclude demonstratives etc. from the united-in-meaning by placing. So if demonstratives and their likes enter multiple meaning, this qualification becomes superfluous. And it can be answered in a way that keeps literal and figurative in multiple meaning and corrects the qualification by the word 'by placing', which is that by his saying 'its meaning is united' he means the meaning actually placed for the word, and by his saying 'even if it is multiple' he means the meaning used in it, and there is no obstacle to this; it is called 'istikhdam' from the chapters of badi', as in his saying: 'Wa saqa al-ghada wa al-sakinihi wa annahum bayna jawarihi wa qulub', and his saying: 'Idha nazala al-sama'u bi-ardi qawmin ra'ainahu wa in kanu ghidaba', and his saying: 'Ra'a al-'aqiq fa-ajra dhalika nazirahu mutayyamun lajja fi al-ashwaqi khatiruhu', and his saying: 'Ya tariki fi hubbihi abada hadithi laysa bi-l-mansukh', for example from the well-known examples, except in the notebooks. (70) That is, in his saying 'even if it is multiple' etc., this is far because the actual object of placing in literal and figurative does not have to be multiple, as is not hidden. (71) Because the used meaning in them is not united but multiple, as is apparent. Also, on this, it is not correct to make univocal and equivocal from the divisions of united meaning, necessarily because the used meaning in them is also not united but multiple. It seems the annotator did not address it due to its obviousness.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a super-commentary (hashiya) that critically refines the earlier commentary's classification of singular terms (mufrad) and the concept of 'unity of meaning' (ittihad al-ma'na). The author begins by defending the original division: the division of singular into types (proper noun, univocal, etc.) is not absolute but conditioned on the term being qualified by either unity or multiplicity. Verbs and particles, if not so qualified, are excluded from the division. This answers an objection about the enumeration being incomplete.

The author then clarifies that universality and particularity are attributes of meanings, not of words themselves. The term 'unity of meaning' does not mean the word agrees in number (like having one name for multiple referents), but rather the meaning is one in itself.

A key premise is then presented about types of wad' (linguistic positing). There are three types: (1) particular wad' and particular object (e.g., proper names), (2) general wad' and general object (e.g., 'insan' for the universal human), and (3) general wad' but particular object (e.g., pronouns and demonstratives, where the meaning is general but each instance is particular). The author notes that scholars disagreed on the classification of pronouns, demonstratives, etc., and the original author (musannif) considered them as type (1) or (2) and thus excluded them with the qualification 'bi-wad''.

A detailed dilemma arises about the term 'meaning'. If 'meaning' means the actual object of placing, then figurative (majaz) and literal (haqiqa) cannot both be in the 'multiple meaning' division, since only the literal is actually placed. If 'meaning' means what is used in speech, then demonstratives etc. would fall under 'multiple meaning', making the qualification redundant. The author offers a resolution using the rhetorical device 'istikhdam', where 'meaning' is understood differently in two parts of the same statement: in 'itihada ma'nahu' it refers to the placed meaning (so demonstratives are excluded), and in 'wa in kathura' it refers to the used meaning (so literal and figurative are included). This preserves the original classification.

Finally, marginal notes critique this resolution, suggesting it is far-fetched, and note that if usage is taken as criterion, then univocal and equivocal would also be multiple in meaning, contradicting their classification as united-in-meaning. The annotator (muhashshi) is said to have omitted discussion of this due to its obviousness.

Key Terms
الاستخدام
الحقيقة والمجاز
الموضوع له
المستعمل فيه
المتواطئ
المشكك

Key Takeaway
The term 'meaning' in the division can be polysemous; istikhdam allows a single statement to refer to both placed and used meanings.

Objection and Resolution on Univocal/Equivocal and Hypothetical Individuals
Author addresses objection about univocal/equivocal terms and mental individuals, then resolves it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyse the objection against the definitions of mutawati and mushakkik regarding hypothetical individuals and explain the author's resolution using nafs al-amr.

Core Idea
Univocality and equivocality are judged by the real nature, not by actual existence.

This passage presents a subtle objection: if we define mutawati (univocal) and mushakkik (equivocal) based on real equality or difference among individuals, what about universals that have only hypothetical individuals? And if based on mental supposition, our certain classifications become arbitrary. The author provides a precise answer that preserves the objectivity of logical distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two horns of the objection
The objector argues that if the criterion is real existence (nafs al-amr), hypothetical universals like 'partner of God' have no individuals in reality and thus cannot be classified. If the criterion is mental supposition, then established rulings about specific concepts (e.g., 'human' is univocal, 'existence' is equivocal) become subjective.
The objection aims to find a flaw in the standard definitions.
2
Identify the consequences of each horn
Under the first horn, concepts like 'partner of God' (which has no real individuals) cannot be univocal or equivocal, undermining their logical status. Under the second horn, any universal could be arbitrarily classified, so logicians would lose certainty in classifications like 'human is univocal'.
The objector uses these consequences to argue that both options are problematic.
3
Extract the author's answer
The author chooses the first horn and clarifies that 'nafs al-amr' does not require actual individuals. It means that if there were individuals, the truth of the universal would be equal or different in reality, regardless of whether those individuals exist. Thus hypothetical universals can still be classified objectively.
The key move is reinterpreting 'nafs al-amr' as a counterfactual conditional.
4
Apply the refined definition to examples
The author broadens the notion of individuals to include external (like 'human'), mental (like 'partner of God'), or mixed (like 'Necessary Existent' and 'sun'). The criterion remains the real nature, not actual existence.
This shows the definition is robust across all types of individuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أورد هنا بان المساواة المعتبرة في صدق المتواطى والاختلاف المعتبر فى صدق المشكلكان كانا ملحوظين بحسب الواقع ونفس الامر بان يكون المتواطى ما يتساوى فى افراده فى نفس الامر و المشكك ما يختلف في افراده على الوجوه المذكورة في الحاشية الاتية فى نفس الامر يلزم ان لا يكون الكليات الفرضية متواطئة ولا مشككة اذ ليس لها افراد فى نفس الامر حتى يمكن المساواة والاختلاف فى نفس الأمروان كانا ملحوظين بحسب الفرض العقلى بان يكون المتواطى ما يتساوى فى أفراده بحسب الفرض العقلى والمشكك ما يختلف في افراده بحسب الفرض العقلى سواء طابق الواقع أم لا يلزم اختلال قاعدتين مقررتين الأولى انهم جزموا بان الانسان بالنسبة الى افراده متواط لتساوى صدقه فيها والوجود بالنسبة الى افراده مشكك لاختلاف صدقه فيها ، فلو كان التواطى والتشكيك بحسب الفرض العقلى ، لما صح العقلي ، لما صح الجزم منهم بان بعض الكليات كالانسان مثلاً متواط وبعضها كالوجود مثلاً مشكك بل ينبغى لهم ان يقولوا: كلما فرضه العقل مساوياً بالنسبة الى افراده فهو متواط وكل ما فرضه العقل مختلفاً بالنسبة الى افراده فهو مشكك سواء كان ذلك هو الانسان او الوجود او غيرهما .الثانية أنهم حكموا بان المشكك لا يكون ذاتياً لما تحته من الافراد مستدلين بان الذاتى لا يختلف باختلاف الاشخاص كما بين في موضعه فلو كان التشكيك هو الاختلاف الحاصل بالنسبة الى الافراد بحسب الفرض العقلى لما صبح ذلك الحكم منهم كما هو ظاهر اذلاريب في ان هذا المعنى ربما وجد في العقليات ضرورة ان للعقل ان يفرض الذاتي ايضاً مختلفاً و ان كان هذا الفرض منه غير مطابق للواقع هذا. ويمكن ان يجاب بانا نختار الشق الأول ونقول ان كون الافراد فرضية لاينافي في كون التساوى او الاختلاف نفس امرى فان معنى التساوى او الاختلاف بحسب نفس الامر انه لو كانت للشيء افراد محققة كان صدق هذا الشيء لهذه الافراد متساوياً أو مختلفاً بحسب نفس الامر والواقع ولاريب ان هذا المعنى لا يتوقف على تحقق الافراد في نفس الامر والواقع كما هو ظاهر لمن تأمل. وقد ظهر مما تلونا عليك ان الافراد فى المتواطى والمشكك اعم من ان تكون خارجية كالانسان او ذهنية كشريك البارى تعالى او بعضها خارجياً وبعضها ذهنياً كواجب الوجود والشمس فاحفظ هذه الجملة.
It is objected here: If the equality considered in the truth of the univocal and the difference considered in the truth of the equivocal were observed according to reality and the real itself, such that the univocal is what is equal in its individuals in the real itself, and the equivocal is what differs in its individuals in the real itself according to the ways mentioned in the coming footnote, then hypothetical universals would be neither univocal nor equivocal, since they have no individuals in the real itself so that equality and difference could occur in the real itself. And if they were observed according to mental supposition, such that the univocal is what is equal in its individuals according to mental supposition, and the equivocal is what differs in its individuals according to mental supposition, whether it corresponds to reality or not, then two established principles would be undermined. First: They were certain that 'human' with respect to its individuals is univocal because its truth is equal among them, and 'existence' with respect to its individuals is equivocal because its truth differs among them. So if univocality and equivocality were according to mental supposition, then their certainty would not be valid; rather they should say: Whatever the mind supposes equal with respect to its individuals is univocal, and whatever the mind supposes different with respect to its individuals is equivocal, whether that be human, existence, or something else. Second: They ruled that the equivocal is not essential to the individuals under it, arguing that the essential does not differ with the difference of individuals as established in its place. So if equivocality were the difference occurring with respect to individuals according to mental supposition, that ruling from them would not be valid, as is apparent, for there is no doubt that this meaning might be found in mental things, necessarily, for the mind can suppose the essential also to be different, even if this supposition from it is not in accordance with reality. This. And it can be answered: We choose the first branch and say that the fact that individuals are hypothetical does not prevent equality or difference from being in the real itself. For the meaning of equality or difference according to the real itself is that if the thing had actual individuals, the truth of this thing for these individuals would be equal or different according to the real itself and reality. And there is no doubt that this meaning does not depend on the actual existence of individuals in the real itself and reality, as is apparent to one who reflects. And it has become clear from what we have recited to you that the individuals in the univocal and equivocal are more general: they may be external like 'human', or mental like 'the partner of the Exalted God', or some external and some mental like 'the Necessary Existent' and 'the sun'. So memorize this sentence.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (naqd) and its answer concerning the definitions of the univocal (mutawati) and the equivocal (mushakkik), specifically regarding universals that have only hypothetical individuals. The objector argues that if the equality or difference is judged by real existence, then hypothetical universals (like 'partner of God') cannot be classified. If judged by mental supposition, then established rulings about specific concepts (like human being univocal, existence equivocal) become merely subjective and lose certainty. The author's answer is that the criterion is the real itself, but not requiring actual individuals; it means that if there were actual individuals, the truth would be equal or different in reality, regardless of whether those individuals exist. He then broadens the notion of individuals to include external, mental, or mixed, as in the examples of human, partner of God, and the Necessary Existent.

Priority and Superiority in Equivocal Terms
Explains priority and superiority in equivocal terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between priority (al-awwaliyya) and superiority (al-awlawiyya) in equivocal terms and identify which applies in a given example from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Priority is about causal order; superiority is about perfection degree.

Equivocal terms like 'existence' apply to different individuals in different ways. The concepts of priority and superiority refine our understanding of how such terms can be 'graded'. In this lesson, you'll learn to distinguish between these two types of difference and see how they illuminate important theological distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Extract the two definitions
From the passage, identify the definitions of priority (awwaliyya) and superiority (awlawiyya). Priority means the concept is true of one individual before another in a causal or existential order. Superiority means the concept applies more perfectly, strongly, or appropriately to one than another.
2
Analyze the example of existence
The passage uses existence as an example. Existence in the Necessary Being (God) is prior to existence in contingent beings because it is the source. Also, existence in God is more perfect, established, and stronger—this shows superiority. Note how the same term illustrates both aspects.
3
Distinguish the two differences
The commentator stresses that priority and superiority are distinct. Sometimes the later in order can be stronger (e.g., existence in celestial motion vs. generated bodies). This shows that priority does not entail superiority.
4
Apply the distinction to other terms
Think of another equivocal term like 'knowledge' or 'power'. Determine whether the difference between God's attribute and a human's is one of priority, superiority, or both. Use the criteria: causal order vs. perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله اى يكون صدق هذا المفهوم على بعض .... هذا معنى الأولية في كلام المصنف و :قوله او يكون صدقه» الخ، معنى الاولوية فى كلامه وقد مثلوالهما بالوجود فان حصوله في الواجب و صدقه عليه مقدم على حصوله فى الممكن وصدقه عليه و ايضاً هو في الواجب اتم واثبت واقوى منه في الممكن فيكون صدقه عليه أولى و انسب من صدقه عليه فبالاعتبار الاول يكون مثالاً للاولية وبالاعتبار الثانى يكون مثالا للاولوية وفرق بين هذين الاختلافين بانه قديكون المتأخر أقوى واثبت من المتقدم كالوجود بالقياس الى الحركة الفلكية والاجسام الكاينة، فصدقه على الاولى وان كان مقدماً عن على الأخرى لكنه اضعف و اهون منه فى الاخرى.
His statement: 'i.e. that this concept's truth is on some...' That is the meaning of priority (awwaliyya) in the author's words. And his statement: 'or that its truth...' etc. That is the meaning of superiority (awlawiyya) in his words. They gave as an example of both existence: for its occurrence in the Necessary Being and its truth upon it is prior to its occurrence in the contingent and its truth upon it. Also, it is more perfect, more established, and stronger in the Necessary than in the contingent. So its truth upon it is more appropriate and fitting than its truth upon it. So by the first consideration it is an example of priority, and by the second consideration it is an example of superiority. The difference between these two differences is that sometimes the later is stronger and more established than the prior, like existence in relation to the celestial motion and the generated bodies; its truth upon the former, although prior to the other, is weaker and less significant than in the latter.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage defines two technical senses of gradation in equivocal (mushakkik) terms: awwaliyya (priority) and awlawiyya (superiority/appropriateness). The author of the matn (likely al-Sullam or similar) mentioned that equivocal terms may differ in these two ways. The commentator expands by giving the example of existence: existence is prior in God (al-wajib) since it is the source, while in contingent it is derived; that is priority in causal dependence. Also, existence is more perfect and stronger in God than in contingent, making it superior or more appropriate (awla). The commentator then notes that these two are distinct: sometimes the later may be stronger, as in existence in generated bodies compared to celestial motion, where existence in the former is actually stronger and more enduring despite being later in causal order.

Exemplification vs. Exhaustive Enumeration in Logic
Commentator clarifies that the author's mention of only two types of difference is illustrative, not exhaustive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a logical enumeration is illustrative rather than exhaustive, and explain how qualifiers like 'مثلاً' prevent false objections.

Core Idea
A single example does not close the door—qualifiers like 'مثلاً' prevent misinterpretation.

In this passage, the commentator al-Yazdi protects the original author from a potential misunderstanding. Some readers might think the author claimed that only two types of difference exist in equivocal terms (mushakkik). But the author included the word 'مثلاً' (for example), which flags his list as illustrative. The commentator explains this carefully to avoid a false objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the potential misinterpretation
Read the passage and note that some might think the author limited differences to priority (awwaliyya) and superiority (awlawiyya). This is the misinterpretation the commentator wants to correct.
The commentator says: 'it is not hidden that his statement... is not the quoted speech...'
2
Locate the key qualifier
Find the word 'مثلاً' (for example) in the Arabic. This word signals that the list is not exhaustive but exemplary.
The commentator explicitly states that 'his statement 'for example' is a qualification for it.'
3
Recognize the missing types
The commentator mentions that difference also occurs by increase/decrease (ziyada wa nuqsan) and intensity/weakness (shidda wa da'f). These are valid types not excluded by the author.
These are subsumed under awlawiyya in the original classification.
4
Understand the grammatical structure
The commentator analyzes the grammar to show that the phrase 'that it differs by priority or superiority' is not a direct quote but the subject of the sentence, and 'his purpose' is the predicate. This clarifies that the author's intent is not a claim of exclusivity.
Grammatical analysis is a common tool in hashiya to refine understanding.
5
Summarize the commentator's defense
The commentator concludes that mentioning only two types is by way of illustration (tamthil), not restriction (hasr), so no objection applies.
This shows how a careful commentator preempts and refutes potential criticisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يخفى ان قوله ان تفاوتت باولية او اولوية» ليس مقولاً للقول في قوله من قوله كما يتوهم والالبق المبتداء و هو قوله: «غرضه» بلاخير بل هو خبر المبتداء و قوله : «مثلاً» قيد له و مقول القول المذكور غير مذكور فى اللفظ فالمعنى و غرضه من قوله ان تفاوتت باولية أو أولوية حيث ذكره مطلقا ان تفاوتت بأولية أو أولوية مثلاً اى مراده من هذا المطلق هو هذا المقيد. والحاصل ان ذكره لهما وحدهما ليس من جهة أن وجوه الاختلاف عنده منحصرة فيهما حتى يرد ان الاختلاف كما يكون بهما يكون بالزيادة والنقصان والشدة والضعف ايضاً بل ان ذكره لهما انما هو على سبيل التمثيل فلايرد عليه تشنیع.
It is not hidden that his statement 'that it differs by priority or superiority' is not the quoted speech in his statement from his statement as is supposed, and the more appropriate is that it is the subject, and his statement: 'his purpose' [is the predicate]? Rather it is the predicate? And his statement 'for example' is a qualification for it, and the quoted speech of the mentioned statement is not mentioned in the wording. So the meaning: His purpose from his statement 'that it differs by priority or superiority' where he mentioned it absolutely is that it differs by priority or superiority, for example. That is, his intent from this absolute is this qualified. The upshot: his mentioning them alone is not because the ways of difference in his view are restricted to them, such that it could be objected that difference also occurs by increase and decrease and intensity and weakness. Rather, his mentioning them is only by way of illustration, so no objection applies.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a hashiya (super-commentary) refining the understanding of the original matn. The commentator warns against a misunderstanding: some might think the author limited the types of difference in equivocal terms to just two (priority and superiority). The commentator clarifies that the author's phrase 'for example' (mithalan) indicates that these are examples, not an exhaustive list. The real categories include increase/decrease (ziyada wa nuqsan) and intensity/weakness (shidda wa da'f), but these are subsumed under superiority (awlawiyya). The grammatical analysis shows that 'that it differs by priority or superiority' is not a direct quote but the subject of the sentence, with 'his purpose' as predicate, and 'for example' qualifies it. The point is to prevent a false criticism that the author omitted other types.

A) Because the author's statement includes the word 'مثلاً', showing it is illustrative.
