Distinguishing al-Uliyya and al-Ulawiyya in Tashkik
Author defines two types of priority in graded predication using existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-uliyya (firstness) and al-ulawiyya (precedence) in gradual predication and identify which is at play in the existence example.

Core Idea
Firstness is priority with strength; precedence is priority in appropriateness without strength.

This passage from the hashiya clarifies two forms of priority in tashkik—uliyya and ulawiyya—using the classic example of existence predicated on the Necessary and contingent. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping how graded terms work in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of priority
Look for the terms 'uliyya' and 'ulawiyya'. What does each signify according to the author?
The author defines uliyya as the concept's truth being prior to some individuals, and ulawiyya as its truth being more appropriate.
2
Analyze the existence example
Existence in the Necessary Being is prior to existence in contingent beings. It is also more perfect and stronger. Consider these two aspects: sequence and strength.
3
Determine which consideration fits which type
The first consideration (sequence + strength) is an example of uliyya; the second consideration (appropriateness without requiring strength) is an example of ulawiyya.
The passage says: 'by the first consideration it is an example of firstness, and by the second it is an example of precedence.'
4
Contrast with the counterexample
The later can be stronger, as in generated bodies vs celestial motion. This shows that ulawiyya does not require strength.
‘Sometimes the later is stronger and more established than the prior.’

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله اى يكون صدق هذا المفهوم على بعض .... هذا معنى الأولية في كلام المصنف و قوله او يكون صدقه الخ معنى الاولوية فى كلامه وقد مثلوالهما بالوجود فان حصوله في الواجب و صدقه عليه مقدم على حصوله فى الممكن وصدقه عليه و ايضاً هو في الواجب اتم واثبت واقوى منه في الممكن فيكون صدقه عليه أولى و انسب من صدقه عليه فبالاعتبار الاول يكون مثالاً للاولية وبالاعتبار الثانى يكون مثالا للاولوية وفرق بين هذين الاختلافين بانه قديكون المتأخر أقوى واثبت من المتقدم كالوجود بالقياس الى الحركة الفلكية والاجسام الكاينة، فصدقه على الاولى وان كان مقدماً عن على الأخرى لكنه اضعف و اهون منه فى الاخرى. وقال الشيخ عبد الرحيم ره فى هذا المورد الغرض من تقييد التقدم بالعلية هو ان المعتبر في هذا التشكيك هو التقدم الذاتى ولا عبرة بالتقدم الزماني كما فى افراد الانسان الرجوعه إلى اجزاء الزمان لا الى حصول نفس معناه في افراده والمراد من التقدم بالعلية هيهنا هو ان يستحيل صدق هذا المفهوم على بعض افراده قبل صدقه اولاً على بعض آخر كالوجود فان حصوله فى الواجب قبل حصوله في الممكن بحيث يمتنع حصوله فيه قبل حصوله في الواجب لانه مبدء لما عداه ولا يشترط ان يكون هذا المفهوم في المقدم اقوى واثبت منه في المؤخر فان الوجود فى الاجسام الكاينة الحادثة فى عالمنا هذا اقوى منه في الحركة الفلكية المتقدمة عليها تقدماً بالذات و انما يشترط ذلك في الأولية.
His saying: 'i.e., the truth of this concept applies to some...' This is the meaning of firstness (al-uliyya) in the author's statement. And his saying: 'or its truth...' is the meaning of precedence (al-ulawiyya) in his statement. They gave examples of both with existence, for its occurrence in the Necessary Being and its truth of it are prior to its occurrence in the contingent and its truth of it, and also it is in the Necessary more perfect, more established, and stronger than it is in the contingent, so its truth of it is more prior and more appropriate than its truth of it. By the first consideration it is an example of firstness, and by the second consideration it is an example of precedence. The difference between these two differences is that sometimes the later is stronger and more established than the prior, like existence in relation to celestial motion and generated bodies, for its truth of the first, even though it is prior to the other, is weaker and more insignificant than in the other. And Shaykh Abd al-Rahim said in this regard: The purpose of restricting priority to causality is that what is considered in this gradual predication is essential priority, not temporal priority, as in individuals of man their reference is to parts of time, not to the occurrence of the meaning itself in its individuals. The meaning of priority by causality here is that it is impossible for this concept to be true of some of its individuals before it is true first of some other, like existence, for its occurrence in the Necessary Being before its occurrence in the contingent such that its occurrence in it is impossible before its occurrence in the Necessary Being because it is the source of what is other than it. It is not required that this concept in the prior be stronger and more established than in the posterior, for existence in the generated bodies occurring in this world of ours is stronger than it is in celestial motion which is essentially prior to them. That is only required in firstness.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage defines two types of priority in the context of gradual predication (tashkik). The commentator explains the author's words: 'uliyya' (firstness) refers to temporal or causal priority in the sense of being first in existence, while 'ulawiyya' (precedence) refers to superiority in strength or perfection. Using the example of existence, it is shown that existence is predicated of the Necessary Being (wajib) before contingent beings (mumkin) in terms of both temporal priority (firstness) and essential priority (causal precedence). However, the commentator notes a distinction: what is later in time may be stronger in existence, as in generated bodies compared to celestial motion. Shaykh Abd al-Rahim adds that in gradual predication, the relevant priority is essential (dhati) not temporal. The condition for causal priority is that the concept's truth on one individual is impossible prior to its truth on another, as existence in the Necessary is the source of all else. Importantly, strength is not a condition for causal priority; it is only required for firstness (uliyya).

Key Terms
الأولية
الأولوية
التشكيك
التقدم بالعلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the existence example, which consideration makes it an example of al-ulawiyya?
A) The truth of existence in the Necessary is prior to the contingent.
B) Existence in the Necessary is more perfect and stronger than in the contingent.
C) Existence in the Necessary is more appropriate (ansab) to it.
D) Existence in the contingent is impossible without the Necessary.

Key Takeaway
Gradual predication involves two distinct forms of priority: uliyya (firstness with strength) and ulawiyya (precedence in appropriateness).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the concept of 'life' predicated of God and of humans. Using the definitions from this passage, determine whether the priority is uliyya or ulawiyya, and explain your reasoning.

The Condition for Causal Priority in Tashkik
Shaykh Abd al-Rahim clarifies the condition for causal priority in gradual predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the condition for causal priority (al-taqaddum bi al-illiyya) in gradual predication and why strength is not required.

Core Idea
Causal priority means impossibility of the concept's truth on the posterior without the prior; strength is only required for firstness.

Shaykh Abd al-Rahim adds a crucial clarification: the priority in tashkik is essential, not temporal, and its condition is that the concept's truth on one individual depends on its truth on another. He distinguishes this from the extra condition of strength required for uliyya.

Guided Path
1
Locate the restriction 'taqayyud al-taqaddum bi al-'illiyya'
Understand why causal priority is specified. The commentator restricts the relevant priority to causal (essential) priority, excluding temporal priority.
The phrase 'الغرض من تقييد التقدم بالعلية' indicates the purpose of this restriction.
2
Understand the condition for causal priority
The condition is that it is impossible for the concept to be true of some individuals before it is true of another. This is a dependence relation.
‘It is impossible for this concept to be true of some of its individuals before it is true first of some other.’
3
Apply to the existence example
Existence in contingent beings is impossible without existence in the Necessary Being, because the Necessary is the source. This meets the condition.
4
Contrast with temporal priority
Temporal priority is not sufficient; e.g., individuals of 'man' share the same concept without causal dependence.
‘As in individuals of man their reference is to parts of time, not to the occurrence of the meaning itself.’
5
Explain why strength is not required
The counterexample of generated bodies being stronger in existence than celestial motion shows that strength can be in the later, so it is not part of causal priority.
‘It is not required that this concept in the prior be stronger... That is only required in firstness.’

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His saying: 'i.e., the truth of this concept applies to some...' This is the meaning of firstness (al-uliyya) in the author's statement. And his saying: 'or its truth...' is the meaning of precedence (al-ulawiyya) in his statement. Defining al-Mushtarak: Core Meaning and First Exclusions
Author defines mushtarak and excludes first categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-mushtarak using the condition of multiple independent initial placements and identify which categories of words are excluded from it (e.g., proper names, univocal terms).

Core Idea
Mushtarak is defined by multiple independent initial placements, not by having multiple meanings alone.

In this lesson, we examine the technical definition of al-mushtarak (homonym) as used in Islamic logic. The author carefully delineates what qualifies as mushtarak and what does not, beginning with the distinction between mushtarak and mujmal. We will break down the definition and its first set of exclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and its conditions
Find the term being defined (al-mushtarak) and note that it is defined as a word placed for multiple meanings by multiple independent initial placements.
The definition has three conditions: multiple meanings, multiple placements, and these placements are initial (ibtida'i).
2
Distinguish mushtarak from mujmal
Understand that mushtarak refers to the fact of multiple placements, while mujmal refers to the ambiguity of which meaning is intended in a given utterance. The same word can be mushtarak in itself and mujmal in usage.
This nuance is crucial for avoiding confusion between the two terms.
3
List the first set of exclusions derived from the condition 'placed for multiple meanings'
The condition excludes words that are not placed for multiple meanings: proper names (ilm), univocal (mutawati), equivocal (mushakkik), indefinite (nakira), truth (haqiqa), and metaphor (majaz). Analyze why each is excluded.
For example, univocal terms have one meaning; proper names refer to one individual.
4
Apply the definition to a new example
Test a given word against the definition to see if it qualifies as mushtarak. For instance, the word 'shams' (sun) is not mushtarak because it has one meaning.
Use the definition as a checklist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ای (۸۱) اى متشركاً فيه بالنسبة الى الجميع واما بالنسبة الى كل واحد منها فيسمى مجملاً فان كون اللفظ موضوعاً لهذا وحده ولذلك وحده معلوم فكان مشتركاً فيه من هذه الحيثية لاشتراكه بين تلك المعانى و كون المراد عند اطلاقه هذا وذلك غير معلوم فكان مجملاً من هذه الجملة لعدم وضوح دلالته (عبد الرحيم) و قال الشيخ محمد على ره في تحقيق هذا البحث: قوله والاول يسمى مشتركاً: فالمشترك ما كان موضوعاً لمعان متعددة باوضاع متعددة وضعاً ابتدائياً، فخرج بالاول العلم والمتواطى والمشكك والنكرة والحقيقة و المجاز وبقولنا باوضاع متعددة» ما كان موضوعاً لمعان متعددة بوضع واحد اجمالي كاسماء الاشارة والموصولات وغيرهما من المهمات على قول المتأخرين فانه لوحظ عند الوضع المعنى الكلى ووضع الكل واحد من الجزئيات بوضع واحد فالمهمات خارجة عن حد المشترك على كلا المذهبين وبقولنا وضعاً ابتدائياً» المنقول، فانه وان كان موضوعاً لمعان متعددة باوضاع متعددة، لكن لا بالوضع الابتدائى الظهور أن وضعه بالنسبة الى المعنى المنقول اليه انما هو بالوضع الثانوى واما المرتجل فذهب بعضهم الى انه داخل فيه فانه لما لم يعتبر فى وضعه للمعنى الثانى مناسبة للمعنى الاول كان وضعه له وضعاً أولياً لظهور ان المراد من الاولية ليس ان يكون جميع الاوضاع واقعة في المرتبة الواحدة بان لا يتقدم بعضها على بعض بل المراد ان لا يلاحظ في وضعه للمعنى الاخير حال المعنى الأول ومناسبته له و يكون وضعه له وضعاً مستقلاً. و ذهب الاخرون الى انه قسيم له نظراً الى ان معنى الوضع الابتدائى هو ما لم يلاحظ فيه حال المعنى الأول مطلقا وان كان من جهة عدم المناسبة والمرتجل يلاحظ فيه حاله من جهة عدم المناسبة فيحصل فيه نوع تبعية وثانوية ويخرج عن الاستقلال. (۸۲) قوله : كالعين للباصرة : هى اول ما ذكره محمد بن يعقوب من معانى العين حيث قال: العين الباصرة، مؤثثة جمعه (جمعها) اعيان واعين وعيون و يكسر وجمع الجمع اعينات. (عبد الرحيم)
Meaning: shared with respect to all, but with respect to each one it is called ambiguous (mujmal). For the fact that the word is placed for this alone and for that alone is known, so it is shared (mushtarak) from this perspective due to its sharing among those meanings. And the intended meaning when it is uttered being this or that is not known, so it is ambiguous (mujmal) from this perspective due to lack of clarity of its indication. (Abd al-Rahim) And Shaykh Muhammad Ali said in verifying this investigation: his saying: 'The first is called mushtarak': Thus al-mushtarak is that which is placed for multiple meanings by multiple placements, initial placement (wad' ibtida'i). By the first [i.e. 'placed for multiple meanings'], excluded are proper names (al-ilm), univocal (al-mutawati), equivocal (al-mushakkik), indefinite (al-nakira), truth (al-haqiqa), and metaphor (al-majaz). By our saying 'by multiple placements' is excluded that which is placed for multiple meanings by a single summary placement, like demonstrative pronouns, relative pronouns, and other ambiguous terms (al-mubhamat) according to the later scholars, because at the time of placement the universal meaning was considered, and the whole was placed for each of the particulars with one placement. So ambiguous terms are outside the limit of mushtarak according to both schools. By our saying 'initial placement' is excluded the transferred term (al-manqul), for even if it is placed for multiple meanings by multiple placements, it is not by initial placement, because it is evident that its placement relative to the transferred meaning is only secondary placement. As for the arbitrary term (al-murtajal), some held that it is included in it, because since in its placement for the second meaning it did not consider appropriateness to the first meaning, its placement for it is initial, due to the appearance that the intended meaning of 'initial' is not that all placements occur in one rank with none preceding others, but rather that in its placement for the latter meaning the state of the first meaning and its appropriateness are not considered, and its placement for it is independent. Others held that it is a counterpart to it, in view of the fact that the meaning of 'initial placement' is that in which the state of the first meaning is absolutely not considered, even if it is from the aspect of lack of appropriateness; and in the arbitrary term its state from the aspect of lack of appropriateness is considered, so a kind of dependency and secondariness occurs in it and it leaves independence. (82) His saying: 'Like al-ayn for the seeing eye': It is the first of what Muhammad ibn Ya'qub mentioned of the meanings of al-ayn, where he said: 'Al-ayn: the seeing eye, feminine, its plural is a'yan, a'yun, and 'uyun, and it is broken, and the plural of the plural is a'yinat.' (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'mushtarak' (homonym) and clarifies its boundaries. The author begins by distinguishing between mushtarak and mujmal: a word is mushtarak in that it is known to be placed for multiple meanings, but mujmal in that the specific intended meaning upon utterance is unknown. Then, citing Shaykh Muhammad Ali, the definition is formalized: mushtarak is a word placed for multiple meanings by multiple independent initial placements. Using this definition, several categories are excluded: (1) proper names, univocal terms, equivocal terms, indefinite nouns, truth, and metaphor—these are not placed for multiple meanings; (2) ambiguous terms (mubhamat) like demonstratives, which are placed for multiple particulars by a single summary placement; (3) transferred terms (manqul), which have multiple placements but not initially, as the transfer is secondary. The discussion then covers the arbitrary term (murtajal): some scholars include it in mushtarak because its placement for the second meaning does not consider the first, making it independent; others exclude it because it still considers the first meaning's lack of appropriateness, creating dependency. Finally, an example is provided: the word 'ayn for the seeing eye, citing lexical authorities.

المجمل
العلم
المتواطى
المشكك
النكرة
الحقيقة
المجاز

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following words is most likely an example of mushtarak?
A) 'shams' (sun) - a univocal term
B) 'qur'' (menstruation/purity) - a word with two independent meanings
C) 'hadha' (this) - a demonstrative pronoun
D) 'insan' (human) - a univocal term

Key Takeaway
Mushtarak is defined by multiple independent initial placements, not mere ambiguity; mujmal is the epistemic correlate of mushtarak in usage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the word 'salat' which can mean prayer (the ritual), supplication, or mercy. According to the definition of mushtarak, is it mushtarak or not? Explain your reasoning using the conditions from the passage.
?

Refining al-Mushtarak: Mubhamat, Manqul, and the Dispute on Murtajal
Author excludes mubhamat, manqul, and discusses murtajal with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether ambiguous terms (mubhamat), transferred terms (manqul), and arbitrary terms (murtajal) qualify as mushtarak according to the definition, and articulate the scholarly dispute over murtajal.

Core Idea
Mubhamat and manqul are excluded from mushtarak; murtajal is disputed based on whether its placement is considered independent.

In this continuation, the author applies the definition of mushtarak to several tricky categories: ambiguous terms like demonstratives, transferred terms whose meaning has shifted, and arbitrary terms coined without relation to prior meanings. We will analyze why mubhamat and manqul are excluded and explore the disagreement about murtajal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition 'by multiple placements' (باوضاع متعددة)
This condition excludes words that are placed for multiple meanings by a single summary placement. Mubhamat (ambiguous terms like 'hadha') are placed for many particulars by one act of placing the universal, so they fail this condition.
The passage notes that this holds according to both early and later scholars.
2
Understand the condition 'initial placement' (وضعاً ابتدائياً)
This condition excludes manqul (transferred terms), which are originally placed for one meaning and then transferred to another by a secondary placement. The transfer is not an initial independent act.
Manqul like 'salat' transferred from supplication to ritual prayer is a classic example.
3
Analyze the dispute over murtajal (arbitrary term)
Some scholars include murtajal in mushtarak because its placement for a new meaning does not consider the first meaning, making it independent. Others exclude it because it still implicitly references the first meaning's lack of appropriateness, so it is not fully independent.
The passage presents both views without deciding, showing the nuance in applying the definition.
4
Illustrate with the example of 'ayn
The passage gives 'ayn (eye) as an example of mushtarak, citing lexical authorities. Note that 'ayn has multiple meanings (e.g., eye, spring, essence) each with independent placements.
This grounds the abstract definition in a concrete lexical example.

Meaning: shared with respect to all, but with respect to each one it is called ambiguous (mujmal). For the fact that the word is placed for this alone and for that alone is known, so it is shared (mushtarak) from this perspective due to its sharing among those meanings. And the intended meaning when it is uttered being this or that is not known, so it is ambiguous (mujmal) from this perspective due to lack of clarity of its indication. (Abd al-Rahim) And Shaykh Muhammad Ali said in verifying this investigation: his saying: 'The first is called mushtarak': Thus al-mushtarak is that which is placed for multiple meanings by multiple placements, initial placement (wad' ibtida'i). By the first [i.e. 'placed for multiple meanings'], excluded are proper names (al-ilm), univocal (al-mutawati), equivocal (al-mushakkik), indefinite (al-nakira), truth (al-haqiqa), and metaphor (al-majaz). By our saying 'by multiple placements' is excluded that which is placed for multiple meanings by a single summary placement, like demonstrative pronouns, relative pronouns, and other ambiguous terms (al-mubhamat) according to the later scholars, because at the time of placement the universal meaning was considered, and the whole was placed for each of the particulars with one placement. So ambiguous terms are outside the limit of mushtarak according to both schools. By our saying 'initial placement' is excluded the transferred term (al-manqul), for even if it is placed for multiple meanings by multiple placements, it is not by initial placement, because it is evident that its placement relative to the transferred meaning is only secondary placement. As for the arbitrary term (al-murtajal), some held that it is included in it, because since in its placement for the second meaning it did not consider appropriateness to the first meaning, its placement for it is initial, due to the appearance that the intended meaning of 'initial' is not that all placements occur in one rank with none preceding others, but rather that in its placement for the latter meaning the state of the first meaning and its appropriateness are not considered, and its placement for it is independent. Others held that it is a counterpart to it, in view of the fact that the meaning of 'initial placement' is that in which the state of the first meaning is absolutely not considered, even if it is from the aspect of lack of appropriateness; and in the arbitrary term its state from the aspect of lack of appropriateness is considered, so a kind of dependency and secondariness occurs in it and it leaves independence. (82) His saying: 'Like al-ayn for the seeing eye': It is the first of what Muhammad ibn Ya'qub mentioned of the meanings of al-ayn, where he said: 'Al-ayn: the seeing eye, feminine, its plural is a'yan, a'yun, and 'uyun, and it is broken, and the plural of the plural is a'yinat.' (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'mushtarak' (homonym) and clarifies its boundaries. The author begins by distinguishing between mushtarak and mujmal: a word is mushtarak in that it is known to be placed for multiple meanings, but mujmal in that the specific intended meaning upon utterance is unknown. Then, citing Shaykh Muhammad Ali, the definition is formalized: mushtarak is a word placed for multiple meanings by multiple independent initial placements. Using this definition, several categories are excluded: (1) proper names, univocal terms, equivocal terms, indefinite nouns, truth, and metaphor—these are not placed for multiple meanings; (2) ambiguous terms (mubhamat) like demonstratives, which are placed for multiple particulars by a single summary placement; (3) transferred terms (manqul), which have multiple placements but not initially, as the transfer is secondary. The discussion then covers the arbitrary term (murtajal): some scholars include it in mushtarak because its placement for the second meaning does not consider the first, making it independent; others exclude it because it still considers the first meaning's lack of appropriateness, creating dependency. Finally, an example is provided: the word 'ayn for the seeing eye, citing lexical authorities.

The Five Universals

Five Cases When a Word is Not Individually Placed for Each Meaning
Author enumerates five cases when a word is not placed for each meaning individually, with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify the five possible relationships between a word and its multiple meanings as described in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Words are never used without some placement—either initial or secondary—and every usage falls into one of five clear categories.

This lesson unpacks a key classification in Islamic semantics: what happens when a single word carries multiple meanings but isn't individually assigned to each one? The author lists five logical possibilities, invalidates one, and gives concrete examples from Arabic grammar and usul al-fiqh. Understanding these divisions is essential for precise interpretation of terms in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the assumption
The passage is based on the assumption that a word is not placed for each of its meanings individually. This means the word has multiple meanings but no one-to-one initial placement.
This assumption is a starting point for the fivefold division.
2
List the five logical possibilities
The author enumerates: (1) no placement at all; (2) all meanings by a single summary secondary placement; (3) all meanings by multiple secondary placements; (4) all meanings by a single summary initial placement; (5) some meanings by initial, some by secondary placement.
These are mutually exclusive and exhaustive under the assumption.
3
Identify the invalid division
The first division (no placement) is ruled out because every used word must have some placement, either initial or secondary. The author emphasizes that 'placement' includes religious (shar'i) placement.
This shows that every word in actual use has a semantic anchor.
4
Connect each valid division to an example
The examples are from Arabic grammar and usul: (a) mubhamat (ambiguous terms) according to early grammarians for division 2; (b) manqul (transferred term) with complete loss of original meaning for division 3; (c) mubhamat according to later grammarians for division 4; (d) haqiqa/majaz and manqul retaining original meaning for division 5.
These examples show how the abstract classification applies to real terms in the Islamic sciences.
5
Reflect on the notion of 'meaning'
The commentator clarifies that 'meaning' here refers to the actual usage, not the absolute essence. For instance, mubhamat are used for specific particulars, not for the universal essence they might also signify.
This prevents confusion between different levels of signification.

(83) His saying: 'And on the second [assumption]' i.e., on [the assumption] that it is not placed for each of those meanings initially with an individual placement. Then either it is not placed for any of them at all, neither by initial nor secondary placement; or it is placed for all of them by a single summary placement, secondary placement; or it is placed for all of them also by secondary placement, but with multiple placements; or it is placed for all of them by initial placement, but not with multiple placements, rather by a single summary placement; or it is placed for some of them by initial placement and for some by secondary placement. I say: it has preceded that the intended meaning of 'meaning' is the meaning in which the word is used, not the absolute. The universal essence, even if the ambiguous terms in significations are placed for it according to them, yet they are not used for it at all, so they are metaphors with no reality according to them. An example of the second division is the transferred term (al-manqul) which has undergone multiple transfers by people of different crafts to diverse meanings such that its usage in the first meaning it was placed for is completely abandoned; so it is an example of what is placed for all its meanings by multiple secondary placements. An example of the third division is also the ambiguous terms according to the later scholars, and reference has already been made to it in our previous gloss. An example of the fourth division is truth (al-haqiqa) and metaphor (al-majaz) and the transferred term that has not completely abandoned its usage in the original meaning. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the second assumption in a previous discussion: that a word is not individually placed for each meaning. The author enumerates five possibilities: (1) the word is placed for none of the meanings at all; (2) placed for all by a single summary secondary placement; (3) placed for all by multiple secondary placements; (4) placed for all by a single summary initial placement; (5) placed for some by initial and some by secondary placement. The first is invalid because every used word must have some placement. The valid divisions are exemplified: (i) the first valid division is the mubhamat (ambiguous terms) according to early grammarians, because they place them for specific particulars by one secondary summary placement; (ii) the second is the manqul (transferred term) that has been transferred multiple times, losing the original meaning; (iii) the third is again the mubhamat but according to later scholars; (iv) the fourth includes haqiqa, majaz, and manqul that retains original usage. The argument clarifies that 'meaning' here means used meaning, not absolute essence.

The first division (no placement) is impossible; every used word must have a placement, either initial or secondary, and the classification helps map the semantic structure of terms in Islamic sciences.

Objections to Definitions of Kulli and Juz'i: Inclusive vs Substitutive Truth
Two objections against the definitions of kulli and juz'i with responses clarifying the type of truth and existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze objections to definitions by distinguishing between inclusive (shumul) and substitutive (badaliyya) truth and between original (asli) and derivative (taba'i) existence.

Core Idea
Definitions of universal and particular only work if 'truth' is inclusive and by original existence.

The definitions of universal (kulli) and particular (juz'i) face classic objections: a distant phantom appears true of many things, and the concept of a particular exists in many minds. These objections seem fatal until we clarify the precise type of truth and existence required. This lesson teaches you how to dissect such dialectical exchanges in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objections
First objection: The distant phantom (shabah mar'i) is true of many things (e.g., a shape could be a man, a tree, etc.), so it would be universal, but it is a particular object. Second objection: The concept of any particular (e.g., Zayd) exists in many minds and is true of those mental instances, so it would be universal, but it is particular.
Both objections attack the definitions as not exclusive (not mani') for kulli and not inclusive (not jami') for juz'i.
2
Extract the logical structure of each objection
Each objection follows a pattern: If X is true of many, then X is universal. X is true of many (phantom/concept). Therefore X is universal. But X is particular. Contradiction, so definitions fail.
The objections rely on an unstated assumption about the kind of 'truth' involved.
3
Analyze the first answer: inclusive vs substitutive truth
The answer distinguishes two ways something can be 'true of many': inclusive (shumul) – true of all simultaneously, and substitutive (badaliyya) – true of any one by substitution. The phantom is only true of many by substitution (it could be this or that), not inclusive, so it remains particular. The same applies to indefinite nouns like 'a scholar' – they are particular even if they can refer to any individual.
The key distinction: 'truth' in the definitions of kulli and juz'i must be inclusive (shumul).
4
Analyze the second answer: original vs derivative existence
The answer distinguishes original existence (al-wujud al-asli) – external reality, and derivative existence (al-wujud al-taba'i) – mental existence. The concept's truth in multiple minds is derivative, not original, so it does not make the concept universal. A particular by original existence remains particular even if its mental image exists in many minds.
This prevents the objection that mental multiplicity would make everything universal.
5
Apply the distinction to an Islamic science example
Consider the term 'aam (general) in usul al-fiqh: 'aam is defined as a word that encompasses all its individuals (e.g., 'all scholars'). This is inclusive truth. An indefinite noun (nakira) like 'a scholar' is substitutive – it applies to any one scholar, not all together. This distinction is exactly what the passage explains.
Understanding this prevents confusion between general and particular in legal rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم هيهنا بحثان مشهوران الأول: ان تعريف الكلى ليس بمانع والجزئى ليس بجامع اما اولاً فلان الشبح المرئى من البعيد مما يجوز العقل صدقه على امور كثيرة و اشياء عديدة فيلزم ان يكون كلياً مع انه جزئى حقيق. و اما ثانياً، فلان مفهوم كل جزئى مطابق لصورة الشيء في اذهان الاشخاص المتعددة ويمكن فرض صدقه عليها مع انه ليس بكل بل جزئى حقيقى. ويمكن الجواب عن الاول بمامر فى تقسيم العلم والمتواطى والمشكك من ان المراد من الصدق المعتبر في تعريف الجزئى و الكلى هو ما يكون على سبيل الشمول لا البدلية والشبح المرئى صدقه على الامور المتعددة انما هو من قبيل الثانى لا الاول ضرورة ان المصداق امر شخصى لاكثرة فيه ومن هنا يعلم ان النكرة التي يراد بها فرد من الافراد على سبيل البدلية داخلة تحت الجزئى و ان كان لها شيوع بحسب الافراد واطلاقهم الكلى عليه مبنى على التجوز كمامر. وعن الثانى بان المراد من الصدق المعتبر ايضاً ما يكون بحسب الوجود الاصلى لا التبعي الكلى ووجود حقيقة الجزئيات فى الاذهان العديدة من الثانى لا الاول ومن هنا يعلم الجواب عمار بما يتوهم من ان الصورة المنتزعة من الجزئى لو كانت جزئياً حقيقياً لزم قيام الحقيقى بمحال متعددة وهو محال قطعاً ضرورة ان الممتنع انما هو الجزئى بحسب الوجود الأصيل لا الكلى التبعى اذ لا منع منه لرجوعه اذا الى اشخاص متعددة و افراد متكثرة فتأمل.
Then here are two well-known investigations. The first: that the definition of the universal is not exclusive and the particular is not inclusive. Firstly, because the distant phantom (shabah) seen from afar is such that the mind allows its truth of many things and numerous objects, so it would have to be universal while it is really particular. Secondly, because the concept of every particular corresponds to the image of the thing in the minds of multiple persons, and it can be supposed to be true of them, while it is not universal but really particular. And the answer to the first is by what was mentioned in the division of knowledge, univocal and equivocal, that the intended truth considered in the definition of the particular and universal is that which is by way of inclusion (shumul) not substitution (badaliyya). And the distant phantom's truth of multiple things is of the second kind, not the first, because the referent is a personal thing with no multiplicity. From this it is known that the indefinite noun intended for an individual by way of substitution falls under the particular, even if it has generality in terms of individuals, and their calling it 'universal' is based on metaphor, as before. And the answer to the second is that the intended truth considered is also that which is according to original existence (al-wujud al-asli), not derivative and universal. And the existence of the reality of particulars in multiple minds is of the second kind, not the first. From this, the answer is known to what is imagined: that if the form abstracted from the particular were really particular, then the imaginary form would subsist in multiple places, which is absolutely impossible. Indeed, what is impossible is the particular according to original existence, not the derivative universal, for there is no prevention of it because it then refers back to multiple persons and numerous individuals. So reflect.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two well-known investigations concerning the definitions of universal (kulli) and particular (juz'i). The objection is that the definition of kulli is not exclusive (mani') and the definition of juz'i is not inclusive (jami'). Two objections are raised. First: a phantom seen from afar (shabah mar'i) can be supposed true of many things (e.g., a shape could be many things), yet it is really particular, so the definition of kulli fails as exclusive (it would include it), and the definition of juz'i fails as inclusive (it would exclude it). Second: the concept of any particular, e.g., Zayd, corresponds to the image in many minds, and can be supposed true of those mental instances, so it would be universal, but it is particular. The answer to the first: truth in the definition is of two kinds: inclusive (shumul) and substitutive (badaliyya). The phantom is true of multiple things only via substitution (i.e., it could be this or that, not all at once), while the definition requires inclusive truth (applicable to all simultaneously). So the phantom remains particular. Indefinite nouns intended for a single individual by substitution also fall under particular. The answer to the second: truth must be by original existence (al-wujud al-asli), not derivative existence (like mental existence). The particulars in multiple minds have only derivative existence, not original existence, so they do not make the concept universal. Thus, the definitions are saved.

Key Terms
الصدق (al-sidq): truth; here distinguished into inclusive (shumul) and substitutive (badaliyya)
الوجود الأصلي (al-wujud al-asli): original existence in external reality
الوجود التبعي (al-wujud al-taba'i): derivative existence, e.g., mental existence

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How is the objection about the distant phantom answered?
A) The phantom is not a real thing.
B) The truth of the phantom over many things is substitutive, not inclusive.
C) The phantom is actually universal.
D) The definition of kulli does not apply to phantoms.

Key Takeaway
The definitions of universal and particular require truth to be inclusive and by original existence; otherwise, apparent counterexamples arise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Linguistic Objection to 'Kathirin' in Definitions
Objections against the definitions based on the word 'kathirin' and its usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the linguistic restriction of 'kathirin' and propose 'kathra' as a suitable replacement in definitions of universal (kulli) and particular (juz'i).

Core Idea
The term 'kathirin' in the definition of universal is restricted to rational beings, making it inadequate.

Al-Yazdi's commentary raises a sharp linguistic objection against the standard definitions of kulli and juz'i. The problem lies in the word 'kathirin' (many), which carries a grammatical restriction that undermines the logical universality of the definition. Let's dissect this objection step by step.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definitions
Remember that kulli (universal) is defined as 'that for which it is not impossible to suppose its truth of many (kathirin)', and juz'i (particular) as 'that for which it is impossible'. These definitions are the target of the objection.
2
First objection: numerical threshold
The objector argues that if a universal concept applies only to two to five individuals, it would be considered particular by the definition because 'kathirin' is lexically defined as six or more (based on the rule that the least of 'many' is two, but the plural 'kathirin' is used for three or more? Actually, the passage says three measures of 'kathir' is six). This means a universal with only 2-5 instances would falsely be classified as particular.
The lexical reference: the least of 'kathir' (many) is two, but 'kathirin' as a plural form is restricted to six+ due to grammatical rules.
3
Second objection: restriction to rational beings
The grammatical form 'kathirin' (with waw and nun) is only used for rational beings. Thus, the definition of universal would not apply to universals of non-rational entities (e.g., 'horse', 'tree'), incorrectly classifying them as particular.
This is a crucial linguistic point: the broken plural 'kathra' (multitude) is general, while 'kathirin' is sound masculine plural for rationals.
4
Proposed replacement and answer
The commentator suggests replacing 'kathirin' with 'kathra' (multitude), as done in the chapter on the five universals. An answer to the first objection is given: every universal has at least six individuals, so the threshold is never problematic. (The passage is cut off here.)
The answer claims that no universal exists that does not apply to six or more, but this is debatable.

And the answer to the first: that there is no universal that does not apply to six and above, because every universal has individuals not... (cut off)

Commentary
This passage presents the second of two investigations. It points out a linguistic issue with the word 'kathirin' (many) in the definitions of kulli and juz'i. The definition of kulli is 'that for which it is not impossible to suppose its truth of many (kathirin)', and juz'i is 'that for which it is impossible to suppose its truth of many'. The objection is twofold. First: if a universal applies only to two to five individuals, it would be considered particular by the definition because 'kathirin' means six or more (based on lexical rules: the least of 'many' is two, but the plural of 'kathir' is 'akthar'? The answer to the first objection is that no universal has individuals only up to five; every universal has at least six. The Meaning of 'Farḍ' in Definitions of Particular and Universal
Author defends definitions by clarifying the intended meaning of 'farḍ'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mere supposition and rational permissibility in the definition of particular and universal, and explain how this distinction resolves a logical doubt.

Core Idea
The definitions hinge on rational permissibility, not on whether we can imagine it.

In defining the particular and the universal, logicians use the term 'farḍ' (supposition). A doubt arises: since we can imagine any particular being true of many individuals (e.g., Zayd), every particular would become universal. Conversely, if we ignore 'farḍ', universals with no real instances (like the partner of God) become problematic. This lesson unpacks the commentator's critical refinement: 'farḍ' here means rational permissibility, not mere imagination.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doubt about 'farḍ' as a condition
The doubt: if 'farḍ' is taken as a condition in the division, then every particular (like Zayd) can be supposed to apply to many, making it universal. This would collapse the distinction.
Look at the Arabic: 'ما من جزئي إلا أنه لا يمتنع فرض صدقه على كثيرين'.
2
Identify the opposite problem without 'farḍ'
If 'farḍ' is not considered, then the definition of particular fails to exclude universals that have no actual instances (e.g., partner of God, Phoenix). These universals are impossible to apply to anything, yet they are universal concepts.
These are called 'kulliyat la afrada laha fi nafs al-amr'.
3
Distinguish the two meanings of 'farḍ'
The author states that 'farḍ' here does not mean mere supposition (mujarrad al-farḍ wa al-taqdīr), but rather rational permissibility (tajwīz al-'aql wa irtḍā'uhu). This is the key move.
The difference: mere supposition is any mental act; rational permissibility is the mind's judgment that the concept could coherently apply.
4
Apply the correct meaning to the definitions
For the particular (like Zayd), the mind does not deem it permissible for the concept to apply to many, because his essence is particular. For the universal (like animal), the mind deems it permissible. Thus the definitions are preserved.
The doubt is resolved without requiring actual instances for universals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في المفاهيم صدق الجزئى على كثيرين لم لا يجوز ان يقدر ان زيداً مثلاً يصدق على افراد كثيرة و جزئيات غفيرة و ليس هذا بابعد مما قالوا: من انه يجوز ان يفرض انسان ذورؤس متعددة او لا رأس له وبالجملة ان اعتبر قيد الفرض في التقسيم - كما فعله المصنف لزم جعل الشيء قسيماً له لنفسه اذ ما من جزئي الا انه . لا يمتنع فرض صدقه على كثيرين كما هو ظاهر و ان لم يعتبر اختل حد الجزئى منعاً والكلى جمعاً بالكليات التي ليست لها افراد محققة في نفس الامر كشريك البارى تعالى والعنقاء مثلاً، لظهور انها يمتنع صدقها على شيء. و حاصل الدفع : ان ليس المراد من الفرض هيهنا مجرد الفرض و التقدير حتى يرد ما ذكر على تقدير اعتباره قيداً بل تجويز العقل وارتضائه به وسيأتى فى هذا زيادة كلام انشاء الله تعالى.
Regarding the concepts: The truth of the particular for many [individuals] — why is it not permissible to suppose that Zayd, for example, is true for many individuals and numerous particulars? And this is not more remote than what they said: that it is permissible to suppose a human with multiple heads or no head. In short, if the condition of supposition (farḍ) is considered in the division — as the author did — then a thing would become a correlate of itself, because there is no particular except that it is not impossible to suppose its truth for many, as is apparent. And if it is not considered, then the definition of particular fails to be exclusive (man'u) and the definition of universal fails to be inclusive (jami'), due to universals that have no actual individuals in re, like the partner of God Almighty and the Phoenix, for example, because it is clear that their truth for anything is impossible. And the gist of the rebuttal is that the intended meaning of 'farḍ' here is not mere supposition and estimation such that what was mentioned would apply if it were considered a condition, but rather it is the mind's deeming permissible and approving it. And there will be further discussion on this later, God willing.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of a definition. The author addresses a doubt about the definitions of 'particular' (juz'i) and 'universal' (kulli). The doubt is that if we take a particular like Zayd, we can imagine it applying to many individuals, which would make it universal. The author points out that this doubt arises from misunderstanding the term 'farḍ' (supposition) in the definition. If 'farḍ' is taken as a condition in the division (as the author of the original text did), then every particular would become a universal because there is no particular that cannot be imagined to apply to many. This leads to a contradiction. Conversely, if 'farḍ' is not considered a condition, then the definition fails to exclude some universals (like the partner of God) which are impossible to apply to anything. The author's response is to distinguish two meanings of 'farḍ': mere supposition (al-farḍ wa al-taqdīr) versus rational permissibility (tajwīz al-'aql wa irtḍā'uhu). The intended meaning in the definition is the latter: the mind deems it permissible that the concept applies to many, not that we actually imagine it. This resolves the doubt: Zayd's concept is such that the mind does not deem it permissible to apply to many, because his essence is particular. The author also mentions that there will be more clarification later. This is a typical hashiya move: refining the meaning of a key term in the matn to preserve the definitions.

Key Terms
الجزئي (al-juz'ī)
الكلي (al-kullī)
الفرض (al-farḍ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Conditional possibility
D) Imaginary occurrence

Key Takeaway
The definitions of particular and universal are preserved by interpreting 'farḍ' as rational permissibility, not mere supposition.

Defending the Definitions of Universal and Particular
Author presents two objections to the definitions and answers them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between inclusive (shumūlī) and substitutive (badalī) truth, and between original (aṣlī) and derivative (tabi'ī) existence, and use these distinctions to defend the standard definitions of universal and particular against common objections.

Core Idea
The definitions are saved by twin distinctions: inclusive vs. substitutive truth and original vs. derivative existence.

In this passage, the author addresses two famous objections that appear to break the standard definitions of universal and particular. The first uses the example of a shadow seen from afar, which seems truly applicable to many things yet is itself a particular. The second uses the concept of a particular, which exists in many minds. The answers rely on two key distinctions: the mode of truth (inclusive vs. substitutive) and the mode of existence (original vs. derivative). Understanding these distinctions is essential for mastering the logical definitions.

Guided Path
1
Recall the standard definitions
A universal is that which can be truly predicated of many (e.g., 'man' applies to Zayd, 'Amr, etc.). A particular is that which cannot be truly predicated of many (e.g., 'Zayd' applies to only one). The objections claim these definitions are not exclusive (do not exclude non-universals) and not inclusive (do not include all particulars).
2
First objection: the shadow seen from afar
The shadow seen from afar can be deemed by the mind to be true of many individuals (one might think it is Zayd, then 'Amr, etc.). Yet the shadow itself is a single particular thing. So it seems to satisfy the definition of universal while being particular.
The objection hinges on the meaning of 'truth' (ṣidq) in the definition.
3
First answer: inclusive vs. substitutive truth
The truth considered in the definition is inclusive (shumūlī): the concept applies to each individual simultaneously and fully. The shadow's truth for multiple things is substitutive (badalī): it applies to one individual at a time. Since the shadow is not true of many in the inclusive sense, it remains particular. This also explains why indefinite nouns like 'a man' are particular.
4
Second objection: concept of a particular in multiple minds
The concept of a particular (e.g., the concept of Zayd) corresponds to an image in many minds. One might suppose it is truly predicated of those mental images, making it seem universal while it is the concept of a single individual.
5
Second answer: original vs. derivative existence
The truth considered is based on original existence (al-wujūd al-aṣlī), i.e., external reality. The concept exists in multiple minds in a derivative (tabi'ī) manner. The definition concerns original existence, so the mental copies do not count. A true particular in original existence is only one; the mental existence is different, so no impossibility arises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم هيهنا بحثان مشهوران الأول: ان تعريف الكلى ليس بمانع والجزئى ليس بجامع اما اولاً فلان الشبح المرئى من البعيد مما يجوز العقل صدقه على امور كثيرة و اشياء عديدة فيلزم ان يكون كلياً مع انه جزئى حقيق. و اما ثانياً، فلان مفهوم كل جزئى مطابق لصورة الشيء في اذهان الاشخاص المتعددة ويمكن فرض صدقه عليها مع انه ليس بكل بل جزئى حقيقى. ويمكن الجواب عن الاول بمامر فى تقسيم العلم والمتواطى والمشكك من ان المراد من الصدق المعتبر في تعريف الجزئى و الكلى هو ما يكون على سبيل الشمول لا البدلية والشبح المرئى صدقه على الامور المتعددة انما هو من قبيل الثانى لا الاول ضرورة ان المصداق امر شخصى لاكثرة فيه ومن هنا يعلم ان النكرة التي يراد بها فرد من الافراد على سبيل البدلية داخلة تحت الجزئى و ان كان لها شيوع بحسب الافراد واطلاقهم الكلى عليه مبنى على التجوز كمامر. وعن الثانى بان المراد من الصدق المعتبر ايضاً ما يكون بحسب الوجود الاصلى لا التبعي الكلى ووجود حقيقة الجزئيات فى الاذهان العديدة من الثانى لا الاول ومن هنا يعلم الجواب عمار بما يتوهم من ان الصورة المنتزعة من الجزئى لو كانت جزئياً حقيقياً لزم قيام الحقيقى بمحال متعددة وهو محال قطعاً ضرورة ان الممتنع انما هو الجزئى بحسب الوجود الأصيل لا الكلى التبعى اذ لا منع منه لرجوعه اذا الى اشخاص متعددة و افراد متكثرة فتأمل.
Then here are two famous investigations. The first: The definition of the universal is not exclusive (man'u) and the particular is not inclusive (jami'). First, because the shadow seen from afar is such that the mind deems its truth for many things and numerous entities permissible, so it would follow that it is universal, while it is truly particular. Second, because the concept of every particular corresponds to the image of the thing in the minds of multiple persons, and it is possible to suppose its truth for them, while it is not universal but truly particular. The answer to the first is by what was mentioned in the division of 'ilm, mutawāṭi', and mushakkik: that the intended truth considered in the definition of the particular and the universal is that which is in the mode of inclusion (shumūl), not substitution (badaliyya). The truth of the shadow seen for multiple things is of the second type, not the first, necessarily because the referent is a personal matter with no multiplicity in it. From this it is known that the indefinite noun (nakira) intended for an individual from among individuals in the mode of substitution falls under the particular, even if it has commonality (shuyū') among individuals, and their applying the universal to it is based on metaphor (tajawwuz), as previously. The answer to the second is that the intended truth considered is also according to original existence (al-wujūd al-aṣlī), not derivative (al-tabi'ī). The existence of the reality of particulars in multiple minds is of the second type, not the first. From this the answer is known regarding what is imagined: that if the form abstracted from the particular were truly particular, it would necessitate the subsistence of the true particular in multiple loci, which is absolutely impossible, because what is impossible is only the particular according to original existence, not the derivative universal, since there is no prevention from it because it then refers to multiple individuals and numerous particulars. So reflect.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two famous investigations concerning the definitions of universal and particular. The investigation is that the definition of universal is not exclusive (it does not exclude what should be excluded) and the definition of particular is not inclusive (it does not include what should be included). Two objections are raised. The first objection: a shadow seen from afar can be deemed by the mind to be true of many things (e.g., a person could think it is Zayd, 'Amr, etc.), yet that shadow is a true particular (it is a single, individual shadow). So the definition of universal incorrectly includes it. The second objection: the concept (meaning) of any particular (e.g., the concept of Zayd) corresponds to the mental image in multiple minds, and we can suppose it to be true of those mental images, but the concept is still particular (it is the concept of a single individual). So again the definition fails. The author answers these using distinctions already made earlier. For the first: the truth (ṣidq) considered in the definition is inclusive truth (shumūlī), meaning the concept applies to each individual fully and simultaneously, not substitutive truth (badalī) where it applies to one individual at a time. The shadow's truth for multiple things is substitutive (it can be Zayd, 'Amr, etc., only one at a time), not inclusive. Hence it is not universal. This also explains why indefinite nouns (nakira) like 'a man' are particular, because they are used for one individual at a time, even though they have generality. For the second: the truth considered is according to original existence (al-wujūd al-aṣlī), which means external existence. The concept of Zayd exists in multiple minds in a derivative (tabi'ī) way, not original. Since the definition concerns original existence, the mental copies do not count. The imagined consequence that a true particular would exist in multiple loci is avoided because the particular in original existence is only one; the mental existence is different. This shows the sophistication of the logician's definitions.

Key Terms
الصدق الشمولي
الصدق البدلي
الوجود الأصلي
الوجود التبعي
النكرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is a particular object in reality.
B) Because its truth for multiple things is substitutive, not inclusive.
C) Because it has no concept.
D) Because it is not a legal entity.

Key Takeaway
The definitions of universal and particular are robust when truth is understood as inclusive (shumūlī) and existence as original (aṣlī).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

(Answer: universal, because it applies to all prophets inclusively; e.g., 'Muhammad is a prophet', 'Isa is a prophet' – the concept is true of each simultaneously.)

(Answer: The concept in multiple minds has derivative existence; the truth considered in the definition of universal is based on original existence, so the mental copies do not count.)

Defending the Definition of Universal from Linguistic Objections
Author addresses two objections based on 'kathīrīn' and its linguistic implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two linguistic objections to the definition of universal and evaluate the author's responses.

Core Idea
Definitions in logic are not bound by strict linguistic rules; logicians use language flexibly for conceptual clarity.

In this passage, the author of the Hashiya defends the standard logical definitions of 'particular' and 'universal' against two objections arising from the word 'kathīrīn' (many). The objections are based on language: one on the number that 'many' implies, the other on the grammatical form of the plural. The author's replies show that logicians prioritize conceptual meaning over narrow linguistic conventions. Understanding this defense helps you see how Islamic logic maintains precision without being enslaved to grammar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions under fire
Locate the definitions of particular (juz'ī) and universal (kullī) that the objections target. The objection claims that the definition of particular is not exclusive (māni') and the universal is not inclusive (jāmi').
The definitions are implied: particular is 'that whose concept does not prevent it being predicated of many', universal is the opposite.
2
Analyze the first objection: the number six
The objector argues that if a universal applied only to two to five individuals, it would satisfy the definition of particular, because 'kathīrīn' (many) is only used for six or more. Find the linguistic premise that 'much' (al-kathīr) starts at six.
The passage states that the minimum of much is six, based on language books.
3
Examine the author's first response
The author replies that no universal exists that is not true for six and above, because every universal has unlimited individuals, at least mentally. The truth considered in definitions includes mental as well as external existence.
This response highlights that logical definitions consider all possible individuals, not just actual.
4
Analyze the second objection: grammatical plural for rational beings
The objector points out that 'kathīrīn' is a sound masculine plural (jam' mudhakkar sālim), which in Arabic is used only for rational beings. Therefore, non-rational universals (e.g., 'horse') would not be called 'kathīrīn' and thus would not be covered by the definition.
The term 'kathīrīn' appears in the standard definition of universal.
5
Assess the author's second and third responses
The author gives two responses: first, the plural can be used generically for both rational and non-rational; second, logicians are not bound by strict linguistic conventions and use words for their conceptual meanings, citing examples like 'Daraba Hind' without the feminine marker.
This shows the principle that logical discourse prioritizes meaning over grammatical form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثانى ان تعريف الجزئى ليس بمانع والكلى ليس بجامع، اما اولاً ، فلاته اذا فرض كلى يصدق على الاثنين فصاعداً الى الخمسة ولا يطلق على ما فوقها، يصدق عليه تعريف الجزئي دون الكلى لان الكثيرين لا يطلق الأعلى السنة فصاعداً لما تقرر من ان اقل الجمع ثلاثة مقادير مفرده و ثلاثة مقادير الكثير سنة لا محالة لما تبين فى كتب اللغة من ان اقل ما يطلق عليه الكثير اثنان. وأما ثانياً، فلانه يصدق تعريف الجزئى على كل كلى من غير ذوى العقول ولا يصدق عليه تعريف الكلى وذلك لان «الكثيرين» جمع بالواو والنون وكل جمع بهما لا يطلق الاعلى ذوى العقول كما هو المذكور في كتب الفحول فالأولى ان يقال: «على الكثرة بدل على كثيرين كما فعله فى باب الكليات الخمس. ويمكن الجواب عن الاول : بانه لا يوجد كلى لا يصدق على الستة فافوقها اذ كل كلى له افراد غير متناهية ولو بحسب الذهن، والصدق المعتبر فى التعريف اعم من الخارجي والذهني. و عن الثاني بان الجمع بالواو والنون وان كان موضوعاً ليستعمل في العقلاء فقط الا انه قد يستعمل فيما يعمهم وغير هم و في غيرهم خاصة. وقد اجيب عنهما بان ارباب المعقول لا ينظرون الى الاصطلاحات اللغوية والنحوية و يكتفون في المقاصد بمجرد الكلم الدالة على المعاني والمفاهيم فلو سمعت من احد منهم «ضرب هند» بلاتاء او ضربت زید معها فلا تعجب عن ذلك.
And the second [investigation]: That the definition of particular is not exclusive and the universal is not inclusive. First, because if a universal is supposed to be true for from two up to five, and not applied to what is above that, the definition of particular would apply to it, not the universal, because 'kathīrīn' (many) is only applied to six and above, since it is established that the minimum of plural is three singulars, and the minimum of much (al-kathīr) is necessarily six, as explained in books of language that the minimum to which 'much' is applied is two. Second, because the definition of particular applies to every universal that is not of rational beings, and the definition of universal does not apply to it, because 'al-kathīrīn' is a plural with wāw and nūn, and every plural with these two is only applied to rational beings, as mentioned in authoritative books. So it would be better to say: 'upon kathra (multitude)' instead of 'upon kathīrīn', as was done in the chapter on the five universals. The answer to the first: There is no universal that is not true for six and above, because every universal has unlimited individuals, even mentally, and the truth considered in the definition is more general than external and mental. The answer to the second: The plural with wāw and nūn, although used only for rational beings, is sometimes used generically for both rational and non-rational, and also specifically for non-rational. And it has been answered that the people of logic (arbāb al-ma'qūl) do not look at linguistic and grammatical conventions, and are content in their purposes with mere words that indicate meanings and concepts. So if you hear from one of them 'Daraba Hind' without tā' or 'Darabat Zayd' with it, do not be surprised.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage presents the second investigation concerning the definitions. Two objections are based on the word 'kathīrīn' (many). First objection: If there were a universal that applies to only two to five individuals, it would meet the definition of particular (since it does not apply to many, where 'many' is at least six). The author answers that no such universal exists; every universal has unlimited individuals, at least mentally (fī al-dhihn), so it will always be true for six or more. Second objection: The word 'kathīrīn' is a plural using the wāw-nūn suffix, which grammatically is used only for rational beings (like 'muslimūn'). So non-rational universals (like 'horse') would not be called 'kullī' because they apply to non-rationals, not to 'kathīrīn'. The author responds by noting that the plural form can be used generically, or that logicians are not bound by strict grammatical rules; they use language loosely for conceptual purposes. Thus, the definitions are sound.

Logical definitions are judged by conceptual precision, not by adherence to grammatical rules; logicians may use language flexibly to convey meaning.

Why Particular Is Called Juz'ī and Universal Kullī: The Part-Whole Relation
Author explains why juz'i is called juz'i and kulli is called kulli based on part-whole relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the part-whole relation between particular and universal and identify which universals are parts of particulars.

Core Idea
The particular is a whole containing the universal as a part.

This passage clarifies why the particular is named 'juz'ī' (part-related) and the universal 'kullī' (whole-related). The naming is based on a part-whole relation: the universal is a part of the particular's essence. However, this applies only to essential universals (genus, differentia, species), not to accidental attributes. Understanding this relation helps in forming correct definitions and avoiding confusion between essential and accidental predicates.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the naming puzzle
The particular is called juz'ī, which literally means 'part-related', but intuitively the particular is a whole containing the universal. Note the inversion: the particular is a whole relative to the universal.
This inversion is key to understanding the terminology.
2
Understand the part-whole hierarchy
Trace the hierarchy: Zayd (particular) contains 'human' (species) as a part; 'human' contains 'animal' (genus) as a part; 'animal' contains 'growing body', 'absolute body', 'substance'. Each higher universal is a part of the lower.
The part-whole relation here is compositional, not set-theoretic.
3
Apply the qualification 'mostly'
The author says 'ghāliban' (for the most part) to exclude accidental universals (khāṣṣa, 'araḍ 'āmm) which are not parts of the individual's essence. For example, 'laughing' is not part of Zayd's essence.
Accidental attributes are external to the essence (māhiyya).
4
Connect to definition (hadd)
In a proper definition (hadd), the genus and differentia are parts of the species, and the species is part of the individual. This structure mirrors the part-whole relation explained here.
Essential universals are constituents of the definiendum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم انما سمى الجزئى جزئياً و الكلى كلياً، لان الجزئى كل للكلى والكلى جزء للجزئي غالباً كالانسان فانه جزء لزيد حيث انه مركب منه و من التشخص الخارجى و كالخيوان فانه جزء للانسان حيث انه حيوان ناطق و هكذا الجسم النامي جزء للحيوان والجسم المطلق جزء له و الجوهر جزء له ولاشك ان كلاً من الكل والجزء منسوب الى الاخر ضرورة ان تحقق مفهوم كل منها انما هو بالنسبة الى الآخر فالجزئى كل منسوب الى الجزء و الكلى جزء منسوب الى الكل هذا . و انما قيدنا بقولنا: ((غالباً» احترازا عن بعض الكليات ا الكليات التي ليست الجزئيات كلا لها كالخاصة والعرض العام حيث انهما خارجان عن ماهية الافراد وحقيقتها فلا يكونان جزء لها بخلاف النوع والجنس والفصل فان الاخيرين جزئان للاول و هو للشخص وسيأتي بيان ذلك انشاء الله تعالى
Then indeed the particular is called 'juz'ī' (particular) and the universal 'kullī' (universal) because the particular is a whole (kull) for the universal, and the universal is a part (juz') for the particular, for the most part (ghāliban). For example, 'human' (insān) is a part of Zayd, because Zayd is composed of it and of external individuation (tashakhkhuṣ khārijī). And 'animal' (ḥayawān) is a part of 'human', because 'human' is a rational animal. Likewise, 'growing body' (jism nāmī) is a part of 'animal', and 'absolute body' (jism muṭlaq) is a part of it, and 'substance' (jawhar) is a part of it. There is no doubt that each of the whole and the part is related to the other, necessarily because the realization of the concept of each is only in relation to the other. So the particular is a whole related to the part, and the universal is a part related to the whole. We only qualified with our saying 'mostly' (ghāliban) to exclude some universals that are not such that the particulars are wholes for them, like the property (khāṣṣa) and the general accident ('araḍ 'āmm), because they are external to the essence (māhiyya) of the individuals and their reality, so they are not parts of them. This is unlike the species (naw'), genus (jins), and differentia (faṣl), because the last two are parts of the first (the species), and the species is part of the individual. And its explanation will come, God willing.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage explains the terminological reason for why the particular is called 'juz'ī' and the universal 'kullī'. The author states that the particular is actually a whole (kull) in relation to the universal, because the universal is a constituent part of the particular's essence. For example, Zayd is a particular human; his essence includes the universal 'human' as a part (with external individuation). Similarly, 'human' has 'animal' as a part, and so on up to 'substance'. So the universal is a part of the particular. Conversely, the particular is a whole containing that part. This is why the particular is called 'juz'ī' (relative to the part, i.e., it is a whole containing parts), and the universal is called 'kullī' (relative to the whole, i.e., it is a part of a whole). The author adds the qualification 'mostly' (ghāliban) to exclude non-essential universals like property (khāṣṣa) and general accident ('araḍ 'āmm) which are not parts of the essence of their individuals. For example, 'laughing' (property of human) is not a part of the essence of Zayd. This will be clarified later.

Key Terms
الكل
الجزء
غالباً
الخاصة
العرض العام

Key Takeaway
The naming of particular and universal is based on a part-whole relation: the universal is a part of the particular's essence, but only for essential universals.

The Four Modal Concepts: Universal Possibility and Its Derivatives
Defines modal concepts and resolves classification objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-mumkin al-'amm and al-mumkin al-khas, and identify how al-wajib and al-mumtani' are derived from universal possibility.

Core Idea
Universal possibility is the key; all modal categories are its subcases.

The tradition of Islamic logic carefully distinguishes between broad and narrow possibility. This passage defines the four fundamental modal categories and shows how the necessary and impossible are actually subsumed under universal possibility. It also defends a logical division against a subtle objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
Find the four terms in the Arabic: الممكن العام, الممكن الخاص, الواجب الوجود, الممتنع. Note that the last three are said to be individuals of the first.
2
Understand the definition of universal possibility
Universal possibility (الإمكان العام) is defined as negation of necessity from one of the two sides of a thing, regardless of the other side. It has two subcases: negating necessity from existence, or negating necessity from non-existence.
3
Derive the other three categories
From the first subcase (negation from existence): if non-existence is necessary, it is impossible; if not, it is particular possibility. From the second subcase (negation from non-existence): if existence is necessary, it is necessary existent; if not, it is again particular possibility.
Thus particular possibility appears twice, and necessary and impossible are each derived from one subcase.
4
Analyze the objection
The objector argues that if 'possible' means universal possibility, then impossibility cannot be its opposite because impossibility is an individual of universal possibility (from the first subcase). If it means particular possibility, then the division with necessary existent is invalid.
5
Understand the response
The author distinguishes two senses of universal possibility: one that includes the impossible (negation from existence) and one that does not (negation from non-existence). The intended sense here is the latter, so impossibility is not an individual of it, and the division stands.
This shows the importance of clarifying which side of the thing is being considered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم ان هنا امور اربعة : الممكن العام و الممكن الخاص والواجب الوجود و الممتنع. والثلاثة الاخر افراد الاول و مندرج تحته. توضيح المقام ان الامكان العام عبارة عن سلب الضرورة عن احد طرفي الشيء مع قطع النظر عن الآخر و بعبارة اخرى هو عبارة عن سلب الضرورة اما عن وجود الشيء مع قطع النظر عن طرف العدم واما عن عدمه مع النظر عن طرف الوجود، فاذا اعتبرنا الأول بان سلبنا الضرورة عن الوجود فعدمه اما ان يكون ضروريا فهو المتنع أو لا يكون كذلك فهو الامكان الخاص و اذا اعتبرنا الثاني بان سلبنا الضرورة عن العدم موجوده اما ان يكون ضرورياً فهو الواجب الوجود او لا يكون كذلك فهو الممكن الخاص ايضاً. اذا عرفت هذا فاعلم انه اورد على قوله او امكنت بان المراد من الامكان ان كان هو الامكان العام، فلا يصح جعله مقابلاً لقوله : او امتنعت اذ الامتناع كما عرفت فرد من الامكان العام وفرد الشيء لا يكون مقابلاً له وقسيماً وان كان المراد منه الامكان الخاص فلا يصح تقسيمه به و بالواجب الوجود بقوله : ولم يوجد او وجد الواحد. والجواب ان المراد به الامكان العام وقوله لا يصح جمله مقابلاً لقوله امتنعت الخ، ممنوع، لان الامتناع فرد للامكان العام الذى هو سلب الضرورة عن طرف الوجود و مرادنا بالامكان هنا ليس ما ذكر بل الامكان العام الذى هو سلب الضرورة عن طرف العدم والممتنع ليس فرداً له. (آخوند ملا على اکبر اهری)
Know that there are four matters: the universal possible, the particular possible, the necessary existent, and the impossible. The other three are individuals of the first and subsumed under it. Clarification of the position: universal possibility is the negation of necessity from one of the two sides of a thing regardless of the other; in other words, it is the negation of necessity either from the existence of a thing regardless of the side of non-existence, or from its non-existence regardless of the side of existence. So if we consider the first, that we negate necessity from existence, then its non-existence is either necessary—so it is impossible—or not necessary—so it is particular possibility. And if we consider the second, that we negate necessity from non-existence, then its existence is either necessary—so it is the necessary existent—or not necessary—so it is also particular possibility. When you understand this, then know that an objection was raised against his statement 'or it is possible': that if by ability is meant universal possibility, then making it opposite to his statement 'or it is impossible' is not valid, for impossibility, as you know, is an individual of universal possibility, and the individual of a thing is not opposite to it and a co-ordinate; and if by it is meant particular possibility, then dividing by it and by the necessary existent with his statement 'and it did not exist or one existed' is not valid. The answer: what is meant by it is universal possibility, and his statement 'it is not valid to make it opposite to his statement 'or it is impossible' etc.' is denied, because impossibility is an individual of universal possibility which is the negation of necessity from the side of existence, but our intention by ability here is not what was mentioned; rather it is universal possibility which is the negation of necessity from the side of non-existence, and the impossible is not an individual of it.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage defines the four modal categories and defends a division against an objection. The author begins by stating that there are four affairs: al-mumkin al-'amm (universal possible), al-mumkin al-khas (particular possible), al-wajib al-wujud (necessary existent), and al-mumtani' (impossible). The last three are individuals of the first and subsumed under it. Then he clarifies the meaning of al-imkan al-'amm: it is the negation of necessity from one of the two sides of a thing (existence or non-existence) without considering the other. He elaborates: it is either negation of necessity from existence (regardless of non-existence) or negation of necessity from non-existence (regardless of existence). From the first perspective, if we negate necessity from existence, then the non-existence of that thing is either necessary (making it impossible) or not necessary (making it al-mumkin al-khas, or particular possible). From the second perspective, if we negate necessity from non-existence, then the existence is either necessary (al-wajib) or not necessary (again al-mumkin al-khas). Then he raises an objection: the author's statement ('or it is possible') is criticized because if 'imkan' here means universal possibility, then it cannot be opposite to 'or it is impossible', since impossibility is an individual of universal possibility; an individual cannot be opposite to its genus. If it means particular possibility, then dividing things by it and by the necessary existent (as in 'it did not exist or one existed') is invalid. The answer: the intended meaning is universal possibility, but as the negation of necessity from the side of non-existence, not from the side of existence. Impossibility is an individual of the latter sense, not the former. Thus the division is sound. This lesson uses an example from logic itself, but the same reasoning applies to modal concepts in kalam, e.g., the world's contingency.

Key Terms
الممكن العام
الممكن الخاص
الواجب الوجود
الممتنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is al-mumkin al-khas (particular possibility)?
A) The negation of necessity from one side regardless of the other.
B) That which is neither necessary in existence nor necessary in non-existence.
C) The necessary existent.
D) The impossible.

Key Takeaway
The four modal categories are derived from the two senses of universal possibility, and a careful distinction resolves the objection about opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the following statement: 'A thing is either necessary or impossible or contingent.' Explain how this classification relates to the four modal categories defined in this passage.
?
Construct a short argument using the concept of al-mumkin al-khas to prove that the world requires a creator, and identify which modal concepts appear.
