The Concept of Wajib al-Wujud as a Universal
Defending wajib al-wujud as a universal via two glances

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to defend the classification of wajib al-wujud as a universal using the distinction between correct reasoning and the first glance, and evaluate objections based on impossibility of multiple instances.

Core Idea
A concept is universal if the mind can conceive multiple instances, even if reality limits it to one.

This lesson tackles a subtle objection from classical logic: if the concept of the Necessary Existent (wajib al-wujud) cannot apply to more than one individual in reality, how can it be considered a universal? The author, commenting on a division of universals into those whose individuals are possible or impossible, argues that the relevant sense of impossibility is only by correct reasoning, not by the naive 'first glance'. This distinction preserves the universality of the concept and has deep implications for kalam arguments on tawhid. We will walk through the dialectical exchange and sharpen our tools for assessing definitional classifications.

Guided Path
1
Extract the objection
Identify the claim that the concept of wajib al-wujud is particular because the mind judges it impossible to be true of many. Note that this objection rests on the assumption that impossibility in the mind disqualifies a concept from being universal.
The objection is stated as: 'the concept of the necessary existent is particular, because it is impossible for the mind for it to be true of many'.
2
Find the author's core distinction
Locate where the author distinguishes two types of mental impossibility: (1) impossibility by both correct reasoning (al-nazara al-sahiha) and the first glance (al-nazara al-ula, also called the foolish glance), and (2) impossibility by correct reasoning only. The concept of wajib al-wujud is impossible only in the second sense.
The author says: 'mental impossibility has two meanings...' This is the key move.
3
Apply the distinction to the division of universals
See how the author's division of universals into 'impossible individuals' and 'possible individuals' uses impossibility in the first sense (both glances). Since wajib al-wujud is possible in the first glance, it belongs to 'possible individuals', making it a universal.
The author states: 'the author, when he said its individuals are impossible or possible, intended by impossibility the first meaning'.
4
Assess the alternative defenses
The author also mentions two other answers: one interpreting 'individuals' as a genus applying to one or many, and another analyzing the phrase 'its individuals are impossible' as a universal affirmative whose negation yields a particular negative. Evaluate how these reinforce the main argument.
These are supplementary; the primary defense is the distinction of two glances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله او امتناعه كمفهوم واجب الوجود و بما عرفت من معنى تجويز العقل لايرد ما قيل: من ان مفهوم الواجب الوجود جزئى، لانه يمتنع عند العقل صدقه كثيرين، فلا يصح عده من اقسام الكلى فانه و ان كان العقل لا يحكم بالنظر الصحيح على صدقه على اكثر من فرد واحد، الا انه يجوزه بالنظرة الاولى التى تسمى بالنظرة الحمقاء والا لما اتخذ الشرك كثير من الاشقياء سبيلاً ولما افتقرنا في ابطاله الى ان نأتى دليلاً و لما احتيج إلى ان يرسل الله تعالى اليهم رسولاً، هذا مارى و قد اوردهنا ان مفهوم الواجب الوجود لا يصح عده من اقسام الممكنة الأفراد لان الممكن منه فرد واحد لا افراد متعددة فان المراد من الامتناع والامكان فى قوله امتنعت افراده او امکنت» هو الخارجيان والا لم يصح انقسام الكلى اليهما، لأنه يجب ان يكون الكلى ممكن الافراد بحسب الذهن والا لم يصح التقابل بينه و بين الجزئى كما هو ظاهر. والجواب : ان الامتناع العقلى ايضاً معنيين: الاول : الامتناع بالنظرة الصحيحة والنظرة الأولى معاً. والثاني الامتناع بالنظرة الصحيحة فقط والمصنف حيث قال: امتنعت افراده او امكنت اراد بالامتناع المعنى الاول وبالامكان ما يقابله فيشمل الامكان بهذا المعنى الامتناع بالمعنى الثاني ضرورة ان انتفاء المركب كما يكون بانتفاء جميع الاجزاء يكون بانتفاء احدها ايضاً ولا شك أن مفهوم واجب الوجود داخل في اقسام الممكنة الافراد بهذا المعنى فتأمل فانه دقيق. وقد أجيب بان المراد من الافراد جنس الفرد واطلاق الجنس على الواحد والكثير كثير. و اقول : هذا فى الجمع المحلى باللام ظاهر واما في الجمع المضاف كما هنا فلا ، فتأمل. وقد اجيب ايضاً بان :قوله امتنعت افراده موجبة كلبة فان الاضافة تفيد الاستغراق حيث لا عهد فيكون قوله: (أو امكنت» رفعاً للايجاب الكلى ورفع الايجاب الكلى كما يحتمل السلب الكلى يحتمل السلب الجزئى ايضاً فافهم. (محمد على)
His statement: 'or its impossibility like the concept of the necessary existent' and with what you have known of the meaning of mental permission, the objection that was raised is not valid: that the concept of the necessary existent is particular, because it is impossible for the mind for it to be true of many, so it should not be counted among the divisions of the universal. For even though the mind does not judge by correct reasoning that it is true of more than one individual, yet it permits it by the first glance which is called the foolish glance. Otherwise, many of the wretched would not have adopted polytheism, and we would not need to bring a proof to refute it, nor would there be need for Allah Almighty to send a messenger to them. This is what I see. And we have mentioned here that the concept of the necessary existent should not be counted among the divisions of the possible of individuals because its possible is one individual, not multiple individuals. For what is meant by impossibility and possibility in his statement 'its individuals are impossible or possible' are the external ones. Otherwise, the division of the universal into them would not be valid, because the universal must be possibly multiple individuals mentally; otherwise, the contrast between it and the particular would not be valid, as is apparent. The answer: mental impossibility also has two meanings: first, impossibility by the correct glance and the first glance together; second, impossibility by the correct glance only. The author, when he said 'its individuals are impossible or possible', intended by impossibility the first meaning, and by possibility what is opposed to it. So possibility in this sense includes impossibility in the second meaning, of necessity, because the negation of a compound occurs by the negation of all its parts as well as by negation of one of them. And there is no doubt that the concept of the necessary existent is included in the divisions of the possible of individuals in this sense, so reflect, for it is subtle. And it has been answered that by 'individuals' is meant the genus of individual, and the application of the genus to one and many is frequent. I say: this is apparent in the plural with the definite article, but not in the plural construct state as here, so reflect. And it has also been answered that his statement 'its individuals are impossible' is a universal affirmative, for the construct state indicates generality when there is no covenant. So his statement 'or it is possible' negates the universal affirmative. And the negation of a universal affirmative can be either a universal negative or a particular negative, so understand.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage addresses whether the concept of wajib al-wujud (the necessary existent) can be considered a universal, given that it can only apply to one external individual. The objection is that because the mind finds it impossible for this concept to be true of many, it must be a particular, not a universal. The author responds by appealing to the distinction between two types of mental impossibility: (1) impossibility by both correct reasoning (al-nazara al-sahiha) and the first glance (al-nazara al-ula), and (2) impossibility by correct reasoning only. In the author's division of universals into those whose individuals are impossible or possible, he means impossibility in the first sense (impossible even in the first glance). The concept of wajib al-wujud is impossible in the sense that correct reasoning limits it to one individual, but it is possible in the first glance (i.e., a naive mind can imagine multiple gods). Hence it falls under 'possible individuals' in the division. The author then considers two alternative defenses: one appealing to the generic meaning of 'individuals', and another analyzing the logical form of the phrase 'its individuals are impossible' as a universal affirmative whose negation yields a particular negative. This discussion is important for kalam because if the concept of God were a particular, it would not be subject to universal predicates, and arguments for tawhid would be affected.

النظرة الصحيحة
النظرة الأولى
النظرة الحمقاء
الممكنة الأفراد

B) Because the first glance (al-nazara al-ula) allows the mind to imagine it applying to many.
C) Because it is a genus that applies to one and many.
D) Because it is impossible in both senses of mental impossibility.

Key Takeaway
A concept is universal if the mind can conceive it as having multiple instances, even if correct reasoning restricts it to one; the distinction between the correct glance and the first glance resolves the apparent paradox.

Then, refute the objection using a real kalam example like the proof from alternate possibilities.

Division of Universals by Individual Existence
Author defines three cases for universals based on existence of individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any universal into one of three categories based on the existential status of its individuals.

Core Idea
Universals are divided by whether their individuals are impossible, necessary, or possible in external existence.

This passage explains how universals are categorized according to the existence of their individuals in the external world. This classification is fundamental in distinguishing between concepts like the Necessary Being and mythical entities like the phoenix. We will explore the three cases and the logical transition from possibility to necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three cases
Extract from the passage the three states of individuals for a universal: impossible (ممتنعة), one necessary (واجب الواحد), and possible (ممكنة).
The commentator also lists a finer subdivision: possible but non-existent, one exists with possibility of others, many finite or infinite.
2
Understand the logical transition
Note that the intellect first negates impossibility. Then it judges either necessity for one individual or possibility for all. The commentator affirms that moving from no impossibility to necessity is valid.
Necessity does not contradict possibility; rather, it is a stronger determination.
3
Apply to Islamic examples
Map each case to a real concept: the Necessary Being (واجب الوجود) for wajib al-fard, contingent beings (ممكنات) for mumkin, and the phoenix (عنقاء) for mumtani'.
The phoenix is a universal known by name but with no actual external individuals.
4
Recognize the commentator's refinement
The commentator suggests a clearer enumeration that explicitly lists all subcases. Compare with the original tripartite division.
This shows how later scholars expanded and clarified logical classifications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله أي لم تمتنع افراده في الخارج: بمعنى ان العقل لا يحيل تلبس فرد الكلى بالخروج الى الاعيان و بعد ان سلب العقل محالية تلبس فرد هذا الكلى بالخروج الى الاعيان فتارة يحكم بوجوب تلبس الفرد الواحد بالخروج الى العين ومنع البقية واخرى يمشى بالامكان على كافة افراد الكلى فالاول هو الواجب والثاني هو الممكن. ولا يخفى ان الترقى من سلب المحالية الى الوجوب لامانع منه، فان الواجب ليس بمحال قطعاً و ان كان الماتن لو عبر بهذا اللون من التعبير لكان أوضح و اسلس امتنعت افراده أو وجب الواحد منها او أمكنت ولم توجد او وجد الواحد منها مع امكان الغير و الكثير مع التناهى او عدمه» (التقريب ص ٢٥) (۸) هو بالفتح طائر عظيم معروف الاسم مجهول الجسم، قاله الجوهري في حياة الحيوان على ما نقل: العنقاء طائر غريب يبيض بيضاً كالجبال (في الجبال، خل) قيل : انما سميت به، لان في عنقها بياضاً كالطود. (محمد علی)
His statement: 'i.e. its individuals are not impossible in external existence' means: the intellect does not deem impossible the instantiation of the universal's individual in external existents. After the intellect removes impossibility from the instantiation of an individual of this universal in external existents, sometimes it judges that one individual must instantiate in externality and prevents the rest, and sometimes it allows possibility for all individuals of the universal. The first is the necessary, the second is the possible. It is not hidden that the transition from negation of impossibility to necessity is not prevented, for the necessary is certainly not impossible. However, if the author had expressed with this style it would be clearer and smoother: 'its individuals are impossible, or one of them is necessary, or they are possible but not existent, or one of them exists with possibility of others, whether many with finitude or not' (al-Taqrib p. 25). (8) It is with fatha: a great bird, name known, body unknown. al-Jawhari said in Hayat al-Hayawan as quoted: 'al-'Anqa' is a strange bird that lays eggs like mountains (in the mountains? it was said it is named because in its neck is whiteness like a mountain). (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage defines the division of universals based on the status of their individuals in external existence. The commentator explains that when the intellect does not consider it impossible for an individual of a universal to exist externally, then it may judge either that one individual must exist, making that universal 'obligatory' in its individual (wajib al-fard), or that all individuals are possible, making it 'possible' (mumkin al-afrad). The commentator notes that moving from no impossibility to necessity is acceptable, as necessity does not entail impossibility. He suggests the author could have phrased it more clearly by listing: individuals impossible (mumtani'), one necessary (wajib), possible but not existing, possible with one existing while others possible, and possible with many finitely or infinitely. Then a lexical note on 'Anqa' (phoenix) as an example of a universal with no existing individuals; it is a mythical bird known by name but not seen.

Necessary Being as Universal: The First Glance Rebuttal
Author rebuts the claim that the Necessary Being is a particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the concept of the Necessary Being is considered a universal despite having only one possible individual in reality, using the distinction between the first glance and correct reason.

Core Idea
The Necessary Being is a universal in concept because the intellect's first glance permits multiple possibilities.

In classical logic, a universal (kulli) must be mentally applicable to multiple individuals. An objection claims the concept of the Necessary Being (واجب الوجود) cannot be a universal because reason deems it impossible to apply to more than one existent. The author refutes this by introducing a key distinction: the intellect's initial, pre-reflective glance (النظرة الأولى) allows multiple individuals, even if correct reason later restricts it to one. This lesson unpacks that rebuttal and its logical foundations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
Locate the claim that the concept of the Necessary Being is a particular (جزئي) because it cannot apply to many individuals. The objector argues it therefore cannot be a division of the universal (كلي).
This objection challenges the classification in the five universals.
2
Understand the First Glance
The author introduces 'al-nazrat al-ula' (the first glance) – a pre-reflective intellectual permission that allows possibilities even if correct reason later denies them. Without it, shirk would not have arisen and prophets would not have been sent.
Also called 'the foolish glance' (النظرة الحمقاء).
3
Distinguish Two Meanings of Impossibility
The author clarifies that 'intellectual impossibility' can mean: (1) impossibility by both correct reason and the first glance, or (2) impossibility by correct reason only. The author intends the first meaning when stating 'its individuals are impossible', so the concept remains possible in the sense that includes the first glance.
This allows the concept to be classified under 'possible individuals'.
4
Analyze the Logical Form
The statement 'its individuals are impossible' is a universal affirmative due to the genitive construction (imtana'at afraduhu). Negating it with 'or possible' can yield either a universal or partial negative, leaving room for the Necessary Being as a universal with a single actual individual.
This shows how logical operators affect classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله او امتناعه كمفهوم واجب الوجود و بما عرفت من معنى تجويز العقل لايرد ما قيل: من ان مفهوم الواجب الوجود جزئى، لانه يمتنع عند العقل صدقه كثيرين، فلا يصح عده من اقسام الكلى فانه و ان كان العقل لا يحكم بالنظر الصحيح على صدقه على اكثر من فرد واحد، الا انه يجوزه بالنظرة الاولى التى تسمى بالنظرة الحمقاء والا لما اتخذ الشرك كثير من الاشقياء سبيلاً ولما افتقرنا في ابطاله الى ان نأتى دليلاً و لما احتيج إلى ان يرسل الله تعالى اليهم رسولاً، هذا مارى و قد اوردهنا ان مفهوم الواجب الوجود لا يصح عده من اقسام الممكنة الأفراد لان الممكن منه فرد واحد لا افراد متعددة فان المراد من الامتناع والامكان فى قوله امتنعت افراده او امکنت» هو الخارجيان والا لم يصح انقسام الكلى اليهما، لأنه يجب ان يكون الكلى ممكن الافراد بحسب الذهن والا لم يصح التقابل بينه و بين الجزئى كما هو ظاهر. والجواب : ان الامتناع العقلى ايضاً معنيين: الاول : الامتناع بالنظرة الصحيحة والنظرة الأولى معاً. والثاني الامتناع بالنظرة الصحيحة فقط والمصنف حيث قال: امتنعت افراده او امكنت اراد بالامتناع المعنى الاول وبالامكان ما يقابله فيشمل الامكان بهذا المعنى الامتناع بالمعنى الثاني ضرورة ان انتفاء المركب كما يكون بانتفاء جميع الاجزاء يكون بانتفاء احدها ايضاً ولا شك أن مفهوم الواجب الوجود داخل في اقسام الممكنة الافراد بهذا المعنى فتأمل فانه دقيق. وقد أجيب بان المراد من الافراد جنس الفرد واطلاق الجنس على الواحد والكثير كثير. و اقول : هذا فى الجمع المحلى باللام ظاهر واما في الجمع المضاف كما هنا فلا ، فتأمل. وقد اجيب ايضاً بان :قوله امتنعت افراده موجبة كلبة فان الاضافة تفيد الاستغراق حيث لا عهد فيكون قوله: (أو امكنت» رفعاً للايجاب الكلى ورفع الايجاب الكلى كما يحتمل السلب الكلى يحتمل السلب الجزئى ايضاً فافهم. (محمد على)
His statement: 'or its impossibility like the concept of Necessary Being.' And with what you know of the meaning of the intellect's permission, the objection is not valid that the concept of Necessary Being is particular because the intellect deems it impossible to apply to many, so it cannot be counted among the divisions of the universal. For even if the intellect does not judge by correct reasoning that it applies to more than one individual, it permits it by the first glance called the foolish glance; otherwise many wretched ones would not have taken shirk as a way, nor would we need to bring a proof to refute it, nor would God have needed to send a messenger to them. This is my view, and it is mentioned here that the concept of Necessary Being cannot be counted among the divisions of possible individuals because the possible from it is one individual, not multiple individuals. For the intended meaning of impossibility and possibility in his statement 'its individuals are impossible or possible' is external impossibility and possibility; otherwise the division of the universal into them would not be sound, because the universal must be possible of individuals mentally, otherwise the contrast between it and the particular would not be sound, as is apparent. The answer: Intellectual impossibility also has two meanings: first, impossibility by both correct reason and the first glance together; second, impossibility by correct reason only. The author, when he said 'its individuals are impossible or possible', meant by impossibility the first meaning and by possibility its opposite, so possibility in this sense includes impossibility in the second sense, necessarily because the negation of a composite can be by negation of all parts or by negation of one of them. There is no doubt that the concept of Necessary Being is included in the divisions of possible individuals in this sense, so reflect for it is subtle. Another answer: the intended meaning of 'individuals' is the genus of individual, and applying the genus to one and many is common. I say: This is apparent in the plural with definite article, but in the plural as genitive as here, it is not, so reflect. Another answer: His statement 'its individuals are impossible' is a universal affirmative, for the genitive construction implies universal scope when there is no covenant, so his statement 'or possible' is a negation of the universal affirmative, and negation of a universal affirmative can be either a universal negative or a partial negative, so understand. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage defends the inclusion of the concept of the Necessary Being (واجب الوجود) among universals with possible individuals. The objection claims it is particular because reason deems it impossible for more than one existent. The rebuttal argues that the intellect's initial glance (النظرة الأولى) allows multiple individuals; otherwise, shirk would not have arisen, and prophets would not have been needed. Further, the author clarifies that in the division of universals, 'impossibility' and 'possibility' refer to external existence, but mental possibility still holds for the universal itself. The first answer distinguishes two senses of intellectual impossibility: one includes both correct and first-glance judgment, the other only correct. The author intends the first, so the Necessary Being is possible in that sense. A second answer reinterprets 'individuals' as the genus of individual, which can apply to one or many. The commentator critiques that this works for 'al-afrad' but not for 'afraduhu' (genitive). A third answer analyzes the logical form: 'imtana'at afraduhu' is a universal affirmative, and 'amkanat' negates that, which can be a universal or partial negative, leaving room for the Necessary Being as a possible universal with one individual.

Key Terms
النظرة الأولى
الامتناع العقلي

D) Because the Necessary Being is not a concept at all.

Key Takeaway
The concept of the Necessary Being is a universal because the intellect's pre-reflective glance permits multiple individuals, and the classification relies on the first sense of impossibility that includes both correct and first-glance judgment.

Infinity of Individuals in Universals
Clarifying infinity of individuals for universals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the sense in which a universal can have infinite possible individuals, using the examples of rational souls and God's knowledge.

Core Idea
Infinity of individuals means no last in a series, not all existing at once.

In the study of universals, some universals have individuals that could be infinite in number. But what does 'infinite' mean here? This passage clarifies that for a universal like 'rational soul,' the individuals are not all existing simultaneously, but rather are generated endlessly in time. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the concept of 'kulli' in Islamic philosophy and theology, especially in debates about the eternity of the world and divine omniscience.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two examples
Find the two universals mentioned as having infinite possible individuals: rational souls (النفوس الناطقة) and things known by God (معلومات الباري).
2
Understand the philosophers' claim about rational souls
The philosophers believe the world is eternal with no beginning or end, so rational souls are generated endlessly. This is the basis for claiming they are infinite in number.
3
Distinguish two senses of infinity
Note the author's clarification: the infinity is not that all souls exist at once (which would be impossible), but that there is no last soul; each soul is followed by another ad infinitum.
This is a potential infinity, like the natural numbers.
4
Apply the analogy of numbers
The author compares the series of souls to numbers: no matter how far you go, there is always a next number. Apply this to the example of God's knowledge: His knowledge encompasses all possibilities, which are infinite in this sense.
5
Check the commentators' usage
Notice that the commentator (المحشى) used the word 'individuals' for rational souls, showing it as an example of the universal. Others explicitly say 'like the rational souls' as an example of existing individuals.
This reinforces that the universal has many individuals.

( محمد على)
His statement: 'like the things known by God' meaning that the knowledge emanations of God are unlimited in number, and likewise the rational souls are uncounted according to the philosophers, because they are eternal, as they say, and its verification is in the section on the soul from philosophy (al-Taqrib p. 25). (11) For they said: the world is eternal, has no beginning, and whatever has no beginning has no end, so the world according to them has no beginning and no end. Thus the rational souls are infinite in number according to them, not meaning that they fall under existence at once infinitely such that it would be objected that what existence is added to must be finite, but rather in the sense that they do not reach a limit beyond which there is no rational soul, but whenever a rational soul exists, another rational soul also exists after it, ad infinitum, like numbers: they do not reach a limit beyond which there is no number, but every rank reached can be reached beyond it endlessly. And likewise for the things known by God, exalted. Then know that the statement of the commentator (may God have mercy on him) is apparent in that the rational soul is an example of the universal where he used the word 'individuals', while the statement of some: 'like the rational souls' is explicit in it being an example of existing individuals of the universal. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage provides examples of universals whose individuals are possible but not limited in number. The first example is the things known by God (معلومات الباري): the objects of God's knowledge are unlimited. The second is rational souls (النفوس الناطقة) according to the philosophers, who hold that the world is eternal and thus rational souls are generated endlessly. The commentator clarifies that the infinity is not in the sense of all existing simultaneously, but in the sense of an endless succession: whenever a soul exists, another follows, like the natural numbers. He also notes that the commentator (المحشى) used the term 'individuals' for rational souls, showing it as an example of the universal, while others explicitly say 'like rational souls' as an example of many existing individuals of that universal.

Key Terms
النفوس الناطقة (al-nufus al-natiqa): Rational souls; according to philosophers, they are eternal and infinite in number in the sense of temporal succession. [Note: In later Islamic philosophy, the rational soul is usually one per person, but here it's a universal with many individuals.]
معلومات الباري (ma'lumat al-Bari): Things known by God; infinite in number because God's knowledge encompasses all possibilities. [Note: Stable in theology; used here as an example of a universal with possible individuals.]

D) They are finite but beyond human count.

Key Takeaway
The infinity of a universal's individuals is often a potential infinity (endless succession) rather than an actual infinite set existing simultaneously.

Why the Four Relations Apply Only to Two Universals
Commentator explains why the text restricts the four relations to two universals rather than any two concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'two universals' and 'two concepts' in Islamic logic and identify which pairs of concepts can exhibit which of the four relations.

Core Idea
Not every pair of concepts can exhibit all four relations; only two universals do.

In this passage, the commentator explains a crucial terminological precision: why the logician specifies 'two universals' when discussing the four relations (equality, complete difference, absolute generality, and partial generality). If the text had said 'two concepts', one might mistakenly think all relations apply to any pair. The commentator walks through the three possible types of concept pairs to show why only two universals can accommodate all four relations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the distinction
Understand that 'two concepts' is a broader term that includes any pair of concepts, while 'two universals' restricts to concepts that can be predicated of many individuals. The commentator aims to justify this restriction.
Note the Arabic terms: الكليان (two universals) vs المفهومان (two concepts).
2
List the three types of concept pairs
The commentator identifies three possibilities: both universals, both particulars, or one universal and one particular. Write them down and note the Arabic: كليان, جزئيان, كلي وجزئي.
3
Determine applicable relations for each type
For two particulars: only complete difference (التباين) is possible. For a universal and a particular: either complete difference (if the particular is not an instance of the universal) or absolute generality (if the particular is an instance, the universal is more general). For two universals: all four relations (equality, complete difference, absolute generality/specificity, partial generality) can hold.
Remember that 'absolute' means one completely includes the other; 'partial' means overlapping.
4
Conclude why the text uses 'two universals'
Since only two universals can exhibit all four relations, using 'two universals' avoids the false implication that all relations apply to every pair. The term is precise and correct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن كلام المحشى (ره) ظاهر في أن النفس الناطقة مثال الكلي حيث أتى بلفظ الأفراد. وأما قول بعضهم: وكالنفوس الناطقة، فهو نص في كونه مثالا للأفراد الموجودة من الكلي. (محمد علي) (١٢) قوله: أي كل كليين يعني أن السلام في قوله: (والكليان) للاستغراق، فيعم جميع الكليات وفيه أن الكلام إنما في الكليات التي لها مصداق في الخارج كما سنشير. ثم إنما اعتبر النسب الأربع بين الكليين ولم يعتبر بين المفهومين، لأن المفهومين إما كليان أو جزئيان أو كلي وجزئي، فلو قال: "المفهومان" لربما يتوهم جريان جميع أقسام النسب في كل واحد من الأقسام الثلاثة وليس كذلك بل إنما يتحقق بين الكليين بمعنى أنه يوجد كليان مخصوصان بينهما تبايناً وكليان آخران بينهما تساو وهكذا. وأما الجزئيان فلا يتحقق بينهما إلا التباين وأما الجزئي والكلي فلا يتحقق بينهما إلا التباين والعموم مطلقا، لأن الجزئي إن كان جزئياً لهذا الكلي فيكون أخص منه مطلقا وإن لم يكن جزئياً له فيكون مبايناً، فلما قال الكليان علم أن ليس حال القسمين الآخرين كذلك هكذا يستفاد من كلام بعضهم.
Then know that the statement of the commentator (may God have mercy on him) is apparent that the rational soul is an example of the universal where he used the term 'individuals'. As for the statement of some: 'and like the rational souls', it is explicit in being an example of the individuals that exist from the universal. (Muhammad Ali) (12) His statement: 'meaning every two universals' means that the word 'the two universals' in his statement is for generality, so it includes all universals. And in it [the statement] that the discussion is only about universals that have an instance in the external, as we will indicate. Then he only considered the four relations between the two universals and did not consider between the two concepts, because the two concepts are either two universals, or two particulars, or a universal and a particular. If he said 'the two concepts', it might be mistakenly thought that all types of relations apply to each of the three divisions, but it is not so. Rather, they are realized only between two universals, meaning that there are two specific universals between which there is complete difference, and two other universals between which there is equality, etc. As for two particulars, only complete difference is realized between them. As for a particular and a universal, only complete difference and absolute generality are realized between them, because if the particular is a particular of this universal, then it is absolutely more specific; if it is not a particular of it, then it is completely different. So when he said 'the two universals', it is known that the case of the other two divisions is not like that. Thus is derived from the statement of some of them.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a clarification of why the text specifies 'two universals' rather than 'two concepts' when discussing the four relations (equality, complete difference, absolute generality/specificity, and partial generality). The commentator first notes that the rational soul is an example of a universal having individuals. Then the gloss (12) explains that the expression 'the two universals' is inclusive of all universals, but only those with external instances matter. The reason for restricting to universals is that concepts can be of three types: both universals, both particulars, or one universal and one particular. If the text had said 'two concepts', one might wrongly think that all four relations apply to every pair, but they do not. For two particulars, only complete difference (tabayun) is possible, because they are distinct individuals. For a universal and a particular, only complete difference or absolute generality/specificity is possible: if the particular falls under the universal, it is more specific; otherwise they are completely different. Therefore, the four relations are properly defined only between two universals, as the text correctly states. This is a parsing exercise because the student must understand the terminological distinction between concepts and universals and the logical relations.

المفهومان

The four relations (equality, complete difference, absolute generality, partial generality) are properly considered only between two universals because only such pairs can exhibit all four relations without exception.

The Relation of 'Umum min Wajh
Author defines 'umum min wajh with example of animal and white.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the relation of 'umum min wajh between two universals and provide an Islamic science example.

Core Idea
'Umum min wajh: each universal includes the other and also includes others — reciprocal overlap.

Among the four relations between universals in logic, 'umum min wajh (general from one side) is a subtle but crucial relation. Here, each universal is both more general and more specific than the other, depending on the aspect considered. This lesson unpacks this definition with the classic example of 'animal' and 'white', as explained by the commentators.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
The passage states: each of the two universals is more general from one aspect (it includes the other and other things) and more specific from another aspect (it is included by the other, while still including other things). Grasp this reciprocal inclusion relationship.
2
Visualize with the example
Consider 'animal' and 'white'. Animal includes white (e.g., a white horse) and non-white (e.g., a black cat). White includes animal (any white animal) and non-animal (e.g., a white cloth). Thus each includes the other and includes other things, from different perspectives.
This visualization clarifies the 'one aspect' condition.
3
Recognize the naming logic
Because each universal is simultaneously more general (includes the other plus others) and more specific (included by the other) but from different aspects, the relation is aptly named 'umum min wajh (general from one side).
The name captures the asymmetry in the symmetry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في النسب الأربع بحسب الطاقة البشرية والاغراض المطلوبة من الفن ولاغرض لهم في الكليات الفرضية (عبد الرحيم) (١٥) اى كل واحد منهما اعم من وجه و هو كونه شاملاً له ولغيره في الجملة و اخص من وجه وهو كونه مشمولاً للاخر مع انه شامل لغيره ايضاً. (محمد على). (١٦) الحيوان شامل للابيض وغيره وبالعكس فباعتبار ان كل واحد منهما شامل للآخر، يكون اعم منه وباعتبار انه مشمول له اخص منه و لهذا سمى بالاعم والاخص من وجه . شيخ عبد الرحيم).
Regarding the four relations according to human capacity and the desired objectives of the art, and they have no purpose concerning hypothetical universals. (Abd al-Rahim) (15) i.e. each one of them is more general from one aspect, meaning it includes it and others in general, and more specific from one aspect, meaning it is included by the other while also including others. (Muhammad Ali) (16) Animal includes white and others and vice versa. So considering that each one includes the other, it is more general than it, and considering that it is included by it, it is more specific than it. Hence it is called the more general and more specific from one aspect. (Shaykh Abd al-Rahim).
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage defines the relation of 'umum min wajh (general from one side) among the four relations between universals. The author first notes that these relations are discussed according to human capacity and the purposes of the art, not for hypothetical universals. He then explains that each of the two universals (e.g., animal and white) is more general from one aspect because it includes the other and others, and more specific from another aspect because it is included by the other while also including things other than it. The example clarifies: animal includes white and other colors; white includes animal (since white is a quality found in animals) and other white things. Thus each is both more general and more specific relative to the other, hence the name 'umum min wajh.

'Umum min wajh describes a reciprocal relation where two universals partially overlap, each being simultaneously more general and more specific than the other from different perspectives.

Three Differences Between Whole and Universal
Author lists three differences between whole and universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given term is a whole (kull) or universal (kullī) by applying the three criteria of constitution, finitude, and predication.

Core Idea
The whole constitutes its parts; the universal is predicated of its particulars.

This passage from Yazdi’s Hashiya lays out three crisp differences between the whole (kull) and the universal (kullī). Although both terms involve multiplicity, they operate in distinct logical domains. Mastering these differences will sharpen your ability to analyse definitions and avoid category confusions in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Extract the three differences
Read the Arabic passage and list the three criteria: (1) the whole constitutes its parts, the universal does not; (2) the parts of the whole are finite, the particulars of the universal infinite; (3) the whole is not predicated of its parts, the universal is predicated of its particulars.
The third difference is illustrated by the examples 'al-baytu jidār' (false) and 'al-insānu Zaydun' (true).
2
Apply the first criterion (constitution)
Check whether the term in question is a composite whose parts are constitutive. For example, 'house' is a whole because walls, roof, etc. are its parts; 'man' is a universal because individuals are not constitutive parts of the concept 'man'.
A universal does not constitute its individuals; it only applies to them.
3
Apply the second criterion (finitude/infinitude)
Determine whether the parts/particulars are finite or infinite. A house has a limited number of parts, but the universal 'man' can apply to infinitely many individuals (past, present, future).
This difference relates to potential infinity; in reality, the number of existing humans is finite.
4
Apply the third criterion (predication)
Test whether the term can be truly predicated of its parts/particulars. You cannot say 'the house is a wall', but you can say 'Zaid is a man' (or in the passage, 'man is Zaid' as a reverse predication for illustration).
Predication here means the universal can be said of each individual; the whole cannot be said of its parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرق بين الكل والكلى بوجوه منها : ان الكل مقوم الجزئياته ومنها : ان اجزاء الكل متناهية و جزئيات الكلى غير متناهية ومنها: ان الكل لا يحمل على اجزائه والكلى يحمل على جزئياته فلا يقال: «البيت جدار ويقال: «الانسان زيد».
The difference between the whole (kull) and the universal (kulli) is from several aspects: (1) the whole constitutes its parts; (2) the parts of the whole are finite, while the particulars of the universal are infinite; (3) the whole is not predicated of its parts, while the universal is predicated of its particulars. Thus one does not say 'the house is a wall' but one says 'man is Zaid'.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage presents three distinctions between the whole (kull) and the universal (kulli). First, the whole is constitutive of its parts: e.g., the house is a whole made up of walls, roof, etc., and the whole is what gives the parts their identity as parts. The universal, by contrast, is not constitutive but is a concept that applies to its individuals. Second, the parts of a whole are finite in number (a house has a limited number of parts), while the particulars of a universal are potentially infinite (e.g., 'man' can apply to an infinite number of individuals). Third, the whole is not predicated of its parts: we do not say 'the house is a wall' because the whole is not a part. But the universal is predicated of its particulars: we say 'Zaid is a man' or 'man is Zaid' (the latter is an example of predication in the sense of identity or classification). The examples illustrate: 'al-baytu jidaar' (the house is a wall) is false, while 'al-insaanu Zaidun' (man is Zaid) is true in the sense that Zaid is a man, or in the context of specifying an individual.

Key Terms
الكل
الكلى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The whole constitutes its parts; the universal does not constitute its particulars.
B) The parts of the whole are finite; the particulars of the universal are infinite.
C) The whole is predicated of its parts; the universal is not predicated of its particulars.
D) The whole is not predicated of its parts; the universal is predicated of its particulars.

Key Takeaway
The whole (kull) is a composite with finite, constitutive parts and is not predicated of them; the universal (kullī) is a concept with potentially infinite particulars and is predicated of them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Why Negations of Universal Relations are Not a Fifth Relation
Explanation of why partial exclusivity is not a fifth relation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the negations of al-'umum min wajh and al-tabayun al-kulli exhibit partial exclusivity rather than constituting a fifth independent relation.

Core Idea
Partial exclusivity is already present within each relation; it cannot be a fifth type.

When logicians analyze universal relations like 'generality from one aspect' and 'total exclusivity', they also consider the relations between the negations of those terms. The commentator of this gloss clarifies why the relationship between these negations is not an independent fifth relation, but rather partial exclusivity. Understanding this helps us see how logical classifications are kept consistent and universal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two universal relations involved
The passage discusses two relations: 'generality from one aspect' (al-'umum min wajh) and 'total exclusivity' (al-tabayun al-kulli). Review what each means: the first involves partial overlap, the second no overlap at all.
These are two of the four universal relations in classical logic.
2
Understand the logical structure of 'generality from one aspect'
The author explains that this relation resolves into one particular affirmative and two particular negatives. For example, 'Some animal is not human, some animal is non-human, some non-human is not animal'. This shows overlap and separation.
This structure is key to seeing why its negation is not a simple universal.
3
Recognize that partial exclusivity appears in both relations
Partial exclusivity means that two terms are not mutually exclusive in all cases. The passage states that this occurs within both 'generality from one aspect' and 'total exclusivity', so it is not unique to the negations.
If it appears in both, it cannot be a separate fifth relation.
4
Follow the logical reasoning for the naming
The logicians said the negations have 'partial exclusivity' (tabayun juz'i) rather than 'generality from one aspect' or 'total exclusivity'. Why? Because if they said any of the latter, it would not hold in all cases. Partial exclusivity is the only description that applies universally to the negations.
This ensures the classification is sound for every instance.
5
See the rhetorical defense (inverted comparison)
A possible objection is that comparing the negations of 'generality from one aspect' to those of 'total exclusivity' requires the point of similarity to be known. The commentator rebuts by invoking inverted comparison (tashbih maqlub), where the purpose is to emphasize the compared-to, not to rely on prior knowledge.
This defense shows how logic can use rhetorical tools to clarify relationships.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاجاب بقوله : التباين الجزئى يتحقق فى ضمن العموم من وجه وفي ضمن التباين الكلى ايضاً فليس مستقلاً حتى يقال : نسبة خامسة.
(۳۷) لان مرجع العموم من وجه الى موجبة جزئية وسالبتين جزئيتين وهما قولنا بعض اللاحيوان لا أبيض كالحجر الاسود وبعض اللاحيوان ليس بلا ابيض كالثلج وبعض اللا أبيض ليس بلا حيوان كالفرس الاسود.
(۳۸) بعض الحيوان لا انسان كالفرس وهو مادة الاجتماع وبعض الحيوان ليس بلا انسان مثل زيد وبعض اللانسان ليس بحيوان مثل الشجر و هو مادة الافتراق.
(۳۹) مثل : لاشيء من اللاحيوان بلا انسان ولاشيء من اللاانسان بلاحيوان.
(۴۰) ای ولاجل ان نقيضي الاعم والاخص من وجه قد يكون بينهما عموم من وجه وقد يكون تباين کلي قالوا: ان بين نقيضيها تبايناً جزئياً ولم يقولوا: ان بينها عموماً من وجه اوتبايناً كلياً ليصح في الكل ذلك . ( محمد على)
(۴۱) يعنى ان الاحكام الموردة في هذا الفن كليات فلو قيل ان بين نقيضى الاعم والاخص من وجه عموماً من وجه لاقاد العموم من وجه في جميع الصور وليس كذلك كما قرره المحشى و هكذا الحال في التباين الكلى فلهذا قالوا ان بين نقيضيهما تبايناً جزئياً حتى يصح في الكل.
(۴۲) قوله اى كما ان بين نقيضى الاعم.... الغرض من هذا الكلام دفع ما ربما يتوهم في المقام من ان التشبية يستدعى ان يكون وجه الشبه فى المشبه به معلوماً كما هو الظاهر من قولنا : «زيد کالاسد و امثاله و هنا ليس بمعلوم، فانه لم يعلم قبل ان بين نقيضى المتباينين تبايناً جزئياً أم لا، تشبيه نقيضى الاعم و الاخص من وجه بها؟ فكيف يصح و حاصل الدفع ان الغرض من التشبيه كما ذكره البيانيون قد يعود الى المشبه وقد يعود الى المشبه به و ما ذكر انما يجب في القسم الأول وما نحن فيه من القسم الثانى وليس يجب فيه ان يكون وجه الشبه فى المشبه به معلوماً قبل فالتشبيه فيه كقوله: ويد الصباح كان غرته وجه الخليفة حين يمتدح. هذا حيث شبه غرة الصباح فى الوضوح و الضياء بوجه الخليفة ولم يكن اتصافه به معلوماً قبل، و يسمى التشبيه التشبيه المقلوب فان المشبه فى الحقيقة فى البيت وجه الخليفة و فيها نحن فيه نقيضا المتباينين لكنه قلب و عكس قصداً الى ادعاء انه زايد فتأمل . (محمد علی)
He replied by saying: Partial exclusivity occurs within generality from one aspect and also within total exclusivity, so it is not independent that it could be called a fifth relation. (37) Because the reference of generality from one aspect is to a particular affirmative and two particular negatives, which are our statements: Some non-animal is not white, like black stone; Some non-animal is not not-white, like snow; Some not-white is not non-animal, like black horse. (38) Some animal is not human, like a horse—that is the matter of overlap; Some animal is not non-human, like Zayd; Some non-human is not animal, like a tree—that is the matter of separation. (39) Example: Nothing of non-animal is non-human, and nothing of non-human is non-animal. (40) That is, because the negations of the more general and more specific from one aspect may have between them generality from one aspect and may have total exclusivity, they said: Between their negations there is partial exclusivity, and they did not say: Between them there is generality from one aspect or total exclusivity, so that it would be correct in all cases. (Muhammad Ali) (41) Meaning: The rulings in this art are universal. So if it were said that between the negations of the more general and more specific from one aspect there is generality from one aspect, it would necessitate generality from one aspect in all cases, but it is not so, as the commentator has established. Similarly for total exclusivity. Therefore they said that between their two negations there is partial exclusivity, so that it is correct in all cases. (42) His statement: i.e. just as between the negations of... The purpose of this speech is to repel what might be imagined in this context, that the comparison requires that the point of similarity in the compared-to be known, as is apparent from our statement 'Zayd is like a lion' and similar examples, and here it is not known. For it is not known before whether between the negations of the two totally exclusive terms there is partial exclusivity or not. [One might ask:] How can the comparison of the negations of the more general and more specific from one aspect to them be valid? And the summary of the rebuttal is that the purpose of the comparison, as the rhetoricians have mentioned, may relate to the compared item or to the compared-to. What was mentioned is necessary only in the first type, and our case is of the second type. In it, it is not necessary that the point of similarity in the compared-to be known beforehand. An example of such a comparison is his saying: 'The hand of the morning, as if its white streak were the face of the caliph when he is praised.' Here, he compared the white streak of the morning in clarity and brightness to the face of the caliph, even though his description with that was not known beforehand. This is called inverted comparison. For in the verse, the compared item is actually the face of the caliph, and in our case it is the two negations of the totally exclusive terms. But he inverted and reversed deliberately to claim that it is additional. So reflect. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is a gloss on a previous statement regarding the relationships between the negations of two types of universal relations: 'general from one aspect' (al-a'am wa al-akhass min wajh) and 'totally exclusive' (al-mutabayinan). The commentator defends why the logicians called the relationship between these negations 'partial exclusivity' (tabayun juz'i) instead of making it a fifth relation or labeling it as total exclusivity or generality from one aspect.

The passage begins by stating that partial exclusivity is found within both generality from one aspect and total exclusivity, so it is not an independent fifth relation. It then explains that 'generality from one aspect' resolves into a particular affirmative and two particular negatives, giving examples from 'animal' and 'white' to illustrate overlap and separation.

Next, it shows via example that the negations of two items that are mutually exclusive can sometimes be partial exclusivity (e.g., 'non-human' and 'non-stone'). The commentator clarifies that the logicians intentionally said 'partial exclusivity' to ensure the statement holds universally: if they had said 'generality from one aspect' or 'total exclusivity', it would not apply to all cases.

The remainder (42) addresses an objection about the validity of comparing the negations of 'general from one aspect' to the negations of totally exclusive terms, since the point of similarity might not be known beforehand. The rebuttal, drawing on rhetoric, explains that the comparison is inverted (tashbih maqlub): the purpose is to emphasize a quality in the compared-to, not to rely on a known similarity. An example from poetry illustrates this.

Key Terms
التباين الجزئي
العموم من وجه
التباين الكلي
نقيض
تشبيه مقلوب

A) Because partial exclusivity is found within both generality from one aspect and total exclusivity, so it is not independent.
B) Because they made a mistake and later corrected it.
C) Because partial exclusivity is the same as total exclusivity.
D) Because the negations are always totally exclusive.

Key Takeaway
Partial exclusivity is not a fifth universal relation; it is a descriptor that accurately applies to the negations of both 'generality from one aspect' and 'total exclusivity' in all cases.

Complete vs. Partial Opposition Among Contradictories
Author explains the two types of opposition among contradictories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between complete opposition (al-tabāyun al-kullī) and partial opposition (al-tabāyun al-juz'ī) among contradictories of universal terms, and correctly classify examples.

Core Idea
Contradictory terms can relate as complete opposites (never coexist) or partial opposites (sometimes coexist).

In this passage, the hashya author unpacks the behavior of the contradictories (naqīḍān) of universal terms. He explains that not all contradictories are like 'existent' and 'non-existent', which never meet. Some, like 'non-human' and 'non-stone', can apply to the same thing (e.g., cloth). This distinction is vital for understanding the four proportional relationships in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two original universals
The passage uses 'human' and 'stone' as original terms. Their contradictories are 'non-human' and 'non-stone'.
2
Recognize the two cases of contradictory behavior
The author says that in general, one contradictory is true without the other, but this resolves into two specific cases: complete opposition and partial opposition.
3
Understand complete opposition (tabāyun kullī)
In complete opposition, the two contradictories never occur together. Example: 'non-existent' and 'non-non-existent' (i.e., existent) – they cannot both apply to the same thing.
4
Understand partial opposition (tabāyun juz'ī)
In partial opposition, the two contradictories can sometimes be true together, sometimes not. Example: 'non-human' and 'non-stone' – they both apply to cloth (cloth is neither human nor stone), but only one applies to stone (stone is non-human but not non-stone because it is stone).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللاحجر يصح TTV في النسب الاربع عين الآخر (بالملازمة، فان الانسان مع ان يقال فيه احد العينين مع نقيض الآخر واحد النقيضين مع عين الآخر و كذلك الحجر مع اللاانسان كما هو واضح فيصدق كل من النقيضين اللاانسان واللاحجر بدون صاحبه في الجملة و ينحل هذا الاجمال الى ان لا يصدق احد النقيضين مع الآخر اصلا كما فى النقيضين المتباينين مثل اللاموجود واللامعدوم او يصدق في قضية جزئية كما في النقيضين اللذين بينهما عموم من وجه مثل اللا انسان واللاحجر فيجوز ان يقال: يصدق اللاانسان بدون اللا حجر كما اذا روعى الحجر فانه لا انسان وليس لا حجراً ويجوز ان يصدق اللاانسان مع اللاحجر كما فى القماش فانه لا انسان ولا حجر ايضاً و هذا هو التباين الجزئى وقد شرحناه آنفاً وكررناه شرحاً لمقصود الشارح ومزيداً للتوضيح التقريب ص۲۸) (۴۴) مثل لا انسان يصدق على الحجر دون لاحجر و كذا لاحجر يصدق على الانسان دون لا انسان. (۴۵) مثل: لاشيء من اللاموجود بلا معدوم ولاشيء من اللامعدوم بلا موجود.
The non-stone is valid TTV in the four relationships: the other of the two [original] terms (by implication). For example, 'human' – it is said of it one of the two [original] terms together with the contradictory of the other, and one of the two contradictories together with the other of the two [original] terms. Likewise 'stone' with 'non-human', as is clear. So each of the two contradictories 'non-human' and 'non-stone' is true without its companion in a general sense. This summary resolves into: one of the two contradictories is never true with the other at all, as with the two completely opposed contradictories like 'non-existent' and 'non-non-existent'; or it is true in a particular proposition, as with the two contradictories that have generality from one aspect like 'non-human' and 'non-stone'. So it is permissible to say: 'non-human' is true without 'non-stone', as when stone is considered, for it is not human and not non-stone; and 'non-human' is true together with 'non-stone', as with cloth, for it is not human and also not stone. This is partial opposition, which we have explained previously and repeated as an explanation of the commentator's intent and for further clarification, approximately p. 28. (44) Example: 'non-human' is true of stone without 'non-stone', and likewise 'non-stone' is true of human without 'non-human'. (45) Example: Nothing of the non-existent is non-non-existent, and nothing of the non-non-existent is non-existent.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage from the hashya expands on the commentator's explanation of the four proportional relationships between universals. The author clarifies the behavior of the contradictories (نقيضان) of the three pairs that remain after specifying the two original universals. He states that the contradictories of one original and the other original's contradictory can be true without each other in general, but this general statement resolves into two cases: complete opposition (تباين كلي) where the two contradictories never coexist (e.g., 'non-existent' and 'non-non-existent'), and partial opposition (تباين جزئي) where they can coexist in some cases (e.g., 'non-human' and 'non-stone'). In partial opposition, each may be true without the other (as 'non-human' is true of stone without 'non-stone') or both may be true together (as with cloth, which is neither human nor stone). Examples (44) and (45) illustrate these cases: (44) shows partial opposition between 'non-human' and 'non-stone'; (45) shows complete opposition between 'non-existent' and 'non-non-existent'. The author emphasizes that this explanation repeats the commentator's intent and provides clarity.

التباين الجزئي
التباين الكلي

C) 'Human' and 'animal'.

Key Takeaway
Contradictories of universals can be either completely opposed (never together) or partially opposed (sometimes together), as illustrated by 'non-existent' and 'non-non-existent' vs. 'non-human' and 'non-stone'.

Defining the Relative Particular: A Verbal or Real Definition?
Explains the definition of the relative particular and critiques defining the wider by the narrower.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the definition of the relative particular and determine whether it is a real or verbal definition based on the commentator's criteria.

Core Idea
The same definition can be real or verbal, and only a real definition carries necessary logical implications.

This lesson unpacks the definition of the relative particular as given by the commentator. We'll examine its components and investigate why the author insists that the definition is verbal, not real, and what difference that makes for the logical relation between the relative particular and the real particular.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definiens
Find the term being defined (al-juz'i al-idafi) and the words that define it: 'it is the universal which another universal is predicated of it universally, and it is not predicated of that other universally.'
2
Extract the two conditions in the definiens
The definition consists of two conjuncts: (1) another universal applies to it universally, and (2) it does not apply to that other universally. Write each condition separately.
3
Determine if this is a real or verbal definition
The commentator argues it is a verbal definition (tafsir lafzi) because it explains the meaning of the term without capturing its essence. A real definition would reveal the essential genus and differentia.
Check whether the definiens could be applied to other things besides the definiendum. If so, it is likely verbal.
4
Assess the consequence of regarding it as real vs. verbal
The earlier commentator concluded that the relative particular is more general than the real particular. But the hashiya shows this follows only if the definition is real; if verbal, the definiens may be broader and no such implication holds.
This step shows the practical impact of the real/verbal distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله فتفسير الجزئى الاضافى بالاخص بهذا المعنى و هو انه الكلى الذي يصدق عليه كلى آخر صدقاً كلياً ولا يصدق هو على ذلك الاخر صدقاً كلياً تفسير ما هو اعم و هو الجزئى الاضافي لصدقه على الكلى وعلى الجزئى الحقيق بالاخص و هو الاخص في باب النسب الذي لا يكون الكلياً. (٥٩) فان الاخص المعلوم سابقاً كما ذكر يجب ان يكون كلياً بخلاف الاخص المذكور هيهنا فانه لا يجب ان يكون كلياً بل كما يكون كلياً يكون جزئياً. (٦٠) قوله و منه يعلم ان الجزئى ....: اى و من كون الاخص المزبور هيهنا اعم من الاخص المذكور آنفا، يعلم ان الجزئى الاضافى أعم من الجزئى الحقيقى وذلك لانه اذا علم ان الاخص المذكور هنا قد يكون كلياً و قد يكون جزئياً حقيقياً و هو تفسير للجزئي الاضافي و عموم المفسر يستلزم عموم المفسر علم ان الجزئى الاضافى قد يكون كلياً و قد يكون جزئياً حقيقياً و هذا معنى قوله : فيعلم بيان النسبة اى بين الجزئيين التزاماً. ولا يخفى: ان هذا انما يصح لوكان هذا تعريفاً حقيقياً و أما اذا كان لفظياً كما هو المراد، ففيه نوع خفاء لظهور ان التعريف اللفظي يجوز ان يكون اعم فلا يلزم من عمومية المفسر - بالكسر عمومية المفسر بالفتح فلا يثبت المطلوب فافهم. (وقال الاستاذ الفاضل الشيخ محمد الکرمی دامت افاداته : و منه يعلم» اى من حال الاخص هنا والاخص هناك يعلم ان الجزئى بهذا المعنى و هو كونه اضافياً حتى جاز صدقه على الكليات اعم من الجزئى الحقيقى، فيعلم بيان النسبة بين الجزئيين بالالتزام من النسبة بين الاخصين هنا وهناك وقد علم ان بينهما عموماً و خصوصاً مطلقاً فيثبت ان بين الجزئيين الحقيقى والاضافي عموماً و خصوصاً مطلقاً ايضاً.)
His statement: 'So the interpretation of the relative particular by the more specific in this sense—that it is the universal which another universal is predicated of it universally, and it is not predicated of that other universally—is an interpretation of what is more general, namely the relative particular, because it is predicated of the universal and of the real particular by the more specific, which is the more specific in the domain of relations that is not universal.' (59) For the more specific known previously, as mentioned, must be universal, unlike the more specific mentioned here, for it does not have to be universal; rather, just as it may be universal, it may be particular. (60) His statement: 'And from it is known that the particular...' means: from the fact that the more specific here is more general than the more specific mentioned earlier, it is known that the relative particular is more general than the real particular. Because if it is known that the more specific mentioned here may be universal and may be real particular—and it is an interpretation of the relative particular—and the generality of the interpreter necessitates the generality of the interpreted, then it is known that the relative particular may be universal and may be real particular. And this is the meaning of his statement: 'so the clarification of the relation between the two particulars is known by implication.' And it is not hidden: this is valid only if this is a real definition, but if it is verbal, as intended, then there is some obscurity because it is obvious that a verbal definition may be more general, so the generality of the interpreter does not necessitate the generality of the interpreted, so the desired conclusion is not established. So understand. (And the excellent teacher, Sheikh Muhammad al-Karmi, may his benefits endure, said: 'And from it is known' i.e., from the state of the more specific here and the more specific there, it is known that the particular in this sense—being relative such that it is permitted to be predicated of universals—is more general than the real particular. Thus the relation between the two particulars is known by implication from the relation between the two more specifics here and there. And it is known that between them there is absolute generality and specificity, so it is established that between the real particular and the relative particular there is also absolute generality and specificity.)
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of a previous commentary on the definition of the relative particular (al-juz’i al-idafi). The author, likely 'Abd al-Rahim, begins by restating the definition: the relative particular is a universal that another universal is predicated of it universally, but it is not predicated of that other universally. This definition interprets the more general (the relative particular) by the more specific (the 'more specific' in the domain of relations). The commentator then notes a nuance: the 'more specific' mentioned here (as the definer) does not have to be universal, unlike the 'more specific' in a previous context. This leads to a discussion about whether the definition is real or verbal. The commentator argues that if the definition were real, the generality of the interpreter (the definer) would imply the generality of the interpreted (the relative particular). But since it is a verbal definition, this implication does not hold, because a verbal definition can be broader than the defined term. Therefore, the conclusion that the relative particular is more general than the real particular is not necessarily established by this logical implication. The passage then includes an additional comment by Sheikh Muhammad al-Karmi, who reaffirms the original point: from comparing the two 'more specifics', one can deduce the relation between the two particulars, but this deduction relies on the definition being real. This hashiya corrects the earlier commentator's presumption, highlighting a subtle distinction in logical definitions.

Key Terms
الجزئي الاضافي
تفسير لفظي
الأخص

Checkpoint

A) The definition must be real (haqiqi)
B) The definition must be verbal (lafzi)
C) The more specific must be a real particular
D) The more specific must be a universal

Key Takeaway
The logical force of a definition depends on whether it is real or verbal; a verbal definition may not imply the broader scope of the defined term.

Real vs Verbal Definitions: The Critical Refinement of the Hashiya
Critically refines the inference about the relative particular's generality by distinguishing real from verbal definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the hashiya's criticism of the earlier commentator's inference and distinguish between real and verbal definitions in logical contexts.

Core Idea
A verbal definition does not force the same logical implications as a real definition—the hashiya corrects an overreach.

In this lesson, we focus on the meta-logical move made by the hashiya author: he identifies that the earlier commentator's inference depends on treating a definition as real, but in fact it is only verbal. We will see how this distinction is central to correct logical reasoning about definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the earlier commentator's claim
The earlier claim (presumably from the base text) is that from the definition of the relative particular by the more specific, it follows that the relative particular is more general than the real particular.
2
Pinpoint the hashiya's objection
The hashiya author states that this inference is valid only if the definition is real (haqiqi). Since the intended definition is verbal (lafzi), the inference does not hold.
3
Understand the reasoning behind the objection
A verbal definition merely explains the meaning of a term; it can be broader or narrower than the term itself. Therefore, the generality of the definiens does not necessitate the generality of the definiendum.
Contrast with a real definition, which captures the essence and thus any property of the definiens necessarily applies to the definiendum.
4
Evaluate the additional comment by Sheikh al-Karmi
Sheikh al-Karmi reaffirms that the relation between the two particulars can be deduced by comparing the 'more specifics', but this again presupposes real definitions. The hashiya's point stands: without that presupposition, the inference is unsound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله فتفسير الجزئى الاضافى بالاخص بهذا المعنى و هو انه الكلى الذي يصدق عليه كلى آخر صدقاً كلياً ولا يصدق هو على ذلك الاخر صدقاً كلياً تفسير ما هو اعم و هو الجزئى الاضافي لصدقه على الكلى وعلى الجزئى الحقيق بالاخص و هو الاخص في باب النسب الذي لا يكون الكلياً. (٥٩) فان الاخص المعلوم سابقاً كما ذكر يجب ان يكون كلياً بخلاف الاخص المذكور هيهنا فانه لا يجب ان يكون كلياً بل كما يكون كلياً يكون جزئياً. (٦٠) قوله و منه يعلم ان الجزئى ....: اى و من كون الاخص المزبور هيهنا اعم من الاخص المذكور آنفا، يعلم ان الجزئى الاضافى أعم من الجزئى الحقيقى وذلك لانه اذا علم ان الاخص المذكور هنا قد يكون كلياً و قد يكون جزئياً حقيقياً و هو تفسير للجزئي الاضافي و عموم المفسر يستلزم عموم المفسر علم ان الجزئى الاضافى قد يكون كلياً و قد يكون جزئياً حقيقياً و هذا معنى قوله : فيعلم بيان النسبة اى بين الجزئيين التزاماً. ولا يخفى: ان هذا انما يصح لوكان هذا تعريفاً حقيقياً و أما اذا كان لفظياً كما هو المراد، ففيه نوع خفاء لظهور ان التعريف اللفظي يجوز ان يكون اعم فلا يلزم من عمومية المفسر - بالكسر عمومية المفسر بالفتح فلا يثبت المطلوب فافهم. (وقال الاستاذ الفاضل الشيخ محمد الکرمی دامت افاداته : و منه يعلم» اى من حال الاخص هنا والاخص هناك يعلم ان الجزئى بهذا المعنى و هو كونه اضافياً حتى جاز صدقه على الكليات اعم من الجزئى الحقيقى، فيعلم بيان النسبة بين الجزئيين بالالتزام من النسبة بين الاخصين هنا وهناك وقد علم ان بينهما عموماً و خصوصاً مطلقاً فيثبت ان بين الجزئيين الحقيقى والاضافي عموماً و خصوصاً مطلقاً ايضاً.)
His statement: 'So the interpretation of the relative particular by the more specific in this sense—that it is the universal which another universal is predicated of it universally, and it is not predicated of that other universally—is an interpretation of what is more general, namely the relative particular, because it is predicated of the universal and of the real particular by the more specific, which is the more specific in the domain of relations that is not universal.' (59) For the more specific known previously, as mentioned, must be universal, unlike the more specific mentioned here, for it does not have to be universal; rather, just as it may be universal, it may be particular. (60) His statement: 'And from it is known that the particular...' means: from the fact that the more specific here is more general than the more specific mentioned earlier, it is known that the relative particular is more general than the real particular. Because if it is known that the more specific mentioned here may be universal and may be real particular—and it is an interpretation of the relative particular—and the generality of the interpreter necessitates the generality of the interpreted, then it is known that the relative particular may be universal and may be real particular. And this is the meaning of his statement: 'so the clarification of the relation between the two particulars is known by implication.' And it is not hidden: this is valid only if this is a real definition, but if it is verbal, as intended, then there is some obscurity because it is obvious that a verbal definition may be more general, so the generality of the interpreter does not necessitate the generality of the interpreted, so the desired conclusion is not established. So understand. (And the excellent teacher, Sheikh Muhammad al-Karmi, may his benefits endure, said: 'And from it is known' i.e., from the state of the more specific here and the more specific there, it is known that the particular in this sense—being relative such that it is permitted to be predicated of universals—is more general than the real particular. Thus the relation between the two particulars is known by implication from the relation between the two more specifics here and there. And it is known that between them there is absolute generality and specificity, so it is established that between the real particular and the relative particular there is also absolute generality and specificity.)
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of a previous commentary on the definition of the relative particular (al-juz’i al-idafi). The author, likely 'Abd al-Rahim, begins by restating the definition: the relative particular is a universal that another universal is predicated of it universally, but it is not predicated of that other universally. This definition interprets the more general (the relative particular) by the more specific (the 'more specific' in the domain of relations). The commentator then notes a nuance: the 'more specific' mentioned here (as the definer) does not have to be universal, unlike the 'more specific' in a previous context. This leads to a discussion about whether the definition is real or verbal. The commentator argues that if the definition were real, the generality of the interpreter (the definer) would imply the generality of the interpreted (the relative particular). But since it is a verbal definition, this implication does not hold, because a verbal definition can be broader than the defined term. Therefore, the conclusion that the relative particular is more general than the real particular is not necessarily established by this logical implication. The passage then includes an additional comment by Sheikh Muhammad al-Karmi, who reaffirms the original point: from comparing the two 'more specifics', one can deduce the relation between the two particulars, but this deduction relies on the definition being real. This hashiya corrects the earlier commentator's presumption, highlighting a subtle distinction in logical definitions.

Key Terms
الجزئي الاضافي
تفسير لفظي
الأخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hashiya, why does the inference that the relative particular is more general than the real particular not hold?
A) Because the more specific is not universal
B) Because the definition is verbal, not real
C) Because the relative particular is not a universal
D) Because the real particular is not a universal

Key Takeaway
A verbal definition does not carry the same logical force as a real definition; one must identify the type of definition before drawing conclusions.

The Five Universals and Their Nomenclature
States that the universals are limited to five types and explains the origin of the term 'Isaghuji'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five universals in Arabic and identify the proposed etymologies of the term 'Isaghuji'.

Core Idea
The five universals are the building blocks of definition; their name 'Isaghuji' has a foreign origin with multiple explanations.

This passage establishes that the universals used in definition are limited to five specific types. It also explores the historical and linguistic origins of the term 'Isaghuji', revealing that the name is not Arabic but derived from Greek or Hebrew with several proposed explanations. Understanding this foundation is key to mastering definition in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the claim of limitation
The passage begins by affirming that the universals are limited to five types. Identify this claim in the first sentence.
This is a foundational statement in Islamic logic; the five are not arbitrary.
2
Extract the three etymological explanations
The passage gives three possible origins for 'Isaghuji': (1) from Greek words meaning 'universal' and 'five', (2) after a philosopher who discovered them, (3) after a student named Isa Ghuji who was addressed by the teacher. List each one.
The author does not endorse any; the variety shows the term's disputed history.
3
Analyze the author's stance
Notice that the author uses 'قيل' (it is said) for each explanation, indicating he is reporting without certifying. This teaches epistemic caution about historical claims in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : منحصرة فى خمسة انواع اعلم ان هذه الانواع الخمسة يقال لها: «الكليات» بالعربية و «الايساغوجى» بالعبرية وقيل باليونانية و هو مركب من «ايسا» ای الکلی و «الفوجی » اي الخمس. وقيل في سبب تسميتها به انه اسم حكيم استخرجها و دونت فسميت باسم مستخرجها و قيل: انما سميت به لان بعض من لان بعض من كان متعلمها شخصاً يسمى بـ «ایسا غوجى» فكان يخاطبه في مسألة منها باسمه ويقول يا ايساغوجي الحال كذا و كذا وقيل : غير ذلك
His statement: 'Limited to five types.' Know that these five types are called 'al-kulliyat' in Arabic, and 'al-Isaghuji' in Hebrew, and it is said in Greek. It is composed of 'Isa' meaning 'universal' and 'al-Fuji' meaning 'five'. And it is said in the reason for its naming: that it is the name of a wise man who extracted them and they were recorded, so they were named after their extractor. And it is said: it was only named that because some of those who studied it was a person called 'Isa Ghuji', so he would address him in a question about it by his name, saying 'O Isaghuji, the situation is such and such.' And it is said: other than that.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage comments on the statement that the universals are limited to five types. It explains that these five types are known as 'the universals' in Arabic and as 'Isaghuji' in other languages, including Hebrew and Greek. The name 'Isaghuji' is analyzed as a compound of 'Isa' meaning 'universal' and 'Fuji' meaning 'five'. Then several explanations are given for why they are called 'Isaghuji': (1) It is the name of a philosopher who discovered them, (2) It is derived from the name of a student named Isa Ghuji who was addressed by the teacher, and (3) other possibilities. This etymological discussion shows the historical interest in the origins of technical terms in logic, although the author does not endorse any single explanation.
