Division of Universals into Derived and Invented
Critical refinement of the five universals by dividing them into derived and invented.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between abstracted (muntaza‘) and invented (mukhtara‘) universals and explain why impossible universals do not refute the fivefold classification.

Core Idea
Not all universals require real instances; some are purely invented by the mind.

Some critics have argued that concepts like 'nothing' and 'non-possible' are universals that cannot be predicated of anything, thus the five predicables are insufficient. The author responds by dividing universals into two kinds: those abstracted from reality and those invented by the mind. This critical refinement shows that the five predicables remain unshaken when properly understood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the critic's claim: that concepts like 'nothing' and 'non-possible' are universals yet cannot be predicated of anything, so they break the fivefold classification.
The objection targets the completeness of the five universal types.
2
Understand the author's division
The author divides all universals into two categories: abstracted (muntaza‘) – those have a foundation in external reality; and invented (mukhtara‘) – those are mere mental creations with no external basis.
This is a critical refinement of the classical theory.
3
Apply the division to the examples
Classify 'nothing' and 'non-possible' as invented universals. Since they have no real instances, the question of essential/accidental predication does not apply to them.
Thus they do not constitute a counterexample to the fivefold classification.
4
Note the scope of the discussion
The author mentions that logicians restrict the discussion to simple concepts for ease, but in principle the five universals apply to composites as well.
This explains why the classification is comprehensive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كاللاشى واللاممكن بالامكان العام فانها كليان لا يمكن صدقهما في نفس الأمر على شيء من الاشياء الخارجية والذهنية لان كل ما يفرض فى الخارج والذهن يصدق عليه شيء وممكن فيمتنع صدق نقيضيهها على مفهوم المفهومات فلا يتعلق بالبحث عن الكليات الفرضية غرض يعتد به فليس لنا بحث عنها فلا ينتقض انحصار الكليات فى الخمس بحدود المعدودات ورسومها كما نقضه بعضهم فقال: ان ثبوت الشىء للشيء فرع ثبوت المثبت له واذا لم يكن المثبت له موجوداً فكيف يدعى ان هذا ذاتى له او عرضى هذا، لأن لنا ان نقول بان الكليات وان كانت كلها بمجرد اعتبار العقل و انتزاعه من الافراد لكن ما يعتبره العقول قسمان: قسم يكون للمنتزع منه اصل الحقيقة وقسم ليس الاعض اختراع فالكلى اما منتزع او مخترع واعتبار الذاتى والعرضى في القسم الأول دون الثاني. ثم اعلم ان القوم خصصوا الكلام في مبحث الكليات بالمعاني المفردة مع انه يمكن اعتبار الخمسة في المركبة ايضاً تسهيلاً للامر على الناظرين والا فكل مفهوم لا يخلو من تلك الخمسة مفرداً كان او مركباً.
Like 'nothing' and 'non-possible' in the general sense of possibility; for they are two universals that cannot be truly predicated in nafs al-amr of any external or mental thing, because everything that is supposed in the external or mental is something and possible, so it is impossible for their two negations to be true of any concept. No worthwhile purpose attaches to the investigation of hypothetical universals, so we do not investigate them. Thus the limitation of the universals to five is not refuted by the definitions and descriptions of numbered things, as some have objected, saying: 'The affirmation of a thing for a thing is a branch of the affirmation of the subject; if the subject does not exist, how can it be claimed that this is essential or accidental to it?' Because we can say that universals, although they are all merely by the consideration of the intellect and its abstraction from individuals, yet what the intellects consider is of two kinds: a kind that has a basis in reality from which it is abstracted, and a kind that is nothing but invention. So the universal is either abstracted or invented, and the consideration of essential and accidental applies only to the first kind, not the second. Then know that the people (logicians) confined the discussion in the topic of universals to simple meanings, even though it is possible to consider the five in composite meanings as well, to make it easier for the viewers. Otherwise, every concept is not free from these five, whether simple or composite.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection against the limitation of universals to five types. The objection says that concepts like 'nothing' (la shay’) and 'non-possible' (la mumkin) are universals but cannot be predicated of anything actual, so how can they be analyzed into the five predicables? The response is a critical refinement: universals are divided into two kinds—abstracted (muntaza‘) from real things, and invented (mukhtara‘) by the mind. Essential and accidental attributes apply only to the first kind. The impossible universals are of the second kind, so they do not break the fivefold classification. The commentator then notes that the logicians restricted the discussion to simple concepts for ease, but in principle the five universals apply to composites as well. This shows that the classification is comprehensive.

The Objection on 'Thing' and 'Existent' as Universals
Presents an objection and response on the classification of 'thing' and 'existent'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the objection that 'thing' and 'existent' do not fall under any of the five universals and evaluate the response.

Core Idea
The highest universals challenge the fivefold classification; the resolution turns on the temporal distinction of 'applying' to oneself.

In this passage, the commentator presents a subtle objection against the claim that every universal is a genus, species, differentia, property, or common accident. The concepts 'thing' (shay’) and 'existent' (mawjud) seem to escape this classification. We will analyze the objection step by step and see how the response saves the system.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
The objection targets the statement that every universal falls under one of the five predicables.
2
Why 'thing' is not species or genus
The objection argues 'thing' cannot be species because everything has an extra quality beyond mere thingness; it cannot be genus because it is not part of the essence.
3
Why 'thing' is not differentia
It is not part of the quiddity, so it cannot be a differentia. Even if granted, it would be the totality of the common, which is not a differentia.
4
The problem with regarding it as an accident
If considered a property or common accident, it would be an accident. But its subject would then be itself, leading to self-application, or non-thing, leading to contradiction.
5
Examine the response
The response chooses a specific sense of 'urud (applying) and denies self-application by positing a temporal distinction: thingness applies to a thing that becomes a thing after that application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ثم الكلى اذا نسب .... اورد عليه بان من الكليات ما ليس بداخل تحت واحد من الاقسام كالشيء والموجود مثلاً، اما انهما ليسا بداخلين تحت النوع فظاهر، ضرورة ان في جميع الاشياء والموجودات مزية على الشيئية والوجود واما انهما ليسا بجنس فلاتهما ليسا بجزه الحقيقة والايلزم أن لا يصح ما جعلوه من عالى الاجناس عالياً و اما انهما ليسا بفصل فلكونهما غير جزء الماهية ولو سلم فيكونان تمام المشترك كما هو ظاهر واما انهما ليسا داخلين تحت الخاصة والعرض العام فلاتها عرض كما سيأتي ولا يصح كونهما من الأعراض لان معروضهما اما ان يكون هو الشيء والموجود فيلزم عروض الشيء لنفسه او اللاشيء واللا موجود فيلزم اجتماع النقيضين. واجيب بانا نختار القسم الأول من قسمى العروض ولا يلزم شيء. وأما ما ادعيتم من لزوم عروض الشيء لنفسه فممنوع لان الشيئية انما تعرض الشيء الذي يصير شيئاً بعد ذلك العروض و كذلك الوجود فتامل
His statement: 'Then the universal, when it is related...' An objection was raised against it that among universals are those that do not come under any one of the divisions, like 'thing' (shay’) and 'existent' (mawjud) for example. As for them not being under species, it is obvious, because all things and existents have an extra something beyond thingness and existence. And as for them not being genus, because they are not part of the essence; otherwise what they have made as highest genera would not be valid as highest. And as for them not being differentia, because they are not part of the quiddity; and even if granted, they would be the totality of the common (tamam al-mushtarak), as is obvious. And as for them not being under property or common accident, because they are accidents, as will come, and it is not valid for them to be accidents, because their subject would either be 'thing' or 'existent', leading to an accident applying to itself, or 'non-thing' and 'non-existent', leading to a contradiction. And it was answered: we choose the first of the two kinds of 'applying' (al-‘urud), and nothing follows. As for what you claimed of the necessity of an accident applying to itself, it is denied, because thingness applies only to a thing that becomes a thing after that applying; likewise existence. So reflect.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage presents a sophisticated objection against the claim that all universals fall under one of the five predicables (genus, species, etc.). The objection argues that 'thing' (shay’) and 'existent' (mawjud) are counterexamples: they cannot be species, because everything has an extra quality beyond mere thingness; they cannot be genus, because they are not part of the essence; they cannot be differentia for the same reason; and if they were considered property or common accident, they would have to be accidents, but then their subject would be themselves or nothing, leading to a problem. The response chooses one of two senses of 'applying' (al-‘urud) and denies the self-application problem. Specifically, thingness applies to a thing that becomes a thing after the application—so the subject is not the same. This resolution preserves the classification. The passage demonstrates the subtlety of logical categorization and typical objection-response format.

Key Terms
الشيء
الموجود
عروض الشيء لنفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) By reclassifying it as a differentia
D) By accepting the objection as valid

Key Takeaway
The objection that 'thing' and 'existent' cannot be classified is resolved by a nuanced understanding of 'accident' (arad) that avoids self-application.

The Names and Etymology of the Five Universals
Author explains the names and etymology of the five universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the etymological meaning of 'Īsāghūjī' and identify the different opinions on why the five universals are so named.

Core Idea
The term 'Īsāghūjī' literally means 'universal five'—a name rooted in Greek, not Arabic.

Before diving into the definitions of the five universals, it is important to understand why they are called 'al-kulliyyāt' in Arabic and 'Īsāghūjī' in other languages. This passage from the hashiya clarifies the etymology and the various historical reasons for the name, helping you appreciate the cross-cultural transmission of logical terminology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two primary names
Find in the Arabic text the two names given to the five universals: one in Arabic (الكليات) and one in Hebrew/Greek (الإيساغوجي). Notice that the author mentions both languages.
The term 'al-kulliyyāt' is a direct Arabic translation meaning 'the universals'.
2
Parse the Greek etymology
Break down the word 'Īsāghūjī' into its components: 'īsā' (universal) and 'fūjī' (five). Confirm that the passage explicitly gives this meaning.
This is the most widely accepted etymology among logicians.
3
Analyze the first opinion on naming
Read the first opinion: that 'Īsāghūjī' was the name of a sage who discovered or compiled the five universals, so the work was named after him.
This is a common pattern in Greek sciences (e.g., Euclid's Elements).
4
Analyze the second opinion
Read the second opinion: that a student named Īsāghūjī was addressed by the teacher, and the name stuck as a form of address.
This opinion is more anecdotal and less systematic.
5
Summarize the different opinions
List all opinions mentioned in the passage: (1) named after a sage, (2) named after a student, (3) other unidentified views. Note that the author does not settle on one.
This shows that the origin of the term is not definitively known.

قوله : منحصرة فى خمسة انواع اعلم ان هذه الانواع الخمسة يقال لها: «الكليات» بالعربية و الايساغوجى» بالعبرية وقيل باليونانية و هو مركب من «ايسا» ای الکلی و «الفوجی » اي الخمس. وقيل في سبب تسميتها به انه اسم حكيم استخرجها و دونت فسميت باسم مستخرجها و قيل: انما سميت به لان بعض من لان بعض من كان متعلمها شخصاً يسمى بـ «ایسا غوجى» فكان يخاطبه في مسألة منها باسمه ويقول يا ايساغوجي الحال كذا و كذا وقيل : غير ذلك (عبد الرحيم)
He said: 'Restricted to five types.' Know that these five types are called 'al-kulliyyāt' in Arabic, 'al-Isāghūjī' in Hebrew, and it is said in Greek. It is composed of 'īsā' meaning 'universal' and 'fūjī' meaning 'five'. It is said regarding the reason for its naming that it is the name of a sage who extracted them and they were compiled, so they were named after their extractor. And it is said: it was only named thus because some of those who learned it were a person named 'Īsāghūjī', and he used to address him regarding a question from it by his name and say: 'O Īsāghūjī, the case is such and such.' And it is said: other than that. (Abd al-Rahīm)
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage explains the terminology for the five universals, known as 'al-kulliyyāt' in Arabic and 'Isāghūjī' in Hebrew and Greek. The author breaks down the Greek etymology: 'īsā' meaning universal and 'fūjī' meaning five. He then provides various opinions on why this term was chosen: some say it was the name of a sage who discovered them, others say it was the name of a student who was addressed. This is typical of introductory materials in logic. The passage serves to orient the student to the nomenclature before delving into the definitions of the five universals. Note that this is a gloss on the original matn by the hashiya author, clarifying the naming.

Abstracted vs. Invented Universals
Author refutes a counterexample against the five universals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between abstracted and invented universals and explain why impossible concepts do not refute the five universals.

Core Idea
Not all universals need real instances; some are purely mental inventions.

The five universals are foundational for definition, but an objection arises: what about concepts like 'non-thing' that have no instances? This lesson shows how the logician refines the theory by distinguishing two types of universals, preserving the completeness of the scheme.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector claims that concepts like 'non-thing' (lā shay') and 'non-possible with universal possibility' have no real instances, so essential/accidental cannot apply, thus the five universals are incomplete.
2
Extract the author's response
The author responds by distinguishing between abstracted universals (kullī muntaqa'), which have a basis in external reality, and invented universals (kullī mukhtara'), which are purely mental constructs.
3
Apply the distinction
Show that 'non-thing' and similar concepts are invented universals, not abstracted ones. Therefore, the requirement of having real instances does not apply, and the five universals are not threatened.
4
Recognize the refinement
The author's refinement shows that the five universals only cover abstracted universals where essential and accidental attributes are meaningful. Invented universals are outside this discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كاللاشى واللاممكن بالامكان العام فانها كليان لا يمكن صدقهما في نفس الأمر على شيء من الاشياء الخارجية والذهنية لان كل ما يفرض فى الخارج والذهن يصدق عليه شيء وممكن فيمتنع صدق نقيضيهها على مفهوم المفهومات فلا يتعلق بالبحث عن الكليات الفرضية غرض يعتد به فليس لنا بحث عنها فلا ينتقض انحصار الكليات فى الخمس بحدود المعدودات ورسومها كما نقضه بعضهم فقال: ان ثبوت الشىء للشيء فرع ثبوت المثبت له واذا لم يكن المثبت له موجوداً فكيف يدعى ان هذا ذاتى له او عرضى هذا، لأن لنا ان نقول بان الكليات وان كانت كلها بمجرد اعتبار العقل و انتزاعه من الافراد لكن ما يعتبره العقول قسمان: قسم يكون للمنتزع منه اصل الحقيقة وقسم ليس الاعض اختراع فالكلى اما منتزع او مخترع واعتبار الذاتى والعرضى في القسم الأول دون الثاني. (عبد الرحيم)
Such as non-thing and non-possible with universal possibility, for they are two universals that cannot be truly predicated in themselves of any external or mental thing, because everything supposed in the external or mental is something and possible, so it is impossible for their contradictories to apply to any concept. So no worthwhile purpose attaches to the investigation of hypothetical universals, and we have no discussion of them. Thus the restriction of the universals to five is not refuted by the definitions and descriptions of nonexistent things, as some have objected, saying: 'The affirmation of a thing for a thing is subordinate to the affirmation of that for which it is affirmed, and if that for which it is affirmed does not exist, how can one claim that this is essential or accidental for it?' This is because we can say that universals, even though all of them are merely a mental consideration and abstraction from individuals, yet what the intellects consider are two types: a type that has a basis of reality from which it is abstracted, and a type that is merely invention. So the universal is either abstracted or invented, and the consideration of essential and accidental applies only to the first type, not the second. (Abd al-Rahīm)
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection that some universals like 'non-thing' (i.e., nothingness) and 'non-possible' (i.e., impossibility) do not correspond to any real individuals, so how can they be considered universals? The objector argues that since these concepts have no actual instances, it is meaningless to speak of essential or accidental properties for them, and thus the scheme of five universals seems incomplete. The author responds by distinguishing between two types of universals: abstracted (muntaqa') and invented (mukhtara'). Abstracted universals derive from real individual instances, like 'human' from individual humans. Invented universals are purely mental constructs with no counterpart in reality, such as impossible concepts. The discussion of essential and accidental only applies to abstracted universals, not invented ones. Therefore, the objection fails. This is a key refinement in the theory of universals, showing that the five categories cover only meaningful concepts.

C) Because they are not relevant to logic.

Key Takeaway
The five universals only apply to abstracted universals; invented ones are not part of the scheme, so objections based on impossible concepts are invalid.

Objection Against 'Thing' and 'Existent' as Universals
Author presents and answers an objection that 'thing' and 'existent' do not fall under any of the five universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection that 'thing' and 'existent' do not fall under the five universals and explain the hashya author's response using the concept of second intention.

Core Idea
'Thing' and 'existent' are second intentions, not first-order properties.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a sophisticated objection: if the five universals cover all universal concepts, then what about 'thing' and 'existent' which seem broader than any species, yet not genera or differentiae? The objection aims to show a gap in the five-universal scheme. The answer refines our understanding of what kind of concepts those are.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of the objection
The objection targets the claim that all universals are under the five. Notice the objector uses 'thing' and 'existent' as counterexamples.
2
List the four arguments
The objector provides separate arguments why 'thing'/'existent' are not species, genus, differentia, property, or general accident. Track each reasoning.
The argument against property/accident involves a dilemma: the subject is either itself (self-inherence) or non-existent (contradiction).
3
Understand the dilemma over inherence
The objector claims that if 'thing' is an accident, its subject leads to absurdity. Identify the two horns: subject is the thing itself or non-thing.
4
Grasp the hashya author's response
The author chooses the first horn and explains that 'thingness' inheres in a thing that becomes a thing after the inherence. This means 'thing' is a second-intention concept, not a first-order property.
Second-intention concepts are about other concepts, not directly about extramental realities.
5
Reflect on how this preserves the five universals
The five universals apply to first-intention concepts; second-intention concepts like 'thing' are meta-categories and thus are not required to fit into the scheme.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ثم الكلى اذا نسب .... اورد عليه بان من الكليات ما ليس بداخل تحت واحد من الاقسام كالشيء والموجود مثلاً، اما انهما ليسا بداخلين تحت النوع فظاهر، ضرورة ان في جميع الاشياء والموجودات مزية على الشيئية والوجود واما انهما ليسا بجنس فلاتهما ليسا بجزه الحقيقة والايلزم أن لا يصح ما جعلوه من عالى الاجناس عالياً و اما انهما ليسا بفصل فلكونهما غير جزء الماهية ولو سلم فيكونان تمام المشترك كما هو ظاهر واما انهما ليسا داخلين تحت الخاصة والعرض العام فلاتها عرض كما سيأتي ولا يصح كونهما من الأعراض لان معروضهما اما ان يكون هو الشيء والموجود فيلزم عروض الشيء لنفسه او اللاشيء واللا موجود فيلزم اجتماع النقيضين. واجيب بانا نختار القسم الأول من قسمى العروض ولا يلزم شيء. وأما ما ادعيتم من لزوم عروض الشيء لنفسه فممنوع لان الشيئية انما تعرض الشيء الذي يصير شيئاً بعد ذلك العروض و كذلك الوجود فتامل (میرزا محمد علی)
His statement: 'Then the universal when it is related...' It was objected against him that some universals are not included under any of the divisions, such as 'thing' and 'existent' for example. As for them not being included under species, it is obvious, necessarily because all things and existents have a superiority over thingness and existence. As for them not being a genus, it is because they are not a part of reality; otherwise it would necessitate that what they made as the highest genera would not be highest. And as for them not being a differentia, it is because they are not a part of the essence; and even if it were granted, they would be the complete commonality as is apparent. And as for them not being included under property and general accident, it is because they are accident as will come, and it is not correct for them to be from the accidents because their subject is either the thing and existent, leading to the inherence of a thing in itself, or non-thing and non-existent, leading to the conjunction of two contradictories. It was answered: We choose the first of the two types of inherence, and nothing follows. As for your claim of the necessity of inherence of a thing in itself, it is denied, because thingness only inheres in a thing that becomes a thing after that inherence, and likewise existence. So ponder. (Mīrzā Muḥammad 'Alī)
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage presents an objection that the concepts 'thing' (shay') and 'existent' (mawjūd) do not fit into any of the five universals. The objector argues: they are not species because they are broader than any species; not genera because they are not constitutive parts of essence (otherwise they would be the highest genera, which is contradictory); not differentiae because they are not part of the essence; not property or general accident because they are themselves the basis for accident—their supposed subjects would be either themselves (causing self-inherence) or non-entities (causing contradiction). The author's response (from Mīrzā) chooses the first horn: that the subject is indeed the thing itself, but this does not cause a problem because thingness inheres in a thing that becomes a thing after the inherence. In other words, 'thing' is a second-intention concept. This is a refined dialectical exchange common in logical commentaries.

Key Terms
الشيء
الموجود

B) It inheres in a thing that becomes a thing after the inherence.
C) It inheres in non-thing.
D) It inheres in the essence of the species.

Key Takeaway
The concepts 'thing' and 'existent' are second intentions, which avoids the dilemma of self-inherence and preserves the completeness of the five universals.

Defining Complete Commonality (Tamām al-Mushtarak)
Author defines complete commonality and addresses remote genera objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given universal qualifies as a 'complete commonality' and explain how remote genera can function as complete commonalities for different pairs.

Core Idea
Complete commonality is the set of all common parts, but it shifts depending on the pair compared.

In defining genus, logicians require that the genus be the 'complete commonality' (tamām al-mushtarak) between the species it includes. This passage from Yazdi's Hashiya clarifies what that means, distinguishing between simple and compound genera, and resolving the puzzle of remote genera. Understanding this nuance is essential for correctly applying the five universals in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of complete commonality
Read the opening: 'If it is the complete commonality...' The complete commonality is the sum total of all attributes shared between two things. For compound genera like 'animal', it is multiple (substance, growing body, etc.); for simple genera like 'substance', it is a single attribute.
The 'لام' in 'تمام المشترك' is for the genus, so it includes both types.
2
Identify the objection about remote genera
An objector points out that remote genera like 'growing body' are not the complete commonality between man and horse (that is 'animal'), so they seem to be excluded. But remote genera are still valid genera.
The objection is that if complete commonality is required, remote genera would be invalid, contrary to established usage.
3
Analyze the response: context-dependence
The author responds that 'growing body' is the complete commonality between man and tree. Similarly, 'absolute body' is complete commonality between one thing (e.g., man) and stone. So remote genera are complete commonalities when compared with more distant species.
The same universal can be a complete commonality for one pair and a partial commonality for another.
4
Apply Shaykh 'Abd al-Rahīm's refined definition
He says complete commonality is that beyond which there is no common part: every common part between the two things is either that universal itself or a part of it. For animal and man/horse, all common parts (substance, growing body, etc.) are parts of animal. This criterion works for all cases.
This resolves the issue because it does not require the universal to be the most specific commonality, just that there is no common part outside it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله فان كان تمام المشترك اى تمام المشترك فيه واللام فيه للجنس، فيعم ما اذا كان المشترك فيه متعدداً كما في الاجناس المركبة كالحيوان مثلاً فانه مجموع المشتركات بين الانسان و الفرس مثلاً بمعنى ان ليس بينهما مشترك الا وهو جزء منه و مالم يكن متعدداً كما في الاجناس البسيطة كالجوهر فانه تمام المشترك بين العقل والانسان مثلاً بمعنى ان ليس بينها مشترك الا اياه. ومنهم من فسره بما يختص بالقسم الاول حيث قال المراد بتمام المشترك تمام الاجزاء المشتركة بينهما.  ۲۳۲ حواشي الحاشية كالحيوان فانه مجموع الجوهر والجسم النامي والحساس و المتحرك بالارادة و هى اجزاء مشتركة بين الانسان والفرس فينتقض التعريف بالاجناس البسيطة فتأمل. فان قلت ان هذا غير شامل للاجناس البعيدة فانها ليست تمام المشترك بل جزئه كالجسم النامي بالنسبة الى الانسان و الفرس فان تمام المشترك بينهما هو الحيوان والجسم النامي جزئه و كالجسم المطلق بالنسبة اليهما أو اليهما و الى الشجر فان تمام المشترك على الأول هو الحيوان و على الثانى هو الجسم النامي و ليس الجسم المطلق الاجزء منها فيلزم ان يكون الاجناس البعيدة داخلة فى حيز قوله: «والا فهو الفصل» كما هو ظاهر لمن له ادنى مسكة. قلت ان الجسم النامى و ان لم يكن بالنسبة الى الانسان و الفرس تمام المشترك ، الا انه تمام المشترك بالنسبة الى الانسان والشجر و كذا الجسم المطلق فانه وان كان بالنسبة الى الانسان والفرس او اليهما والى الشجر ليس بتمام المشترك ، الا انه بالنسبة الى احدها والحجر تمام المشترك و هكذا فتأمل. (میرزا محمد علی) وقال الشيخ عبد الرحيم (ره): المراد بتمام المشترك الذى لا يكون ورائه جزء مشترك بينهما اي جزء مشترك لا يكون جزء مشترك خارجاً عنه بل كل جزء مشترك بينهما يكون اما نفس ذلك الجزء أو جزء منه كالحيوان فانه تمام المشترك بين الانسان والفرس اذ لا جزء مشترك الا وهو اما نفس الحيوان او جزء منه كالجوهر والجسم النامى و الحساس و المتجرك بالارادة فكل منها وان كان مشتركا بين الانسان والفرس، الا انه ليس تمام المشترك بينهما بل بعضه وانما تمام المشترك بينهما هو الحيوان المشتمل على الكل.
His statement: 'If it is the complete commonality,' i.e., the complete commonality in it, and the lam is for the genus, so it includes when the commonality is multiple as in compound genera like animal, for it is the sum of the commonalities between man and horse for example, meaning there is no commonality between them except that it is a part of it. And if it is not multiple as in simple genera like substance, it is the complete commonality between intellect and man for example, meaning there is no commonality between them except it. And some interpret it as restricted to the first type, saying: what is meant by complete commonality is all the common parts between them. [PAGE_227] 232 Annotations of the Hashiya: Like animal, for it is the sum of substance, growing body, sentient, and willfully moving, and these are common parts between man and horse, so the definition would be refuted by simple genera, so ponder. If you say: This does not include remote genera, for they are not the complete commonality but a part of it—like growing body relative to man and horse, for the complete commonality between them is animal, and growing body is a part of it; and like absolute body relative to them or to them and to tree, for the complete commonality in the first is animal, in the second is growing body, and absolute body is only a part of them, so it would necessitate that remote genera fall under his statement: 'Otherwise it is the differentia' as is clear to anyone with the least understanding. I say: Growing body, even if relative to man and horse is not the complete commonality, yet it is the complete commonality relative to man and tree; likewise absolute body, even if relative to man and horse or to them and tree is not the complete commonality, yet relative to one of them and stone it is the complete commonality. So ponder. (Mīrzā Muḥammad 'Alī) And Shaykh 'Abd al-Rahīm (may God have mercy on him) said: What is meant by complete commonality is that which has no common part beyond it, i.e., no common part that is outside it, but every common part between them is either that part itself or a part of it, like animal, for it is the complete commonality between man and horse since there is no common part except it or a part of it, such as substance, growing body, sentient, and willfully moving. Each of these, although common between man and horse, is not the complete commonality between them but some of it; the complete commonality between them is only animal, which encompasses all.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage defines the technical term 'complete commonality' (tamām al-mushtarak) used in the definition of genus. The author explains that 'complete commonality' refers to the sum of all common parts shared between two things. In compound genera like animal, the commonality is multiple (substance, growing body, etc.); in simple genera like substance, it is single. Some interpret it only for compound genera, but that would exclude simple genera. The objection is raised about remote genera (e.g., growing body is not the complete commonality between man and horse, but a part of it; yet it is a genus). The answer is that remote genera are complete commonality for different pairs (e.g., growing body is complete commonality between man and tree). Then Shaykh 'Abd al-Rahīm provides a refined definition: complete commonality is that beyond which there is no common part; every common part is either the whole or a part of it. This clarifies the notion. This is an expansion probe typical of a hashya.

Key Terms
تمام المشترك
الأجناس البسيطة
الأجناس المركبة
الأجناس البعيدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Shaykh 'Abd al-Rahīm's explanation, what makes a universal a 'complete commonality'?
A) It is the most general term.
B) It has no common part beyond it; every common part is either itself or a part of it.
C) It is a simple concept.
D) It is shared by many species.

Key Takeaway
A complete commonality is a universal that encompasses all common parts between two things, with no common part outside it, and it may be relative to the pair compared.

Two Senses of 'Essential' and Its Applicability to Species
Author refines the applicability of 'essential' to species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two usages of 'essential' (dhātī) and determine whether species qualifies as essential under each.

Core Idea
Essential can mean either 'part of the essence' or 'not external to it'—species fits only the second.

This passage from a hashya clarifies the technical term 'essential' (dhātī). It shows that logicians use two definitions, and that species, being the whole essence, is not essential in the first sense but can be in the second. Understanding this nuance is crucial for correctly applying the five universals in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions of dhātī
Find where the author states 'essential' is used for what is inside the essence (part of it) and also for what is not external to it. Note that the first is more famous.
2
Apply the first definition to species
Since species is the complete essence (e.g., 'human' for human), it cannot be inside itself. Therefore, under the first definition, species is not essential.
3
Apply the second definition to species
Species is not external to the essence; it is the essence itself. So under the second meaning ('not external'), species qualifies as essential.
4
Consider the objection and response
An objection says attribution requires difference, so essence cannot be attributed to itself. The author answers that logicians use the term conventionally, and even if suitability is required, it suffices that it applies in some cases.
The author also discusses another answer involving multiple meanings of 'essence' but criticizes it for inconsistency.

Source Text

قوله و يقال لهذه الثلاثة ذاتيات: الاشهر الاكثر اطلاق الذاتي على ما يكون داخلاً في اطلاق الماهية كما صرح به بعض المحققين وقد يطلق على ما لا يكون خارجاً عنها، فعلى الاول لا يصح الذاتي على النوع لانه تمام الماهية و الشيء لا يكون داخلاً فى نفسه واما على الثاني فيصح، فان النوع ليس بخارج عن الماهية فان الشيء كما لا يكون داخلاً في نفسه لايكون خارجاً عنه. بقى هنا شيء وهو: ان النوع كما ذكر عين الذات وذات الشيء لا تكون منسوبة الى نفسها بل انما ينسب الى الشيء ما ليس هو فان النسبة يقتضى المغايرة فلا يصح اطلاق الذاتي عليه. والجواب ان اطلاق الذاتى عليه اصطلاح اهل المنطق والمناسبة غير لازمة في المنقولات المرتجلة ،ولوسلم، فالمناسبة يكفى كونها في بعض الافراد كما صرح به البعض، هذا. واجاب بعض المحققين من المحشين بان الذات كما يطلق على الحقيقة يطلق ايضاً على ماصدق عليه الحقيقة فربما يراد بالذات هيهنا المعنى الثانى فيمكن نسبة الحقيقة الى ماصدق عليه الحقيقة كما يمكن نسبة جزئيها اليها . واقول هذا على فرض التسليم يوجب التفكيك بين الذاتيات و هو غير جيد كما لا يخفى على ارباب الطبع السليم. اللهم الا ان يقال بذلك فى جميعها وكذا القول بان ياء النسبة انما جيئت بها للمبالغة كما في قوله : فقربهم لهذميات على وجه فتأمل. وقد ذكر هذا الجواب الشيخ فى الاشارات على ما نقل حيث قال: «ان الماهية ليست ذاتية لنفسها
His statement: 'And these three are called essential (dhātiyyāt).' The most famous and frequent usage of 'essential' is for what is inside the essence, as some scholars have stated. And it may be used for what is not external to it. According to the first, 'essential' does not apply to species because it is the complete essence, and a thing is not inside itself. But according to the second, it does apply, for species is not external to essence, just as a thing is not inside itself nor external to it. There remains something: that species, as mentioned, is the essence itself, and a thing's essence is not attributed to itself; rather, only what is other than it is attributed to it, because attribution requires difference. So applying 'essential' to it is not correct. The answer: The application of 'essential' to it is the terminology of the logicians, and suitability is not necessary in transmitted conventional terms; and even if granted, suitability in some individuals is sufficient as some have stated. And some scholars among the commentators answered that 'essence' (dhāt) is used both for reality (ḥaqīqa) and for what the reality applies to. Perhaps 'essence' here is used in the second sense, so it is possible to relate the reality to what it applies to, just as it is possible to relate its parts to it. I say: This, even if granted, necessitates a disjunction among the essential characteristics, which is not good, as is not hidden from those of sound nature. Unless it is said that it applies to all of them, and likewise the statement that the yā of relation is brought for emphasis, as in his statement: 'their proximity for the purpose of sharpening,' so ponder. And this answer was mentioned by the Shaykh in al-Ishārāt as reported, where he said: 'Essence is not essential for itself.'
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage examines whether the term 'essential' (dhātī) can be applied to species (naw'). The author notes two usages: one meaning 'part of the essence' (so species cannot be essential since it is the whole essence), another meaning 'not external to the essence' (so species qualifies). An objection arises: attribution requires difference between subject and attribute, so essence cannot be attributed to itself. The answer is that logicians use the term conventionally, and even if linguistic suitability is needed, it suffices that it applies in some cases. Another answer suggests that 'essence' (dhāt) can mean 'what the reality applies to', allowing predication. The author (Mīrzā Muhammad 'Alī) criticizes this for creating inconsistency. He then mentions Shaykh al-Ishārāt's (Avicenna) statement: 'Essence is not essential for itself.' This is a careful refinement typical of a hashya, clarifying the scope of the technical term 'essential.'

The Problem of 'Dhati' for Species: Self-Predication in the Five Universals
Commentator critiques views on whether 'dhati' can be applied to species, analyzing definitions and objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the logical problem of applying 'dhati' (essential) to species and evaluate the proposed solutions in the Islamic logical tradition.

Core Idea
Can species be called 'essential'? The self-predication problem reveals deep subtleties in the five universals.

In this passage, the commentator Mirza Muhammad Ali examines a longstanding problem in Islamic logic: whether the term 'dhati' (essential) can be applied to species, given that species is identical to the essence. He distinguishes two meanings of 'dhati' and engages with earlier answers, including one from Avicenna, before proposing his own third alternative. This analysis sharpens our understanding of essential predication and the five universals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of 'dhati'
Find in the Arabic where the commentator distinguishes the first meaning ('internal to the essence') and the second meaning ('not external to it'). Write them down.
2
Apply each meaning to species
According to the first meaning, species cannot be called 'dhati' because it is the complete essence and cannot be internal to itself. According to the second, it can be called 'dhati' because it is not external to the essence.
3
Recognize the self-predication objection
Even with the second meaning, a problem remains: species is identical to the essence, and attribution requires otherness. So 'dhati' would imply a relation of essence to itself, which is impossible.
4
Examine the two answers and the commentator's critique
First answer: it's just a convention of logicians. Second: 'dhat' can mean the referent of the essence. The commentator rejects the second because it creates disunity among essentials.
5
Analyze the third alternative
Following Avicenna's discussion, the commentator proposes that 'dhati' for species means attribution to the quiddity as conjoined with the individual, which avoids the problem because the quiddity in conjunction is different from the quiddity alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله و يقال لهذه الثلاثة ذاتيات: الاشهر الاكثر اطلاق الذاتي على ما يكون داخلاً في الماهية كما صرح به بعض المحققين وقد يطلق على ما لا يكون خارجاً عنها، فعلى الاول لا يصح الذاتي على النوع لانه تمام الماهية و الشيء لا يكون داخلاً فى نفسه واما على الثاني فيصح، فان النوع ليس بخارج عن الماهية فان الشيء كما لا يكون داخلاً في نفسه لايكون خارجاً عنه. بقى هنا شيء وهو: ان النوع كما ذكر عين الذات وذات الشيء لا تكون منسوبة الى نفسها بل انما ينسب الى الشيء ما ليس هو فان النسبة يقتضى المغايرة فلا يصح اطلاق الذاتي عليه. والجواب ان اطلاق الذاتى عليه اصطلاح اهل المنطق والمناسبة غير لازمة في المنقولات المرتجلة ،ولوسلم، فالمناسبة يكفى كونها في بعض الافراد كما صرح به البعض، هذا. واجاب بعض المحققين من المحشين بان الذات كما يطلق على الحقيقة يطلق ايضاً على ماصدق عليه الحقيقة فربما يراد بالذات هيهنا المعنى الثانى فيمكن نسبة الحقيقة الى ماصدق عليه الحقيقة كما يمكن نسبة جزئيها اليها . واقول هذا على فرض التسليم يوجب التفكيك بين الذاتيات و هو غير جيد كما لا يخفى على ارباب الطبع السليم. اللهم الا ان يقال بذلك فى جميعها وكذا القول بان ياء النسبة انما جيئت بها للمبالغة كما في قوله : فقربهم لهذميات على وجه فتأمل. وقد ذكر هذا الجواب الشيخ فى الاشارات على ما نقل حيث قال: «ان الماهية ليست ذاتية لنفسها في الكليات الخمس بل للاشخاص المتكثرة بالعدد ثم ابطله بأنه لو جعل الماهية ذاتية لشخص شخص لم يخلو اما ان يكون نسبتها بالذاتية الى ماهية الشخص من حيث هى هى فيعود المحذوره او الى الجملة التي هي الماهية و التشخص فلا يكون اياها بكمالها بل جزء منها فلا يصح أن النوع عين حقيقة افراده، انتهى . واقول يمكن هنا شق ثالث لايتجه عليه المنع و هو ان يكون نسبتها بالذاتية الى الماهية من حيث اقترانها بالشخص ولاشك في كونها غير الماهية من حيث هي وغير الجملة المركبة من الماهية و التشخص فحينئذ لا يلزم محذور اصلاً فتأمل (میرزا محمد علی)
His statement: 'And these three are called essential (dhatiyyat)': The most famous and most frequent usage of 'dhati' is for what is internal to the essence (mahiyya), as stated by some verifiers. And it may also be used for what is not external to it. According to the first, 'dhati' does not apply to species, because it is the complete essence, and a thing is not internal to itself. But according to the second, it applies, because species is not external to the essence; just as a thing is not internal to itself, it is not external to it. There remains a point: Species, as mentioned, is identical to the essence, and a thing's essence is not attributed to itself; rather, only what is other than it is attributed to it, because attribution requires otherness. So applying 'dhati' to it is invalid. The answer: Applying 'dhati' to it is a convention of logicians, and appropriateness is not necessary in conventional terms. And if conceded, appropriateness suffices by being in some instances, as stated by some. Another answer by some verifiers among the commentators: 'Dhat' (essence) is used for the reality and also for what the reality applies to. Perhaps here 'dhat' means the second sense, so it is possible to attribute the reality to what it applies to, just as one can attribute its parts to it. I say: Even if conceded, this necessitates a disunity among essentials, which is not good, as is not hidden from those of sound nature. Unless it is said that this applies to all of them, and likewise the claim that the 'ya' of attribution is for exaggeration, as in his statement: 'Their closeness...' on a certain view. Ponder. And the Sheikh mentioned this answer in al-Isharat, as reported, where he said: 'The quiddity (mahiyya) is not essential (dhati) to itself in the five universals, but to the numerically multiple individuals.' Then he refuted it: 'If the quiddity is made essential to an individual, it is either attributed essentially to the quiddity of that person as such, in which case the same problem returns, or to the composite of quiddity and individuation, in which case it is not the complete quiddity but a part of it, so it is invalid that the species is identical to the reality of its individuals.' End. I say: There can be a third alternative not faced by this objection: that its essential attribution is to the quiddity in terms of its conjunction with the individual. There is no doubt that it is different from the quiddity as such and from the composite of quiddity and individuation. Then no problem arises at all. Ponder. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) by Mirza Muhammad Ali on the application of the term 'dhati' (essential) to species. He first distinguishes two meanings of 'dhati': (1) what is internal to the essence (mahiyya), and (2) what is not external to it. According to the first, species cannot be called dhati because it is the complete essence and cannot be internal to itself. According to the second, it can be called dhati because it is not external to the essence. However, a problem remains: species is identical to the essence, and attribution requires otherness; so 'dhati' (meaning 'essential') would imply a relation of essence to itself, which is impossible. Various answers are given: one says it's just a convention; another shifts the meaning of 'dhat' to the referent of the essence. Mirza Muhammad Ali critiques these answers, pointing out that the second answer creates disunity among the essentials. He then presents a third alternative: that the essential attribution is to the quiddity as conjoined with the individual, which avoids the problem. This shows the depth of logical analysis regarding self-predication and the subtleties in the terminology of the five universals.

Key Takeaway
The application of 'dhati' to species requires careful handling of self-predication; the third alternative (attribution to the quiddity as conjoined with the individual) resolves the problem.

Distinguishing 'Arad and 'Aradi: Accident vs. Accidental
Commentator explains the terms 'arad and 'aradi, distinguishing their usage and relation to the accident's source.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the terms 'arad and 'aradi in the context of the five universals and explain their relationship to the accident's source.

Core Idea
The ya' of attribution transforms an accident into an accidental, linking the individual to the source.

In the five universals, the terms 'arad (accident) and 'aradi (accidental) appear subtly different. This passage from the commentary clarifies that the difference lies not in the individuals referred to, but in the linguistic form and the perspective: one relates to the origin, the other to the source. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion in definitions and logical classifications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms
Locate 'arad (عرض) and 'aradi (عرضي) in the Arabic. Note that 'aradi has an extra ya' (ياء) indicating attribution.
The ya' is a linguistic marker called 'ya' of attribution' (ياء النسبة).
2
Recognize the individuals
The passage mentions 'the laughing one' (الضاحك) and 'the walking one' (الماشي) as examples of individuals. These are concrete instances that can be called either 'arad or 'aradi depending on perspective.
These individuals are accidents in the sense that they are accidental properties attached to a substance.
3
Determine the perspective: origin vs. source
'Arad is used when these individuals are considered in relation to the origin (mabda') which is the accident itself (e.g., laughter). 'Aradi is used when they are attributed to the accident as a source (ma'khadh).
Origin = the property itself; source = the repository or referent of the property.
4
Compare with the three essentials
The commentator notes that 'dhati' (essential) similarly applies to individuals like 'animal', 'man', 'rational', not the abstract universals. This reinforces the pattern: the term with 'ya' marks attribution to the source.
The three essentials: genus, species, differentia.
5
Summarize the rule
The ya' of attribution changes the term from referring to the direct accident ('arad) to referring to the individual as sourced from the accident ('aradi).
This is a terminological distinction critical for precise logical discourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۶۷) اي بلاياء وذلك لان افراده اعنى الضاحك والماشى ونحوهما يقال له: العرض باعتبار نسبتها الى المبدء الذى هو العرض كالضحك والمشى مثلاً وقد يقال له: العرضي بياء النسبة و ذلك لان افراده منسوبة الى العرض اعنى المأخذ و كذا اطلاق الذاتي على الذاتيات الثلاثة فان الذاتي في الحقيقة هو الحيوان والانسان والناطق و نحوها من الافراد ميرزا محمد علی)
(67) That is, without the ya', because its individuals, i.e., 'the laughing one', 'the walking one', and the like, are called 'accident' ('arad) by virtue of their attribution to the origin which is the accident, like laughter and walking for example. And it may be called 'accidental' ('aradi) with the ya' of attribution, because its individuals are attributed to the accident, i.e., the source. Likewise the application of 'essential' (dhati) to the three essentials, for the essential in reality is animal, man, and the rational, and the like among individuals. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is an annotational expansion (sharh) by Mirza Muhammad Ali on the distinction between 'arad (accident) and 'aradi (accidental). The term 'arad (without the ya') is used when considering the individuals (like 'the laughing one') in relation to the origin (mabda'), which is the accident itself (like laughter). The term 'aradi (with the ya') is used when those same individuals are attributed to the accident as a source (ma'khadh). This parallels the usage of 'dhati' (essential) for the three essentials (genus, species, differentia), where the essential in reality are the individuals like 'animal', 'man', 'rational', not the abstract universals. The key point is that the ya' of attribution marks the relationship to a source, while the form without ya' marks the direct accident.

The Common Accident: The Example of Walking
Author gives an example of a common accident shared by multiple species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a common accident and distinguish it from a genus or differentia.

Core Idea
A common accident is shared across species but does not define their essence.

In the study of the five universals, the concept of 'accident' can be tricky because some accidents are proper to one species, while others are common to many. This short passage provides a clear example of a common accident—walking—to help you grasp how a single property can belong to multiple species without being essential to any of them.

Guided Path
1
Recall the category of accident
Accident ('arad) is one of the five universals. It is a property that is not part of the essence of a thing. It can be present or absent without changing what the thing is.
2
Examine the example: walking
The author gives 'walking' (al-mashi) as an accident. Notice that walking is not essential to being human—a person can be unable to walk yet still be human. It is an accidental property.
3
Identify the species in which walking is found
The passage lists four species: humans, horses, cows, and sheep. All of these can walk. This shows that walking is not limited to one species.
4
Contrast with a proper accident
A proper accident (khassa) is specific to one species, like 'risibility' for humans. Walking is not a proper accident because it is shared, so it is a common accident ('arad 'amm).
5
Distinguish from genus and differentia
Genus and differentia define the essence. Walking does not constitute the essence of any of these species; it is merely a property. Thus it is an accident, not a genus or differentia.

Commentary
This brief passage provides a concrete example of a common accident ('arad 'amm). The property 'walking' (al-mashi) is an accident shared by different species: humans, horses, cows, and sheep. This illustrates the category of accident (one of the five universals) that is not essential but can be common to multiple species. The example is straightforward and serves to clarify the concept of a common accident, distinguishing it from a proper accident (which is specific to a single species).

Resolving the Dilemma of Essence and Individuals
Mirza Muhammad Ali proposes a third option to resolve a contradiction in attributing essence to individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the dilemma of attributing essence to individuals and evaluate Mirza Muhammad Ali's proposed third option as a logical resolution.

Core Idea
The essence relates to the individual neither as whole nor as part, but as conjoined – a third path that saves the doctrine.

In this passage, Mirza Muhammad Ali responds to a logical dilemma about how essence can be essential to individual persons. The objector argues that if essence is essential to an individual, it must relate either to the essence alone (creating a cycle) or to the composite of essence and individuation (making it only a part, not the whole). Mirza proposes a third alternative: the essence relates to the essence as conjoined with the individual, avoiding both problems. This lesson unpacks that solution and its significance for the theory of universals.

Guided Path
1
State the original claim
The claim under discussion is that the essence (māhiyya) of a species is essential (dhātiyya) to each of its individual members. Note what is being asserted about the relationship between essence and individuals.
The original claim is the target of the objector's attack.
2
Understand the objector's dilemma
The objector presents a two-horned dilemma: if essence is essential to an individual, then either it is essential to the essence itself (leading to a regress or cycle) or to the composite of essence and individuation (making it only a part, contradicting the claim that the species is the very reality of its individuals). Identify these two horns.
3
Locate Mirza's response
Find where Mirza says 'I say' (أقول) and identify the third path he proposes. The text says: 'And I say: there is possible here a third path that the refutation does not apply to.'
4
Analyze the third option
Mirza says the relation is to the essence from the perspective of its conjunction with the individual (من حيث اقترانها بالشخص). This is neither the essence alone nor the composite of essence and individuation. Explain why this avoids both problems: it does not cause a cycle because it is not the pure essence, and it does not make the essence only a part because it is not the composite.
The key distinction is that conjunction (iqtiran) is not composition (tarkib).
5
Reflect on the logical move
Consider how introducing a third relation resolves the dilemma. What does this show about the flexibility of logical analysis in Islamic philosophy? It demonstrates that careful modal distinctions can dissolve apparent contradictions.

في الكليات الخمس بل للاشخاص المتكثرة بالعدد ثم ابطله بأنه لو جعل الماهية ذاتية لشخص شخص لم يخلو اما ان يكون نسبتها بالذاتية الى ماهية الشخص من حيث هى هى فيعود المحذوره او الى الجملة التي هي الماهية و التشخص فلا يكون اياها بكمالها بل جزء منها فلا يصح أن النوع عين حقيقة افراده، انتهى . واقول يمكن هنا شق ثالث لايتجه عليه المنع و هو ان يكون نسبتها بالذاتية الى الماهية من حيث اقترانها بالشخص ولاشك في كونها غير الماهية من حيث هي وغير الجملة المركبة من الماهية و التشخص فحينئذ لا يلزم محذور اصلاً فتأمل (میرزا محمد علی)
Concerning the five universals, but for the individuals multiplied in number. Then he refuted it by saying that if the essence is made essential to a person individually, it is not free from either its essential relation being to the person's essence as it is in itself, so the problem returns, or to the composite which is the essence and individuation, so it is not the whole of it but a part of it, so it is not valid that the species is the very reality of its individuals. End. I say: there is possible here a third path that the refutation does not apply to, which is that its essential relation is to the essence from the perspective of its conjunction with the individual, and there is no doubt that it is other than the essence as it is and other than the composite of essence and individuation. Then no problem arises at all. So reflect. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation and response. The objector argues that if the essence (māhiyya) is made essential to an individual person, a contradiction arises: either the relation is to the essence in itself (leading to a cycle) or to the composite of essence and individuation (making it only a part). Mirza Muhammad Ali proposes a third alternative: the essential relation is to the essence as conjoined with the individual but not as part of the composite. This avoids the contradiction. The argument uses distinctions in how the essence relates to individuals, a key topic in universal logic.

Key Terms
الماهية
ذاتية
التشخص

Checkpoint
According to Mirza Muhammad Ali, what is the third option to avoid the contradiction when the essence is made essential to a person?
A) The essence relates to the person's essence as it is in itself.
B) The essence relates to the composite of essence and individuation.
C) The essence relates to the essence from the perspective of its conjunction with the individual, without being the whole composite.
D) The essence does not relate at all.

Key Takeaway
Mirza Muhammad Ali's third option reveals that logical dilemmas can be resolved by careful analysis of the mode of relation, without resorting to either horn.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the debate on whether the attribute of knowledge (sifat al-'ilm) is identical to Allah's essence or additional, a similar dilemma arises: either it is identical (leading to pantheism) or additional (leading to multiplicity). Propose a third option using Mirza's method of 'conjunction without composition' and justify it.
?

The two horns: either the ruling applies to each instance as a part of the general meaning, or the general meaning is a separate entity. Can you formulate a third option based on Mirza's approach?

Distinction Between 'Arad and 'Aradi
Author explains that accident is named from its source (mabda') while accidental is named from its instances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'arad and 'aradi based on whether the attribute is considered relative to its source (mabda') or to its individuals (afrad).

Core Idea
'Arad and 'aradi are the same attribute viewed from two relational perspectives.

In logical analysis, an accidental attribute can be named in two ways: 'arad (without ya of attribution) when considering its relation to the source (mabda'), and 'aradi (with ya) when considering its relation to the individuals that possess it. This distinction clarifies how accidental universals are predicated and avoids confusion in definitional discussions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the accidental attribute
Take an attribute like 'walking' or 'laughing' mentioned in the text. These are accidents that can belong to multiple species.
The attribute itself is a universal predicable of many.
2
Determine the relational perspective
Ask: Are we relating this attribute to its source (the act itself, e.g., the act of walking) or to the individuals (e.g., the walking persons)? The source is the abstract accident; the individuals are the concrete bearers.
The source is the mabda', the individuals are the afrad.
3
Apply the naming rule
If the relation is to the source, call it 'arad (without the ya of attribution). If the relation is to the individuals, call it 'aradi (with the ya). The same attribute can be referred to by either term depending on perspective.
The ya of attribution (yā' al-nisba) marks the shift to 'aradi.
4
Contrast with essential (dhati) usage
The author notes that the term 'essential' (dhati) is similarly applied to the three essentials (genus, species, differentia) when relating to their individuals. This reinforces the pattern: universal terms shift based on relational focus.
This parallel helps solidify the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اي بلاياء وذلك لان افراده اعنى الضاحك والماشى ونحوهما يقال له: العرض باعتبار نسبتها الى المبدء الذى هو العرض كالضحك والمشى مثلاً وقد يقال له: العرضي بياء النسبة و ذلك لان افراده منسوبة الى العرض اعنى المأخذ و كذا اطلاق الذاتي على الذاتيات الثلاثة فان الذاتي في الحقيقة هو الحيوان والانسان والناطق و نحوها من الافراد (میرزا محمد علی) (۶۸) کالماشى فانه عرض مشترك بين افراد الانسان و الفرس والبقر والغنم.
i.e., without ya. And that is because its individuals, namely the laughing, the walking, and the like, are called: al-'arad (without ya) in consideration of their relation to the source which is the accident, like laughing and walking for example. And it may be called: al-'aradi with the ya of relation, and that is because its individuals are related to the accident, i.e., the source. Similarly, the term 'essential' applies to the three essentials, for the essential in reality is animal, man, and speaker, and the like of individuals. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (68) Like walking, for it is a common accident among individuals of man, horse, cow, and sheep.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage defines two related terms: 'arad (accident) and 'aradi (accidental). The same attribute (like laughing or walking) can be called 'arad when considering its relation to its source (mabda') which is the accident itself (like the act of laughing). It is called 'aradi when considering its relation to the individuals that possess it. For example, 'walking' is an accident common to various animals. The passage also notes a similar usage for 'essential' (dhati) when applied to the three essential predicates: genus, species, and differentia.

Proximate vs Remote Genus: The Direct Answer Test
Commentary defines proximate and remote genus with hierarchical examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a proximate genus from a remote genus by applying the direct answer test to any hierarchical classification.

Core Idea
A proximate genus answers without intermediaries; a remote genus requires one or more.

In the Mantiq tradition, the genus is defined by its role in answering 'what is it?' questions across multiple quiddities. This lesson sharpens the distinction between a genus that directly captures the commonality and one that only does so through intermediate genera, using the standard hierarchical example from man to substance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common genus in the answer
When presented with a question about multiple distinct quiddities (e.g., man, horse, stone), note the genus given as the answer. This genus must apply to all.
2
Check directness for each pair
For every pair of quiddities, ask: does the same genus answer the question directly, without needing a lower genus? For example, 'animal' answers both 'what is man?' and 'what is horse?' directly, but not 'what is man and stone?'.
A direct answer means the genus is predicated of each individual quiddity without intermediary.
3
Determine proximity or remoteness
If the genus directly answers for each differing quiddity (i.e., for every pair), it is proximate. If not, it is remote. The degree of remoteness is the number of intermediate genera needed.
Proximate genus is included in the count of answers but not in the degrees of remoteness.
4
Apply the counting rule
Count the number of distinct answers required to cover all pairs. The number of degrees of remoteness is one less than the number of answers. For example, if three answers are needed, the genus is remote by two degrees.
This rule is explicitly stated in the passage.

كلما ازداد عدد الاجوبة ازداد مراتب البعد و يكون عدد المراتب عن عدد الاجوبة بواحد. ناقصاً والسرفيه ان الجنس القريب داخل في عداد الاجوبة وليس من مراتب البعد كما هو ظاهر. و قد تبين من ذلك كله ان الجنس الواحد يجوز ان يكون قريباً وبعيداً بالنسبة الى شيئين و هكذا يجوز ان يكون قريباً وبعيداً بمرتبة وبمرتبتين وبمراتب بالنسبة الى اشياء متعددة كالجوهر فانه جنس قريب للجسم المطلق و جنس بعيد بمرتبة للجسم النامي وبمرتبتين للحيوان و بثلاث مراتب للانسان كما لا يخفى للمتأمل. (میرزا محمد علی)
The quiddity is any quiddity assumed, and [the question is] about realities different from the mentioned quiddity that share with it in the genus, so that it is known that what occurs in the answer is a genus and not a species, because the question about matters differing in reality but sharing in a common essential characteristic cannot be answered except by the genus, because what the question covers of its properties alone. Thus the commentator's statement: 'sharing with it in that genus' is meaningless, because no genus is known to be referred to. And the genus mentioned in his statement 'the genus must occur as an answer' is intended as genus absolutely, not a specific genus to which the demonstrative pronoun refers. (Taqrib p. 70) His statement: 'the species also occurs in the answer': it is not hidden that there is no indication therein that the complete definition does not occur as an answer to the question about matters of identical quiddity, so that it might be said that what was previously mentioned, that... in the definitions of the universals, is either due to assuming the word 'universal' in the discourse or due to restricting the predicate to individuals, otherwise the definition would not be comprehensive. This is not sound because the predicate does not cover the manyness of the definitions of the universals, as is evident from the commentator also. Rather, some investigators stated that the complete definition occurs as an answer to the question about matters of identical quiddity just as the species occurs. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (71) That is, matters gathered together differing in quiddity, whether the question is about them in terms of what they are or in terms of what they are not, because by 'matters' is meant what is more than one. If you say: the genus only occurs in answer to 'what is it?'. I say: what is intended is to specify that the genus is not said in answer to 'any thing'. (Abd al-Rahim) (72) It is not hidden that the universality of a predicate in answer to 'what is it?' in terms of sharing is not the same as its being a part of the quiddity, because it is only said in answer to 'what is it?' when asked about the quiddity and other things together, thus it indicates the completeness of its reality in terms of where the question falls on its entirety, and what is sought is the judgment of the reality that belongs to it by sharing. And this [universal] in this respect is not a particular but a genus. The particular is only said in terms of being composed of it and other things of the quiddity, and this consideration is not obtained from the first consideration. Thus the concept of a thing being a genus is different from the concept of it being a part, even if their substrates are one essence. So do not make a slip that the genus is part of what is a part, and the part is not predicated. So preserve that. (Abd al-Rahim) (73) His statement: 'if it is, i.e., the genus, while being an answer about the quiddity and about some realities different from it sharing with it in the genus, an answer about the quiddity and about each one of the different quiddities sharing with it in that genus, then the genus is proximate, like 'animal' when it occurs as an answer about man and about everything that shares with him in animal quiddity'. And if the genus does not occur as an answer about the quiddity (i.e., any quiddity assumed and mentioned in the question) and about everything that shares with it in the genus except through one intermediary or intermediaries, then it is remote, like 'body' when it is answered to the question 'what is man, horse, and donkey?', for it only occurs in the answer through the intermediary of 'growing body' and after that through the intermediary of 'animal'. And if the question contains quiddities differing in reality but sharing in a characteristic that is remote from some and proximate to others, then if this common characteristic occurs in the answer, it is remote relative to what is remote from it and proximate relative to what is proximate to it. For example, if one asks: 'what is man, tree, and stone?', then what must occur in the answer is what takes into account the whole situation, which is 'absolute body'. Thus absolute body is a proximate genus relative to stone, and remote from man and tree; from man through two intermediaries (growing body and animal), and from tree through one intermediary which is growing body. In the five universals, if what is more general than the common characteristic gestured to in the answer occurs, then it is remote from all, like the answer to the mentioned question of 'what is man, tree, and stone?' that they are 'substance'. Substance is a genus for all, but it is remote from stone by one intermediary (absolute body), from tree by two intermediaries (absolute body and growing body), and from man by three intermediaries (absolute body, growing body, and animal). (Taqrib p. 30) That. (74) His statement: 'Thus the proximate genus is like animal...' Know that because universal rules are not clear to the beginner speaker except through particular examples, and for that reason you see books of the arts filled with particular examples to facilitate the matter for beginners, the people cited in the investigations of this art particular examples and laid down in the arrangement of species and genera specific universals, namely man, animal, growing body, absolute body, and substance. Thus man is a species when it occurs as an answer to the question about matters identical in whatness, and each of the rest is a genus for man. As for animal, because it is the complete commonality between him and, e.g., sheep; as for growing body, because it is the complete commonality between him and plants; as for absolute body, because it is the complete commonality between him and, e.g., stone; as for substance, because it is the complete commonality between him and intellect. It is known from this that it is permissible for one quiddity to have multiple genera, some of which are genus for others. So when you know that, then know: the genus is either proximate or remote. Because, as was mentioned, it must occur as an answer about the quiddity and about some different realities sharing with it in that genus. If, besides that, it is an answer about it and about each one of the different quiddities sharing with it in that genus, then it is proximate, like animal, for just as it occurs as an answer to the question about man and horse, so too it occurs as an answer to the question about man and sheep, and about him and camel, and about him and mule, etc., among animal sharers. Otherwise it is remote, like growing body, when it occurs as an answer to the question about man and tree, but does not occur as an answer to the question about man and horse, even though it shares with man in that genus as well like tree; rather, 'animal' occurs in the answer. And like absolute body, when it occurs as an answer to the question about man and stone, but does not occur as an answer to the question about man and horse, nor about him and tree; rather, the first is answered by animal, and the second by growing body. And like substance, when it occurs as an answer to the question about man and intellect, but does not occur as an answer to the question about man and horse, nor about him and tree, nor about him and stone; rather, the first is answered by animal, the second by growing body, and the third by absolute body. And for the first, i.e., growing body, it is said 'remote by one degree', and for the second, i.e., absolute body, 'remote by two degrees', and for the third, i.e., substance, 'remote by three degrees'. And what regulates that: if there are two answers, then the genus is remote by one degree; if three answers, then by two degrees; if four answers, then by three degrees; and so on. Whenever the number of answers increases, the degrees of remoteness increase, and the number of degrees is one less than the number of answers. And what is clear is that the proximate genus is included in the count of answers and is not among the degrees of remoteness. And it has become clear from all this that one genus can be proximate and remote relative to two things, and likewise it can be proximate and remote by one degree, by two degrees, and by multiple degrees relative to multiple things, like substance, for it is a proximate genus for absolute body, a remote genus by one degree for growing body, by two degrees for animal, and by three degrees for man, as is clear to the contemplative. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a super-commentary (hashiya) on a previous commentary (likely Sharh al-Tahdhib), refining the explanation of proximate and remote genus. The author begins by critiquing the commentator's phrase 'sharing with it in that genus', arguing that no prior genus has been specified to refer to, so the demonstrative pronoun lacks an antecedent. He clarifies that the genus mentioned in the quote means genus as such, not a specific genus. Then he addresses an objection: if the genus only occurs in answer to 'what is it?', the intended meaning is to specify that genus is not said in answer to 'any thing' (which would be a different type of question). He then emphasizes a key distinction: the concept of a universal being predicated in answer to 'what is it?' is not the same as its being a part of the quiddity; the former indicates the complete reality shared, while the latter is a component. This prevents the confusion that the genus is a part and parts are not predicated. The core of the lesson is the definition and exemplification of proximate vs. remote genus. A proximate genus answers the question about the quiddity and about each different quiddity that shares in that genus without intermediaries. For example, 'animal' answers both 'what is man?' and 'what is horse?', and also 'what is man and sheep?' simultaneously — it is immediate. A remote genus requires one or more intermediaries. For instance, 'absolute body' answers 'what is man and stone?' but not 'what is man and horse?' (where animal is the immediate answer). The author gives a detailed hierarchical example using the standard five levels: man (species), animal (first genus), growing body (second), absolute body (third), substance (fourth). He shows how absolute body is proximate to stone but remote from man (via two intermediaries). He also explains that if a still higher genus like substance is used as the answer, it is remote from all, with varying degrees depending on how many sub-genera intervene. The rule is: the number of degrees of remoteness equals the number of distinct answers required minus one. The proximate genus counts as an answer but not as a degree of remoteness. Finally, he notes that a single genus can be both proximate and remote relative to different things; e.g., substance is proximate to absolute body but remote by one degree from growing body, by two from animal, by three from man. This lesson is crucial for understanding how genus functions in definitions and answers to 'what is it?' questions, especially when dealing with multiple quiddities.

Key Terms
جنس قريب
جنس بعيد
ماهية
ما هو
الجواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Based on the passage, if a genus (e.g., animal) directly answers the question 'what is man and horse?' but does not directly answer 'what is man and stone?', then for a question that includes man, horse, and stone, how should that genus (animal) be classified?
A) Proximate genus for all three
B) Remote genus for all three
C) Proximate for man and horse, remote for man and stone
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
A proximate genus answers each differing quiddity directly without intermediary; a remote genus requires one or more intermediaries, and the number of intermediaries determines the degree of remoteness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Given the question 'What is man, tree, and stone?', what genus should be the answer? Is it proximate or remote for each? Refer to the passage's example.
