Distinguishing Near from Distant Genus
Definition and examples of near vs distant genus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given genus is near or distant relative to a species by checking if it answers the 'what is it?' question for all species sharing it.

Core Idea
A genus is near if it directly answers for all species; otherwise it is distant.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies the distinction between near and distant genus using the standard examples from the logical tradition. These concepts are essential for understanding how definitions are constructed and how genera relate to species. We will see why animal is near for human while growing body is distant.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus and species pair
Look at the examples: animal is near for human, growing body is distant. Note which species it is checked against.
The passage uses human as the reference species.
2
Check if the genus answers for all participants
For near genus, it must answer the question for every species that shares that genus (e.g., animal answers for human, horse, sheep). For distant, it fails for some (e.g., growing body fails for horse).
The passage tests by comparing human to another species.
3
Observe the relativity of near and distant
A genus can be near for one species and distant for another. For example, substance is near for absolute body but distant for human.
This is shown later in the passage.
4
Apply to the hierarchy of five universals
Trace the chain: human → animal → growing body → absolute body → substance. Note which are near/distant at each level.
Practice with other species like horse or plant.

His saying: 'For the near genus is like animal...' Know that since universal rules are not clear to the beginner speaker except through particular examples, and you see that books of the arts are filled with particular examples to facilitate the matter for beginners, the people mentioned in the discussions of this art particular examples and arranged the species and genera as specific universals, namely human, animal, growing body, absolute body, and substance. So human is a species because it occurs as an answer to the question about things that agree in what it is, and each of the rest is a genus for human. As for animal, because it is the complete commonality between it and e.g. sheep. As for growing body, because it is the complete commonality between it and plants. As for absolute body, because it is the complete commonality between it and e.g. stone. As for substance, because it is the complete commonality between it and intellect. It has been known from this that it is permissible for one essence to have multiple genera, some of which are genera for others. So when you have known that, know that the genus is either near or distant, because as mentioned it must occur as an answer about the essence and about some of the different realities participating with it in that genus. If besides this it is an answer about it and about every one of the different essences participating with it in that genus, then it is near, like animal, for just as it occurs as an answer to the question about human and horse, so does it occur as an answer to the question about human and sheep, and about it and camel, and about it and mule, and so on from the animal participants. Otherwise it is distant, like growing body, where it occurs as an answer to the question about human and tree, but does not occur as an answer to the question about human and horse, despite being a participant with human in that genus as well, like tree; rather the answer is animal. And like absolute body, where it occurs as an answer to the question about human and stone, but does not occur as an answer to the question about human and horse, nor about human and tree; rather the first is answered with animal and the second with growing body. And like substance, where it occurs as an answer to the question about human and intellect, but does not occur as an answer to the question about human and horse, nor about human and tree, nor about human and stone; rather the first is answered with animal, the second with growing body, and the third with absolute body. And it is said for the first, i.e. growing body: 'distant by one degree', and for the second, i.e. absolute body: 'distant by two degrees', and for the third, i.e. substance: 'distant by three degrees'. And what governs that is: if there are two answers, then the genus is distant by one degree; if three answers, by two degrees; if four answers, by three degrees; and likewise whenever the number of answers increases, the degrees of distance increase, and the number of degrees is one less than the number of answers. And the excess in it is that the near genus is included in the count of answers and is not among the degrees of distance, as is apparent. And it has become clear from all this that one genus can be near and distant with respect to two things, and likewise it can be near and distant by one degree, by two degrees, and by degrees with respect to multiple things, like substance, for it is a near genus for absolute body, and a distant genus by one degree for growing body, by two degrees for animal, and by three degrees for human, as is not hidden from the reflective thinker. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the distinction between near and distant genus, a central topic in the theory of the five universals. It begins by justifying the use of particular examples for beginners, then lists the standard hierarchy used in logic books: human (species), animal, growing body, absolute body, and substance (genera in ascending order). The author notes that one essence can have multiple genera, arranged in a chain. The core definition: a genus is near if it answers the question 'what is it?' for all species that share it, as animal does for human and sheep or horse. If it fails for some (e.g., growing body answers for human and tree but not for human and horse), it is distant. The rule for determining the degree of distance is given: count the number of different answers needed to cover all species under consideration; the degrees of distance are one less than that number. The near genus is included in the count but not counted as a degree. Finally, the author shows that the same genus can be near relative to one species and distant by varying degrees relative to others, using substance as an example: near to absolute body, distant by one degree to growing body, by two to animal, by three to human. All examples are from the Aristotelian-cum-Islamic classification of beings.

Calculating Degrees of Distance for a Distant Genus
Rule for determining degrees of distance of a genus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compute the degree of distance of a genus from a species by counting the number of different answers required when comparing the species to others sharing the genus.

Core Idea
Degrees of distance equal the number of intermediate answers minus one.

After defining near and distant genus, the author provides a precise rule for measuring how distant a genus is. This quantification is important for understanding the hierarchy of genera and for constructing proper definitions. We will learn how to count degrees using the examples of substance relative to different species.

Guided Path
1
Recall the examples of distant genera for human
Growing body, absolute body, and substance are distant. Each fails to answer for certain pairs (e.g., growing body fails for human/horse).
The passage uses human as reference species.
2
Understand the rule: count the number of answers needed
For a given distant genus, list all the different genus-level answers that must be given when comparing the species to others under the same ultimate genus. For growing body vs human, you need growing body (for human/tree) and animal (for human/horse) — two answers → one degree of distance.
The near genus (animal) is counted as an answer but not as a degree.
3
Apply the rule systematically
For absolute body: answers needed are absolute body (human/stone), growing body (human/tree), animal (human/horse) — three answers → two degrees. For substance: answers are substance, absolute body, growing body, animal — four answers → three degrees.
Degrees = number of answers - 1.
4
Observe that one genus can have multiple degrees relative to different species
Substance is near for absolute body, distant by one degree for growing body, by two for animal, by three for human.
This shows the relativity of near/distant.

Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the distinction between near and distant genus, a central topic in the theory of the five universals. It begins by justifying the use of particular examples for beginners, then lists the standard hierarchy used in logic books: human (species), animal, growing body, absolute body, and substance (genera in ascending order). The author notes that one essence can have multiple genera, arranged in a chain. The core definition: a genus is near if it answers the question 'what is it?' for all species that share it, as animal does for human and sheep or horse. If it fails for some (e.g., growing body answers for human and tree but not for human and horse), it is distant. The rule for determining the degree of distance is given: count the number of different answers needed to cover all species under consideration; the degrees of distance are one less than that number. The near genus is included in the count but not counted as a degree. Finally, the author shows that the same genus can be near relative to one species and distant by varying degrees relative to others, using substance as an example: near to absolute body, distant by one degree to growing body, by two to animal, by three to human. All examples are from the Aristotelian-cum-Islamic classification of beings.

Key Terms
الجنس البعيد
المرتبة
الجوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 1
B) 2
C) 3
D) 4

Key Takeaway
The degree of distance of a genus equals the number of different answers required when comparing the species to all other species under the genus, minus one.

The Semantic Shift of 'Jins' from Lineage to Logic
Author explains the original Greek usage of 'jins' and its transfer to logical terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the semantic shift of 'jins' from its original Greek usage as a common ancestor to its logical meaning as a universal predicate.

Core Idea
The logical term 'jins' was borrowed from social lineage before becoming a universal category.

This rare marginal note offers a glimpse into how technical logical terms often originate from everyday language. Understanding the etymology of 'jins' helps you avoid confusing its ordinary meaning with its precise logical definition, and shows the dynamic relationship between language and technical disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original meaning of 'jins'
According to the passage, the first Greek usage of 'jins' referred to a common ancestor or lineage in which many individuals share, e.g., the line of Ali as a genus for the Alawids.
2
Identify the first extension
The Greeks then extended 'jins' to denote any person to whom many individuals are attributed, like a patron, because that person is the cause of the shared lineage.
The passage emphasizes that the cause is more deserving of the name than the effect.
3
Identify further extensions
The term was further extended to crafts and industries (as genera for those who share in them) and even to the shared activity itself.
4
Recognize the logical transfer
Since the logical concept of a genus (a universal common to many species) had no name originally, the Greeks transferred these similar social meanings to it, so the term 'jins' came to be used in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۷۵) قونه فبعيد كالجسم حيث يقع .... البعيد اما بمرتبة أو بمرتبتين ... واعلم ان لفظ الجنس فى لغة اليونانيين بحسب الوضع الاول كان موضوعاً للمعنى الذي يشترك فيه اشخاص كثيرون فيجعلون العلوية جنساً للعلويين و كانوا ايضاً يسمون الواحد المنسوب اليه اشخاص كثيرون جنساً لهم «على» فكان على عليه الصلوة والسلام مثلاً عندهم جنساً للعلويين و بين و كان هذا القسم عندهم اولى بالجنسية لان علياً عليه السلام سبب لكون العلوية جنساً للعلويين والسبب أولى من المسبب اذا وافقه في معناه او خالفه ، و كانوا ايضاً يسمون الحرف و الصناعات اجناساً للمشتركين فيها والشركة نفسها ايضاً جنساً و لما كان المعنى الذى يسمى عند المنطقيين الأن جنساً واحداً له نسبة الى اشياء كثيرة تشترك فيه ولم يكن له فى الوضع الأول اسم نقل هذه الامور المشابهة له اليه، فيسمى جنساً. (عبد الرحيم)
(75) His saying: 'For it is distant like body where it occurs...' The distant is either by one degree or two degrees... And know that the word 'jins' in the language of the Greeks according to the first usage was posited for the meaning in which many individuals share; so they would make the ancestral line a genus for the descendants. And they also called the one to whom many individuals are attributed a genus for them, like 'Ali; so 'Ali, peace be upon him, was for them a genus for the 'Alawids and the Hashemites. And this type was more deserving of genus-ness in their view because 'Ali was the cause of the ancestral line being a genus for the 'Alawids, and the cause is more deserving than the effect, whether it agrees with it in meaning or differs. And they also called the craft and industries genera for those who share in them, and they called the shared activity itself a genus. And since the meaning that is now called by the logicians 'jins' is one that has a relation to many things that share in it, and it did not have a name in the first usage, they transferred these things similar to it to it, so it is called 'jins'. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This marginal note provides an etymological background for the term 'jins' (genus). The author, Abd al-Rahim, explains that in Greek language, 'jins' originally meant a common ancestor or lineage shared by many individuals, e.g., the line of 'Ali as a genus for the 'Alawids. Then it was extended to any person to whom many are attributed (like a patron), and later to crafts and the shared activity itself. The logical term 'jins', denoting a universal predicate common to many species, is a later transfer of these earlier meanings. This note is useful for understanding why the term 'jins' came to be used in logic.

Correcting an Example of Distant Genus in the Five Universals
Author critiques a previous example of distant genus as erroneous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between near and distant genus in a composite question and identify errors in examples of genus predication.

Core Idea
A genus can be near to some species and distant to others—it is not absolute.

The five universals are essential for constructing definitions. A crucial skill is recognizing whether a genus is near or distant when applied to a list of species. In this passage, the author corrects a flawed example from a previous logician, showing that 'body' is near to stone but distant to human and horse. We will unpack this correction so you can avoid similar mistakes when analysing definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the composite question
The previous author posed two questions: one listing human, stone, horse; another listing human, tree, horse. Determine what 'parts' (ajza) are being asked about—the species listed.
2
Check the answer 'body' for each part individually
For each part, ask: is 'body' a valid answer to 'what is it?'? For stone, yes—stone is directly a body. For human, not directly—human is a body but requires 'rational animal' as an intermediate genus. So 'body' is a genus, but the distance varies.
Near genus: directly answers without intermediate. Distant genus: requires intermediate genera.
3
Classify distance for each part
Body is near to stone (stone is only body), distant to human (human is animal, which is body) and horse (horse is animal, which is body).
The distance is relative to each part, not absolute for the whole question.
4
Re-evaluate the original claim
The claim said body answers the first question but not the second. The correction shows body answers both, but with different distances. In the first question, body is not distant from all; in the second, body is distant from all but still a valid answer.
The original error was confusing 'not being an answer' with 'being a distant answer'.
5
Apply to a new example from Islamic law
Consider a composite question: 'What is wine, date wine, and grape wine?' (all intoxicants). Determine whether 'intoxicant' is a near or distant genus for each. For wine (khamr), it is a near genus because khamr is defined as an intoxicating drink. For date wine (nabidh), it is distant because date wine is not inherently intoxicating unless it has fermented. This mirrors the body example.
This skill is used in usul al-fiqh when defining terms and identifying 'illah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۷۶) وقوله : «كالجسم حيث يقع جواباً عن السؤال بالانسان والحجر والفرس ولا يقع جواباً عن السؤال بالانسان والشجر والفرس مثلا» اشتباه في اشتباه فان الجواب بالجسم عن السؤال الأول فليس بالجنس البعيد عن جميع اجزاء السؤال بل هو قريب الى بعض الى بعض بعيد عن بعض قريب الى الحجر بعيد الانسان والفرس والجواب بالجسم ايضاً عن السؤال الثاني صحيح بالضرورة و غاية ما فيه بعد الجنس عن جميع اجزاء السؤال الانسان والشجر والفرس (التقريب ص۳۱)
(76) And his saying: 'Like body where it occurs as an answer to the question about human, stone, and horse, but does not occur as an answer to the question about human, tree, and horse, for example' is error upon error. For the answer with body for the first question is not a distant genus from all parts of the question; rather it is near to some and distant from some: near to stone, distant from human and horse. And the answer with body for the second question is also correct by necessity, and the most that can be said is that the genus is distant from all parts of the question: human, tree, and horse. (al-Taqrib p. 31)
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This is a critical note pointing out a double error in a previous author's example concerning distant genus. The original claim was that 'body' (jism) is an answer for the question about human, stone, and horse, but not for human, tree, and horse. The commentator argues that this is mistaken twice. First, in the first set (human, stone, horse), 'body' is not distant from all three; it is near to stone (since stone is only body) but distant from human and horse (which require additional differentiae). Second, in the second set (human, tree, horse), 'body' is actually a valid answer, but it is a distant genus from all three because none of them are immediately body without further specification. Thus the example fails as a case where body is not an answer.

A) Near genus
B) Distant genus
C) Not a genus

Key Takeaway
A genus can be near to some species and distant to others within the same question; the answer 'body' is valid for both sets but with different degrees of distance.

Defending the Definition of Relative Species
Author rebuts an objection that the definition of relative species fails to exclude subspecies and real individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a logical objection to a definition and identify how the condition 'essential' excludes subspecies.

Core Idea
Extra definitional conditions are not redundant; they block unwelcome cases.

In logic, definitions must be carefully crafted to include all intended cases and exclude all unintended ones. This passage defends the definition of relative species against an objection that it would wrongly include subspecies and individuals. The rebuttal shows why the phrase 'in answer to what is it?' alone is insufficient, and the additional condition of being essential is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the problem with the definition of relative species: it would include subspecies (like Turkish human) and individuals, because the genus 'animal' answers 'what is it?' for them.
2
Understand the first part of the rebuttal
The author says 'essence' here means only a universal answering 'what is it?'. This excludes individuals, which are particulars.
Individuals are not universals, so they cannot answer 'what is it?' in the technical sense.
3
Understand the second part of the rebuttal
The author adds 'essential' to exclude subspecies. Subspecies are characterized by accidental restrictions, not essential predicates.
Subspecies like 'Turkish human' have the same essence as human, but the restriction is accidental.
4
Analyze the further objection about special property
The imaginary objector claims the phrase 'in answer to what is it?' already excludes subspecies because subspecies is a special property (khassa). The author responds that special properties are of two types: those that can be predicated with a genus (like subspecies) and those that cannot (like laughing). Subspecies belong to the first type, so the phrase alone does not exclude them.
5
Summarize the overall defense
The definition of relative species is defended by showing both conditions—universal and essential—are necessary to exclude respectively individuals and subspecies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۷۷) الغرض من هذا الكلام دفع ما يرد في هذا المقام وهو ان تعريف النوع الاضافى ليس بمطرد الصدقه على الصنف والجزء الحقيق فان الصنف وهو النوع المقيد بقيود كلية عرضية كالانسان التركي الهندى يقال عليه وعلى الفرس الحيوان الذى هو جنس في جواب ما هو وكذلك الجزئى الحقيقى. و وجه الدفع: ان المراد من الماهية هو المقول في جواب ماهو، فلا يكون الا كلياً فخرج الجزئى ولا يكون ايضاً الا ذاتياً فخرج الصنف. فان قلت الصنف لكونه خاصة يخرج بقوله: «فى جواب «ماهو فلا حاجة الى هذا التفسير لاخراجه. قلت الخاصة على قسمين قسم يقال عليه وعلى غيره الجنس في جواب ماهو و قسم ليس كذلك والصنف من القسم الأول فلا يخرج بقوله في جواب ماهو. (عبد الرحيم)
(77) The purpose of this statement is to repulse what is advanced in this place, which is that the definition of relative species does not apply uniformly to subspecies and the real individual (partial individual). For the subspecies, which is the species restricted by universal accidental restrictions, like Turkish or Indian human, it is said of it and of the horse that 'animal' (which is a genus) is the answer to 'what is it?'. Likewise for the real individual. And the repulsion: that by 'essence' is meant what is said in answer to 'what is it?' so it is nothing but a universal, thus the real individual is excluded; and it is also nothing but essential, thus the subspecies is excluded. If you say: the subspecies, because it is a special property, is excluded by his saying 'in answer to 'what is it?' so there is no need for this explanation to exclude it. I say: the special property is of two types: a type that is said of it and of other things as genus in answer to 'what is it?' and a type that is not. The subspecies is of the first type, so it is not excluded by his saying 'in answer to 'what is it?'. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (naqd wa jawāb) concerning the definition of relative species (al-nawʿ al-iḍāfī). The objection states that the definition would incorrectly include subspecies (e.g., Turkish human, Indian human) because the genus 'animal' can be predicated of them in answer to 'what is it?'. It would also include concrete individuals (juzʾ ḥaqīqī) for the same reason. The answer: the term 'essence' (māhiyya) in the definition is defined as what is said in answer to 'what is it?' which is always a universal, thus individuals are excluded. Additionally, it must be essential (dhātī), which excludes subspecies because subspecies have accidental restrictions. Then a further objection is raised: subspecies, being a special property (khāṣṣa), should already be excluded by the phrase 'in answer to 'what is it?' alone, because a special property is not a genus. The response explains that there are two types of special properties: those that can be predicated together with a genus in answer to 'what is it?' (like subspecies, since you can say 'human' which is a species, but subspecies like 'Turkish human' also respond with 'animal' as genus), and those that cannot (like 'laughing' for human). Subspecies belong to the first type, so the phrase alone does not exclude them; hence the need for the additional condition of being essential.

Key Terms
النوع الاضافي
الصنف
الخاصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) To exclude individuals
B) To exclude subspecies
C) To exclude genera
D) To exclude accidents

Key Takeaway
A definition's conditions must be layered to address each type of potential intrusion, as seen in the defense of relative species against subspecies and individuals.

The Debate on Real vs. Relative Species
Author presents the views of early and later scholars on the relationship between real and relative species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two positions on the relationship between real and relative species and reconstruct the argument of each side.

Core Idea
The debate over whether all real species are relative species hinges on the scope of the Aristotelian categories.

In this passage, the author introduces a disagreement among scholars: early ones held that every real species is also a relative species, while later scholars denied this. The disagreement turns on whether all real species fall under the ten categories. Understanding this debate helps you apply the five universals correctly to theological and philosophical classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Early scholars: absolute generality (every real species is a relative species). Later scholars: generality from one aspect (some are not).
2
Reconstruct the early scholars' argument
Real species fall under the ten categories; the categories are genera; anything under a genus is a relative species; therefore every real species is a relative species.
This is a deductive argument relying on the premise that real species are confined to the Aristotelian categories.
3
Reconstruct the later scholars' objection
They challenge the premise: not every real species is possible (e.g., necessary intellects), and even possible things are not confined to the ten categories—only physical genera are.
They also appeal to simple beings (basāʾiṭ) that are not composite and thus not under categories.
4
Evaluate the strength of each argument
Consider the implications: the early position relies on a comprehensive category system, the later position emphasizes exceptions like necessary beings, reflecting broader ontological debates in Islamic thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۷۹) اعلم انه قد اختلف فى ان النسبة بين النوع الحقيق والاضافي هل هي العموم مطلقا أو من وجه؟ فذهب المتقدمون الى الأول قالوا ان كل نوع حقيقى مندرج تحت مقولة من المقولات العشرة لانحصار الكليات فيها كما تحقق في موضعه و هى اجناس وكلما هو مندرج تحت جنس نوع اضافي، فكل نوع حقیقی نوع اضافی. والمتأخرون الى الثاني، قالوا: لاتسلم اندراج كل نوع حقيقى تحت مقولة من المقولات العشرة و انما يجب ذلك لو كان كل نوع حقيقى ممكناً وليس كذلك ولو سلم فلانسلم انحصار الممكنات في المقولات العشرة بل المنحصر اجناس ممكنات العالم على ما صرحوا به ، ثم استدلوا على مذهبهم بالبسايط قالوا: فانها ...
(79) Know that there is disagreement concerning the relationship between real species and relative species: is it absolute generality or from one aspect? The early scholars went to the first, saying that every real species is included under one of the ten categories because the universals [i.e., categories] are confined to them, as has been verified in its place, and they are genera, and everything included under a genus is a relative species, so every real species is a relative species. The later scholars went to the second, saying: we do not concede that every real species is included under one of the ten categories; rather that would be necessary only if every real species were possible, but it is not so. And even if conceded, we do not concede the confinement of possible things to the ten categories; rather the confined are the genera of the possible things of the world, as they have stated. Then they argued for their position by simple beings, saying: indeed they...
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage outlines a doctrinal disagreement (ikhtilāf) on the relationship between real species (al-nawʿ al-ḥaqīqī) and relative species (al-nawʿ al-iḍāfī). The early scholars (al-mutaqaddimūn) held that every real species is also a relative species. Their reasoning: all real species fall under one of the ten Aristotelian categories; the categories are genera; anything under a genus is a relative species (since it can be considered a species relative to a higher genus). Therefore every real species is a relative species. The later scholars (al-mutaʾakhkhirūn) disagreed. They challenged the premise that every real species falls under the ten categories. They argued that this would only be true if every real species were possible (mumkin), but some real species are necessary (e.g., intellects) and not in the categories. Even if that were accepted, they denied that all possible beings are confined to the ten categories; only the genera of the physical world are so confined. They then supported their view by appealing to simple beings (al-basāʾiṭ) that are not composable under categories. The text cuts off, but the position is clear. This disagreement shows the evolving critique of Aristotelian categories in Islamic thought.

Key Terms
النوع الحقيق
النوع الاضافي
المقولات العشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) All species are under the ten categories
B) All species are possible
C) Simple beings are categories
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The relationship between real and relative species is contested: early scholars affirm absolute inclusion under categories, later scholars deny it for necessary beings.
